Politics & Economy
Data Suggest U.S. Job Market Is Growing Slowly but Steadily
By Michael Schroeder
688 words
4 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Weak payroll growth and declining unemployment suggest that the U.S. job market is growing at a slow but steady pace.

Employers added a disappointing 110,000 jobs in March, the fewest since July and a big drop from 243,000 in February, the Labor Department reported. Meanwhile, the seasonally adjusted unemployment rate fell to 5.2% in March from 5.4% in February.

Economists believe that last month's lackluster performance in payroll growth is an indication of businesses turning more cautious in the face of profit pressures and another spike in energy prices.

Separately, the Commerce Department reported that construction spending increased 0.4% in February to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.05 trillion. In addition, the Institute for Supply Management, which polls purchasing agents at more than 400 industrial companies, said its index of manufacturing activity was relatively unchanged at 55.2 in March. The new-orders index ticked higher to 57.1 from 55.8, while the employment gauge slipped to 53.3 from 57.4.

Index readings above 50 indicate the sector is expanding. The March number represents the 22nd consecutive month of manufacturing growth.

The ISM also reported its nonmanufacturing index rose to 63.1 in March from 59.8 in the prior month. The employment gauge in this survey also slipped, to 57.1 from 59.6.

"The ISM survey provides a picture of the manufacturing sector that is growing at a moderately strong rate and yet struggling to overcome higher commodity prices," said Daniel Meckstroth, chief economist at the Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI, a policy research group in Arlington, Virginia.

But pressures on profits are holding down hiring, he added. Indeed, the Labor Department's report said that manufacturing employment fell 8,000 in March and the factory head count is down 20,000 since December 2004. Job losses at factories and in the retail sector tempered gains in professional and business services, construction, education and health services.

The low jobless rate may overstate the underlying strength in the labor market.

There were 7.7 million people unemployed in March, with an average duration of 19.5 weeks, the highest since November. The labor-force participation rate remained steady at only 65.8% for the month, the lowest level since 1988.

The participation rate, however, likely will improve if the economy continues to expand. "The slowdown in the labor force has been largely due to young people staying in school. This suggests labor force growth will reaccelerate in the upcoming years as young people go to work," according to a report by ISI Group in New York.

If there is a silver lining to the lukewarm jobs report, it is that it could benefit consumers by keeping a lid on mortgage rates. "The report provides further evidence that labor markets are improving only very gradually, allowing the [Federal Reserve] to maintain its measured pace of tightening" interest rates, said Peter Kretzmer, senior economist at Bank of America Corp.

The jobs report has eased, for now, worries that the Federal Reserve might boost short-term interest rates half a percentage point at its May 3 policy meeting. The fact that employment growth remains sluggish while economic growth is strong suggests that growth in output per worker -- productivity -- has picked up again after slowing late last year. Higher productivity helps firms keep a lid on costs and thus prices. And despite the fall in the unemployment rate, there is still no sign of accelerating wages. Even so, the Fed remains worried about inflation, and price reports will likely play a bigger role in its interest-rate decisions than job reports.

Separately, consumers weren't as upbeat in March, with sentiment toward both present and future economic conditions declining, according to another survey. The University of Michigan survey showed that inflation fears sent consumer confidence down to 92.6 in March from 94.1 a month earlier.

---

Economy: U.S. Job Growth Slows As Unemployment Falls --- Report Indicates Increase In Market Is Slow, Steady; Construction Outlays Rise
By Michael Schroeder
701 words
4 April 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

The Home Front:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
The Home Front: Sold in Translation --- U.S. Home-Sellers Look Abroad As Local Buyer Pools Shrink; Can You Throw in Caretaker?
By June Fletcher
1694 words
1 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WHEN GRAEME GLADE decided to sell his Hawaiian ocean-view home recently, he didn't list it locally, figuring few people would get to tiny Haleiwa on north Oahu to see his "For Sale" sign. He also passed over national advertisements, figuring there's a limited pool of U.S. buyers ready to pay his $3.75 million asking price. Instead, he put his home on a Web site that specializes in international listings, and so far he's fielded inquiries from Japan to the Netherlands.

"My home seems expensive to the locals," says Mr. Glade, a building subcontractor who did get a few nibbles from Americans, but is considering a bid from a British citizen. "But to foreigners, it's a bargain."

With an eye on the weak dollar, American homeowners are increasingly seeking out buyers who come not from around the block or even a nearby town -- but from other countries altogether. And foreigners, hoping for a deal, are more eager than ever to buy here. The upshot: The residential real-estate market is getting a small shot of globalization. Unique Homes, a U.S.-listing publication with distribution in 80 countries, has seen foreign Web hits more than double in the past year, while DuPont Registry of Homes in St. Petersburg, Fla., says demand by foreign distributors for its luxury magazine is up 12% from last year. Meanwhile, in part to pitch more U.S. homes to people in other countries, New Jersey-based Century 21 has expanded to 43 countries, up from 36 two years ago, and property broker Christie's Great Estates, in Santa Fe, N.M., is planning moves into South America and Asia.

Given the fact that buying homes -- and even quickly reselling them -- continues at a fervid pace, it would seem that U.S. homeowners wouldn't have to go abroad to find buyers. But in places like California's Orange County and New York, prices have climbed so high that the pool of potential purchasers has actually shrunk -- to folks who already own homes in the area and have built up equity, or who have quick access to a lot of cash. Expanding a sales pitch to foreigners, especially those whose native currency has risen against the dollar, can help pick up the slack. Another bonus: Since foreigners find it more difficult to get financing here, they tend to pay all cash.

Yet international buyers come with their own set of issues. In cases where a buyer does need a mortgage, U.S. and foreign banks tend to drag the process out as they check credit ratings and financial information abroad and at home. It's also standard protocol in some other countries to "lowball" a property, typically offering about half of what a home is listed for. That means that haggling can take on a larger role, with some foreign buyers bargaining up to the very end. "Don't be surprised if the buyer asks for the Ferrari at the closing table," says Olivia Hsu Decker, a broker in San Francisco.

While there can always be odd demands in real-estate transactions, they sometimes get exaggerated by cultural differences, as Michael and Karen Carroll discovered while fielding proposals for a five-bedroom oceanfront home in Corolla, N.C., (asking price: $7.4 million). One buyer from Australia proposed via e-mail that the Carrolls swap their home for his 1,000-acre ranch. "I can't go flying around the world trying to figure out what someone else's property is worth," says Ms. Carroll, who is still looking for a buyer after turning down offers from the U.S. and abroad.

Ken Knowles has gotten even stranger requests. Since putting his home in Auburn, Ky., on the international market for $1.2 million a month ago, the electronics-firm owner has received an offer from a shopper in Mexico who asked that a caretaker be included in the deal and an Australian who wanted Mr. Knowles's grand piano and backyard lawn tractor. A writer with a return address in Nigeria promised to buy the house -- but first, he asked Mr. Knowles to help him "transfer some funds" by supplying his bank account and personal-identification numbers. "I never realized I'd have to sift through so much mumbo-jumbo," says Mr. Knowles, who is still looking for a buyer.

While no one keeps records of how many foreign buyers are entering the U.S. market, brokers say foreigners prefer resorts or second-homes markets over most major cities. According to the National Association of Realtors, a record 2.82 million second homes were sold in 2004, up 16.3% from the year before. Those purchases were more affordable for foreigners whose buying power has increased: The Canadian dollar has risen 30% against the U.S. dollar over the past three years; the euro is up 50% over the same period, and the Australian dollar has jumped 39%.

Not all sellers are casting abroad for buyers, of course, but the concept is catching on -- and could grow if interest rates continue to rise, as they did last week. In that scenario, the dollar would likely strengthen, economists say, but more U.S. residents who couldn't afford an interest-rate increase would get squeezed out.

Already, many real-estate businesses are stepping up outreach efforts. Later this year, Century 21 plans to roll out an online service that will hook up a foreign buyer with a U.S.-based agent who speaks his language. (Once the buyer finds an American property on the company's site, he will be able to fill out an online form that will be forwarded via the site to a U.S.-based agent who speaks his language; the agent will follow up.) To encourage real-estate agents to cooperate more across borders, the National Association of Realtors launched a program in November that has begun registering agents world-wide, if they pass a new online exam in NAR's database. (The idea is that sellers will trust a foreign agent with the certification and be more likely to pursue a deal.) So far, 150 agents have registered.

Even builders are trying to cash in. Marshal Rothman, a builder in Mill Valley, Calif., just sold a six-bedroom home to a British couple for $4.5 million, after advertising on a free Internet site. In Cape Coral, Fla., Realmark Group has started targeting foreigners with ads in Lufthansa inflight magazines; it also posts signs and ads near the exit at the nearby Fort Myers International Airport. The company's managing broker is Scottish, and a senior sales executive is German; the architect is Cuban. Sales agents are required to speak German or French. "We don't want to miss anybody," says Will Stout, the company's American-born owner. The company says about 15% of its buyers are foreigners, up from 10% two years ago.

But reaching buyers who live abroad isn't always easy. Because most foreign countries don't have multiple-listing services, simply getting your house in front of the public can be challenging. What's more, some brokerage firms that work with foreigners have pretty high standards, dollarwise -- homes listed by Christie's average $5 million -- and tend to work in only a select number of states. For their part, foreign buyers complain that U.S. Web sites provide ample listings, but can skimp on practical information about financing.

And it can be just as hard to snag a foreign buyer willing to pay your price as it is to find an American one. In spite of a flurry of inquiries from Europeans at the end of last year, real-estate broker Mercedes Huff in Jackson Hole, Wyo., says no deals ever materialized. The pickings have been even slimmer in Los Angeles, according to broker Jeff Hyland. "We haven't seen Europeans take advantage of the dollar here. I'm still waiting for it."

---

Amir Efrati contributed to this story

---

Foreign Affairs

Think your home might appeal to a buyer abroad? Here are some tips to help you reach foreign clients and make negotiations smoother.

CONSIDER ADVERTISING in American publications that foreigners may read, including professional or alumni magazines, trade or hobby newsletters or Chamber of Commerce magazines distributed abroad, suggests Beverly Hills, Calif., broker Carmela Ma. Rates in these publications can be low, and because they don't carry many real-estate ads, your house will stand out. Or try local ethnic publications that might be shared with relatives back home, like a Hispanic newspaper.

SINCE FOREIGN BUYERS often have a hard time establishing credit with banks in the U.S., consider advertising your home with seller financing. Offer a rate just below that what local lenders are offering, perhaps adjustable to a lower rate in case the dollar rises against the buyer's currency. (You will manage the loan and be paid periodically by the buyer.)

LOOK FOR AMERICAN real-estate agents who have lived overseas, have dual citizenships or travel often in other countries. "You need someone who speaks the lingo," says Daniel Webster Johnson, a broker in Breckenridge, Colo. The Re/Max site, www.remax.com[http://www.remax.com] , has a "Find an Agent" icon that lets you search for an agent by language skills and location. The National Association of Realtors' site, www.realtor.org[http://www.realtor.org] , lets you search for a "Certified International Property Specialist."

MANY CULTURES are more formal than ours, so when buyers visit, take your cues from them, says Sarasota, Fla., broker Wendy Ross. For instance, some cultures turn to relatives for financing, so don't be surprised if a large extended family flies in to tour your home. Give particular respect to elders in the family, since often they're the ones who will green-light the sale, even if they aren't going to live in the house.

-- June Fletcher

The Economy
Durable Goods, Home Sales Signal Growth Is Continuing
By Timothy Aeppel
441 words
25 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Corrections &Amplifications

JOEL RASSMAN IS chief financial officer of Toll Brothers Inc. An Economy page article in some editions Friday that included the monthly new-home sales report incorrectly said he was chief executive.

(WSJ Mar. 28, 2005)

(END)

Orders for long-lasting factory goods grew modestly and sales of new homes surged last month, indicating that the economy's steady growth continued.

The Commerce Department's durable-goods report showed orders for items intended to last three years or more grew 0.3% in February, boosted by gains in civilian and military aircraft that largely offset weakness in motor vehicles and other transport equipment. The department said orders fell a revised 1.1% in January, somewhat better than the previous estimate of a 1.3% drop.

The durable-goods report showed that orders for transportation equipment had the largest increase -- $900 million, or 1.6%, to $53.6 billion. Excluding transportation goods, orders decreased 0.2%.

Durable-goods orders are highly volatile and most economists emphasize the need to look at the trend over several months.

"If you take the last three months compared to the same period a year ago, new orders are up 10% for durable goods, so we're still seeing relatively strong activity," said Daniel Meckstroth, chief economist of the Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI, a policy-research group in Arlington, Va. This included price increases that somewhat distorted the comparison, he added, but prices of machinery were up only about 2% from a year earlier.

David Greenlaw, an economist for Morgan Stanley, said the main disappointment in the numbers was the pullback in shipments. Shipments of manufactured durable goods dropped $3.4 billion, or 1.6%, to $53.6 billion in February, following four consecutive monthly increases. "In this environment of stronger orders," he said, "you wouldn't expect so much of a pullback in shipments."

Separately, new-home sales jumped 9.4% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1,226,000, the Commerce Department said. "There's tremendous desire on the part of the American consumer to own a home, and there's a very constricted supply," said Joel Rassman, chief executive of Toll Brothers Inc., one of the nation's largest builders of luxury homes.

Separately, initial claims for unemployment benefits rose 3,000 to 324,000 during the week ended March 19, the Labor Department said. The four-week average rose 4,000 to 322,000, its third consecutive increase after hitting its lowest level since October 2000 late last month.

Politics & Economy
Consumer Prices In U.S. Rise 0.4%, Boosted by Energy
By Timothy Aeppel
624 words
24 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Higher energy costs pushed up U.S. consumer prices more than expected last month, fueling inflation worries and heightening concerns that the Federal Reserve could accelerate the pace of interest-rate increases.

The Labor Department reported Wednesday that its consumer-price index rose 0.4% in February on a seasonally adjusted basis from the month before, the largest increase in four months. Energy accounted for "virtually all of the acceleration" in the index, the agency said, but other goods and services also played a role. Medical-care costs rose 0.6% and are now up 4.3% from a year ago. Housing and education also rose, more than offsetting a decline in computers.

Excluding food and energy, items prone to volatile price swings, consumer prices were up 0.3% from the month before, the biggest increase since a matching rise last September and breaking a string of four monthly gains of 0.2%.

A separate report from the National Association of Realtors held further evidence of inflationary pressure in the U.S.: The median price for existing housing hit $191,000 in February, up 11% from a year ago. The report said sales of existing homes fell 0.4% in February, compared with the month before, to a seasonally adjusted rate of 6.79 million. Higher interest rates are expected curb both new-home and existing-home sales, but the February figure was still considered healthy.

Thomas Kunz, president and chief executive of Century 21 Real Estate Corp., a unit of New York-based Cendant Corp., said he is "optimistically cautious" about the outlook for his sector. His company was predicting a decline in sales this year, he said. "But year-over-year, we're still up 6.1%, and last year was a record year."

Wednesday's reports, taken together with increases in producer prices reported earlier this week, suggested that inflation -- while still modest -- is continuing to accelerate. The Fed on Tuesday signaled for the first time in four years that it has inflation concerns, saying that while "longer-term inflation expectations remain well contained, pressures on inflation have picked up in recent months and pricing power is more evident." The Fed raised its target for the federal-funds rate, charged on overnight loans between banks, to 2.75% from 2.5%, its seventh quarter-point increase since last May.

Economists say a big check to overall inflation is American consumers' stinginess. They are quick to switch to cheaper products and have more tools at their fingertips, such as the Internet, to hunt for bargains. Stephen Gallagher, U.S. chief economist at SG CIB, the corporate- and investment-banking arm of France's Societe Generale Group, says he expects consumer resistance and competitive pressures to stabilize the pace of inflation, making more-aggressive Fed action unnecessary.

Still, he and other economists say chances are growing that the Fed may accelerate rate increases, particularly if inflation continues to rise more rapidly than expected. "The Fed's moving in that direction," Mr. Gallagher says, adding that "it would be a classic thing for the bank to do: They typically speed up rate hikes just at the time they should be slowing down."

John Ryding, chief U.S. economist at Bear Stearns, says the Fed's latest statement opens the door to a possible 0.5-percentage-point rate increase at the Fed's next interest-rate policy meeting, May 3. But Mr. Ryding says he still expects the Fed to stick to a quarter-point move instead.

---

The Economy
Consumer Prices Rise 0.4%, Feed Interest-Rate Fears
By Timothy Aeppel
828 words
24 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Higher energy costs pushed up U.S. consumer prices more than expected last month, fueling inflation worries and heightening concerns that the Federal Reserve could accelerate the pace of interest-rate increases.

The Labor Department reported that its consumer-price index rose 0.4% in February on a seasonally adjusted basis from the month before, the largest increase in four months. Energy accounted for "virtually all of the acceleration" in the index, the agency said, but other goods and services also played a role. Medical-care costs rose 0.6%, putting them up 4.3% from a year earlier. Housing and education also rose, more than offsetting a decline in computer costs.

Excluding food and energy, items prone to volatile price swings, consumer prices were up 0.3% from the month before, the biggest increase since a matching rise last September and breaking a string of four monthly gains of 0.2%.

A separate report from the National Association of Realtors contained further evidence of inflationary pressure in the U.S.: The median price for existing housing hit $191,000 in February, up 11% from a year ago. The report said sales of existing homes fell 0.4% in February, compared with the month before, to a seasonally adjusted rate of 6.79 million homes. Higher interest rates are expected curb both new-home and existing-home sales, but the February figure was still considered healthy.

Thomas Kunz, president and chief executive of Century 21 Real Estate Corp., a unit of New York-based Cendant Corp., says he is "optimistically cautious" about the outlook for his sector. His company was predicting a decline in sales this year, he said. "But year-over-year, we're still up 6.1%, and last year was a record year."

Wednesday's reports, taken together with increases in producer prices reported earlier this week, suggested that inflation -- while still modest -- is continuing to accelerate. The Fed on Tuesday signaled for the first time in four years that it has inflation concerns, saying that while "longer-term inflation expectations remain well contained, pressures on inflation have picked up in recent months and pricing power is more evident." The Fed raised its target for the federal-funds rate, charged on overnight loans between banks, to 2.75% from 2.5%, its seventh quarter-point increase since last May.

At 49 Tire in Richland, Miss., an independent tire dealership, inflation is starting to pinch -- but not in the way consumers might expect. Owner Chris Tolleson says tire makers, citing higher oil and other raw-material costs, have pushed up what he pays for tires 3% to 10% during the past year. But ferocious competition -- from both other independent dealers and big-box retailers -- still makes it tough to raise his retail prices.

"We've made up for some of that with labor charges," says Mr. Tolleson, who has increased his service fees about 7% during the past year and started charging for "road hazard plans" that he used to offer free with new tires. Others in the region are doing the same, so his labor rates are still competitive, he adds.

Economists say a big check to overall inflation is American consumers' stinginess. They are quick to switch to cheaper products and have more tools at their fingertips, such as the Internet, to hunt for bargains. Stephen Gallagher, U.S. chief economist at SG CIB, the corporate- and investment-banking arm of France's Societe Generale Group, says he expects consumer resistance and competitive pressures to stabilize the pace of inflation, making more-aggressive Fed action unnecessary.

Still, he and other economists say chances are growing that the Fed may accelerate rate increases, particularly if inflation continues to rise more rapidly than expected. "The Fed's moving in that direction," Mr. Gallagher says, adding that "it would be a classic thing for the bank to do: They typically speed up rate hikes just at the time they should be slowing down."

John Ryding, chief U.S. economist at Bear Stearns, says the Fed's latest statement opens the door to a possible half-point rate increase at the Fed's next interest-rate policy meeting, May 3. But Mr. Ryding says he still expects the Fed to stick to a quarter-point move.

In its report, the Labor Department said overall consumer prices were up 3% in the 12 months through February. Excluding food and energy, prices were up 2.4% in that period, the fastest rate since August 2002. In February, energy prices rose 2%, rebounding from a 1.1% decline in January, but well below the 4% spike seen in October.

---

THE OUTLOOK:
International News
THE OUTLOOK: Demand for Housing In U.S. Speeds Pace Of New Construction
By Constance Mitchell Ford
769 words
22 March 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

New York -- IT IS NO SECRET that U.S. home builders have been putting up houses at a furious pace. But even housing-industry executives -- typically an optimistic lot -- were taken aback when the government reported last week that home-building activity has accelerated.

The U.S. Commerce Department said that construction was started on new homes in February at an annualized rate of 2.2 million units. That was 15.8% higher than a year earlier and the fastest pace of residential construction starts in 21 years. Starts for single-family homes, the largest component of residential housing, reached an annual rate of 1.78 million units, a record. Even though many economists believe these numbers will ease in the months ahead as inventories of unsold homes start to pick up, it still is likely that 2005 could be the biggest year for home construction in decades.

Activity isn't isolated to the affluent suburbs or the trendy sections of cities. Even the Bronx, the poorest of New York City's five boroughs, is seeing record levels of housing construction. All this building raises a natural question: Why does the U.S. need so much new housing?

The simple answer, of course, is rising demand. But what is behind the demand is worth considering. First and foremost, the demand for housing reflects two key demographic trends -- new household formations and immigration. Economists at Harvard University's Joint Center for Housing Studies believe that household formations in the current decade will outpace formations during the 1990s, when they estimate that 12.5 million households were formed. Growth has been due in part to many so-called Generation Xers -- or people born from 1965 to 1980 -- establishing households at younger ages than even their parents did. Over the next 10 years, the Joint Center estimates that between 13.4 million and 14.5 million new households will be formed, depending on the level of immigration.

THE U.S. CENSUS Bureau estimates that immigration is running at about 850,000 a year, but the Joint Center says the actual number is between 1.2 million and 1.3 million. If immigration comes in on the high side, household formations will begin to approach levels last seen "when the baby boomers stormed into adulthood in the 1970s," says Eric Belsky, the center's executive director, referring to the baby-boom generation born from 1946 through 1964.

Rising wealth is a second factor behind strong housing demand. According to a recent report by the National Association of Realtors, 2.82 million second homes were sold in 2004, up sharply from the previous year. Exactly how many of those homes were newly constructed is hard to know, although estimates range from 5% to 15%.

There's another, less-discussed factor fueling demand for new construction: The rapid replacement of unusable and undesirable homes. According to housing-industry economists, the U.S. housing stock is in the process of being rebuilt, as aging stock is torn down to make room for newer stock. The average age of an American home is about 33 years -- older than any previous time in U.S. history. As the housing stock ages, new construction starts "will be permanently elevated because we need to replace housing stock which is wearing out," says Fannie Mae economist David Berson.

NOT THAT THE oldest homes in the U.S. are disappearing the fastest. In some parts of the country, 100-year-old houses remain highly desirable owing to their size, architecture and solid construction. Older homes of shoddy or inferior construction are the prime targets of demolition. Homes also are removed or lost because of disasters such as hurricanes or fires, the conversion of structures between residential and nonresidential use and because of "tear downs," where home buyers tear down a perfectly fine, but smaller, home in an older neighborhood and build a larger house on the same lot. "The key is location," says National Association of Home Builders economist David Seiders. "Older neighborhoods are being systematically upgraded."

Getting a fix on how many homes are being removed from the housing stock is tricky. One study cited in a report by the NAHB found that net removals of housing between 1985 and 1995 totaled 7.4 million, or about 740,000 a year. The Census Bureau estimates are substantially smaller and they are forecasting net removals will total about 450,000 annually between now and 2013.

THE OUTLOOK:
Politics & Economy
THE OUTLOOK: U.S. Housing Starts Have Solid Foundation
By Constance Mitchell Ford
1058 words
21 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New York -- IT'S NO SECRET that U.S. home builders have been putting up new houses at a furious pace. But even housing-industry executives -- typically an optimistic lot -- were taken aback when the U.S. government reported last week that home-building activity has accelerated.

The Commerce Department said that construction was started on new homes in February at an annualized rate of 2.2 million units. That was 15.8% higher than a year earlier and the fastest pace of residential construction starts in 21 years. Starts for single-family homes, the largest component of residential housing, reached an annual rate of 1.78 million units, a record. Even though many economists believe these numbers will ease in the months ahead as inventories of unsold homes start to pick up, it's still likely that 2005 could be the biggest year for home construction in decades.

And the activity isn't isolated to the affluent suburbs or the trendy sections of cities. Even the Bronx, the poorest of New York City's five boroughs, is seeing record levels of housing construction. According to Wilhelm Ronda, director of planning and development in the Bronx Borough President's Office, the value of residential developments under construction rose to $575 million (432 million euros) last year from $174 million in 2001.

All this building raises a natural question: Why does the U.S. need so much new housing?

The simple answer, of course, is rising demand. But what's behind the demand is worth considering. First and foremost, the demand for housing reflects two key demographic trends -- new household formations and immigration. Economists at Harvard University's Joint Center for Housing Studies believe that household formations in the current decade will outpace formations during the 1990s, when they estimate that 12.5 million households were formed. Over the next 10 years, the Joint Center estimates that between 13.4 million and 14.5 million new households will be formed, depending on the level of immigration.

THE U.S. CENSUS Bureau estimates that immigration is running at about 850,000 a year, but the Joint Center says the actual number is between 1.2 million and 1.3 million. If immigration comes in on the high side, household formations will begin to approach levels last seen "when the baby boomers stormed into adulthood in the 1970s," says Eric Belsky, the center's executive director.

Rising wealth is a second factor behind strong housing demand. As baby boomers come into their peak wealth years, a good percentage are buying second homes. According to a recent report by the National Association of Realtors, 2.82 million second homes were sold in 2004, up sharply from the previous year. Exactly how many of those homes were newly constructed is hard to know, although estimates range from 5% to 15%.

There's another, less-discussed factor fueling demand for new construction: The rapid replacement of unusable and undesirable homes. According to housing-industry economists, the U.S. housing stock is in the process of being rebuilt, as aging stock is torn down to make room for newer stock.

The average age of an American home is about 33 years -- older than any previous time in U.S. history. The number of homes older than 50 years rose to 25.8 million in 2000 from about 18.8 million in 1990. The average age of the U.S. housing stock swayed between 23 and 27 years from 1940 to 1990, then started a persistent rise. As the housing stock ages, new construction starts "will be permanently elevated because we need to replace housing stock which is wearing out," says Fannie Mae economist David Berson.

NOT THAT THE oldest homes in the U.S. are disappearing the fastest. In some parts of the country, 100-year-old houses remain highly desirable due to their size, architecture and solid construction. Older homes of shoddy or inferior construction are the prime targets of demolition. Homes also are removed or lost due to disasters such as hurricanes or fires, the conversion of structures between residential and nonresidential use and because of "tear downs," where home buyers tear down a perfectly fine, but smaller, home in an older neighborhood and build a larger house on the same lot. This is occurring as more people decide they want the convenience of living close to the city, which often means living in an older neighborhood, yet they also want the amenities and space of a new house. "The key is location," says National Association of Home Builders economist David Seiders. "Older neighborhoods are being systematically upgraded."

Getting a fix on how many homes are being removed from the housing stock is tricky. One study cited in a report by the NAHB found that net removals of housing between 1985 and 1995 totaled 7.4 million, or about 740,000 a year. The Census Bureau estimates are substantially smaller and they're forecasting net removals will total about 450,000 annually between now and 2013. Most economists believe Census estimates are far too conservative. In any case, all economists agree that the replacement of housing stock has been strong and will likely rise in the years ahead. And that, says Mr. Berson, means "new starts will be permanently elevated because we need to replace housing stock which is wearing out."

That doesn't necessarily mean that home prices will continue to shoot up at double-digit rates or that housing speculators will continue to cash in. But it does suggest there are some strong fundamentals behind the building boom.

Replacing old stock with new housing partly explains the high level of residential construction in older neighborhoods such as the Bronx, where, according to Mr. Ronda, demand for newly constructed homes is so strong that one waterfront development was sold out even though some homes won't be completed for two years. Not surprisingly, it's a trend that Bronx officials applaud because of the broader economic implications. "When you rebuild neighborhoods that were devastated in the '60s and '70s, it all has a domino effect," says Mr. Ronda. "We're now seeing complete shopping areas come back to life because neighborhoods are coming back."

Dow Jones & Company Inc.

The Economy
U.S. Trade Deficit Hits a Record $665.9 Billion
By Michael M. Phillips
1093 words
17 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Corrections &Amplifications

THE NUMBER OF large multifamily houses started in February rose 1.7% from the previous month to 420,000 units. An article on the U.S. trade deficit yesterday incorrectly stated that the number of starts in that category was unchanged at 365,000.

(WSJ March 8, 2005)

(END)

WASHINGTON -- The U.S. current-account deficit ballooned to a record $665.9 billion last year, indicating that the weaker dollar is having little effect so far on the enormous imbalance between what Americans buy from overseas and what they sell abroad.

The current-account deficit -- the most-inclusive measure of the trade gap -- was 25% wider in 2004 than it was in 2003, equaling 6.3% of gross domestic product, the Commerce Department reported yesterday. In 2003, the deficit measured 4.8% of GDP.

The data suggest that not only has the trade imbalance resisted the effects of a weaker dollar but also that the dollar's decline may not be over.

"Over a long period of time the trade balance being so deeply in the red is a cause for concern about the direction of the dollar's equilibrium value," said Jason Bonanca, currency strategist for Credit Suisse First Boston.

The euro has gained 47% against the dollar since May 2002, trading at about $1.34 yesterday afternoon. The dollar, which bought 104.24 yen yesterday afternoon, has lost 22% of its value against the Japanese currency since April 2002.

In theory the interplay between the current-account deficit and the dollar should work like this: The expanding trade gap makes foreigners less inclined to invest money in the U.S., eventually pushing down the value of the dollar. A weaker dollar makes American goods and services more attractive abroad and imported products pricier in the U.S., gradually narrowing the deficit in an automatic correction.

In the worst-case scenario, foreigners get so skittish about dollar assets -- U.S. stocks, bonds, factories and the like -- that they pull their money out, the dollar plunges, U.S. interest rates shoot up and the American economy slows.

In fact, the current-account deficit keeps expanding, driven by fast growth in the U.S. that generates demand for imports, relatively slower growth in Europe and Japan that stifles demand for American exports, rising oil prices and a continued foreign appetite for dollar financial assets. In addition, Americans -- including the U.S. government -- are spending rather than saving at a time when foreigners are saving money and looking for a place to invest. While the dollar has fallen, its weakness has so far apparently been overwhelmed by the forces that are contributing to the widening of the trade deficit.

"The weaker dollar is probably helping a bit, but basically U.S. demand is running very strong," said Robert Mellman, vice president and senior economist at J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. "Consumer spending is strong and especially consumer spending on goods is strong. Capital spending is growing in double digits."

The current-account deficit has been expanding since the mid-1990s, and for years economists and currency traders have been warning that it cannot continue to do so indefinitely. "So far we're living relatively well with a problem that we were all taught would be a difficult problem with which to live," said Maury Harris, chief economist at UBS.

There are even suggestions that the current-account deficit could continue to grow. Foreigners seem willing to continue to put their money in the U.S. and finance American imports, despite some recent hints that Asian central banks are eager to diversify their holdings away from dollars. The net inflow of foreign capital into U.S. stocks rose $170.2 billion in the fourth quarter of 2004, compared to a $93 billion increase in the third quarter. It was the largest net inflow since 2000.

Yesterday's report also showed a narrowing U.S. surplus in the income category, the difference between what Americans receive in interest and dividends on foreign investments and what foreigners make on their investments in the U.S. Generally, the U.S. is the net beneficiary of those payments. The Commerce Department report showed that the U.S. surplus narrowed to $24 billion in 2004 from $33 billion in 2003, making the current-account deficit wider.

That trend could exert some further downward pressure on the dollar.

On the other hand, Harvard economist Jeffrey A. Frankel, an adviser in the Clinton White House, said that the weakened dollar may yet help narrow the current-account deficit. After the 1985 Plaza Accord, an agreement among the U.S. and other major economic powers, the dollar fell precipitously. But it took until 1987 before exports rose and imports fell, narrowing the trade gap. "In retrospect the main explanation is just that there are lags and to get the really big effects took more than a year," Mr. Frankel said.

In addition, while the dollar has fallen against the euro and yen, it hasn't done so against other Asian currencies, especially the Chinese yuan, which is kept artificially in a narrow range by Beijing.

In a separate report, the Federal Reserve said U.S. factories, mines and utilities picked up the pace of production in February in line with market expectations, while January activity was revised up slightly.

Industrial production rose 0.3% last month as factory production climbed steadily and utilities output dropped for the second straight month. The Fed revised industrial production in January to show a 0.1% growth rate compared with its initial estimate of unchanged output in that month.

February industrial capacity use was reported at 79.4%, its highest level since December 2000, and January capacity use was revised up to 79.2% from the previously reported 79%.

In February, manufacturing production rose 0.5%, matching the 0.5% growth rates in January and December, which were revised up in the latest monthly report from previous estimates of 0.4% growth. February manufacturing capacity use came in at 78.5%, up from January's 78.2% and the highest level since November 2000.

Meanwhile, housing starts increased by 0.5% to an annual rate of 2.195 million in February from an upwardly revised 2.183 million in January. Single-family homes increased by 0.3% to 1.775 million, while large multifamily starts were unchanged at 365,000.

International News
U.S. Trade Gap Hit Record in 2004 --- Appetite for Foreign Goods, High Oil Prices Contribute To $665.9 Billion Deficit
By Jeff Bater and Campion Walsh Dow Jones Newswires
748 words
17 March 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A6
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

Corrections & Amplifications

THE NUMBER OF large multifamily houses started in February rose 1.7% from the previous month to 420,000 units. An article on the U.S. trade deficit Thursday incorrectly stated that the number of starts in that category was unchanged at 365,000.

(AWSJ March 21, 2005)

(END)

Modest gains in U.S. industrial production and housing starts were overshadowed by reports of a record widening of the current-account deficit, the broadest gauge of the nation's global trade.

The trade gap last year soared to a high of $665.9 billion in 2004, the Commerce Department said yesterday, up 25.5% from the previous record of $530.7 billion, set the year before.

Pressured by rising oil prices, the shortfall widened even more than expected at the end of 2004 despite the dollar's fall against major currencies. The fourth-quarter deficit hit a record $187.9 billion, expanding 13.3% from an upwardly revised $165.9 billion in the third quarter, the Commerce Department said .

The current-account balance combines trade of goods and services, transfer payments, and investment income. But about 90% of the deficit is accounted for by the balance in goods and services. Increasing oil prices late last year and Americans' continuing appetite for foreign goods caused a sharp deterioration of the deficit.

The current account gap has widened to about 5.5% of gross domestic product, up from 1.5% in 1996. Last week, Federal Reserve Gov. Ben Bernanke said a significant surge in the global supply of saving during the past decade helps explain the deterioration in the current account deficit.

Imports of goods climbed to $392.1 billion in the fourth quarter from $372 billion. "Much of the increase was in petroleum and petroleum products and in consumer goods," the Commerce report said.

Exports of goods advanced to $208.6 billion from $205 billion. Much of the increase was in industrial supplies and materials and in consumer goods.

The report also shows data on foreign-owned assets in the U.S. Foreigners purchased $124.7 billion of U.S. corporate and agency bonds in the fourth quarter, up from the $88.2 billion of purchases in the third quarter. Foreigners bought $12.1 billion of U.S. Treasury securities during the quarter, up from $900 million purchased in the prior three months, and a net $45.5 billion of U.S. stocks, compared with net purchases of $4.8 billion in the third quarter.

In a separate report, the Federal Reserve said U.S. factories, mines and utilities picked up the pace of production in February in line with market expectations, while January activity was revised up slightly.

Industrial production rose 0.3% last month as factory production climbed steadily and utilities output dropped for the second straight month. The Fed revised industrial production in January to show a 0.1% growth rate compared with its initial estimate of unchanged output in that month.

February industrial capacity utilization was reported at 79.4%, its highest level since December 2000, and January capacity utilization was revised up to 79.2% from the previously reported 79%.

In February, manufacturing production rose 0.5%, matching the 0.5% growth rates in January and December, which were revised up in the latest monthly report from previous estimates of 0.4% growth. February manufacturing capacity use came in at 78.5%, up from January's 78.2% and the highest level since November 2000.

U.S. manufacturers have been projecting their growth rate will exceed the overall economy in 2005 and 2006 in a continuing recovery from their 2001-02 recession.

Meanwhile, housing starts increased by 0.5% to an annual rate of 2.195 million in February from an upwardly revised 2.183 million in January. Single-family homes increased by 0.3% to 1.775 million, while large multifamily starts were unchanged at 365,000.

Permits fell by 2.7% to an annual rate of 2.071 million. On balance, housing is continuing to hold up well in the face of firming interest rates.

The Property Report
Real Estate's Foreign Affair --- Declining Dollar Enables Europeans to Buy U.S. Homes, Pushing Up Prices for Locals
By Ray A. Smith and Ryan Chittum
1251 words
9 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

PAM GILHOOLY and her sister Valerie King, both of Edinburgh, Scotland, spent many holidays in Florida and often dreamed of buying a place there. Their wish came true last year when the weakening dollar enabled them to pool their savings to buy a four-bedroom, 3 1/2-bath home in Orlando for $214,000.

"We thought it was a fantastic time to buy," Ms. Gilhooly, a lab technician, says. A similar property back home would have cost about $950,000, she says. "The currency definitely helped."

The weakness of the dollar has suddenly made the cachet of owning a second home in the U.S. possible for many Europeans -- and they are snapping up houses and condos across Florida, as well as New York and other locations like Chicago and Colorado's ski resorts.

The euro has risen about 10% against the dollar in the last year and more than 50% in the last three years. Meanwhile, the British pound is up about 35% from three years ago and about 5% in the last year. Though housing prices in the U.S. have hit record highs -- the median price of a single-family home in West Palm Beach-Boca Raton, Fla., rose 36% last year to $361,800, for example -- foreign buyers are undeterred because of the strength of their own currencies and housing prices that are often even higher in their own countries.

No one keeps statistics on foreign buying of U.S. residential real estate, but brokers and housing experts say foreigners have stepped up their purchases. Liz Agnello, director of broker services at the Ginn Co., of Orlando, says a broker in England recently referred 14 people to her, all of whom were interested in the company's new condos at the Reunion Resort &Club of Orlando, which range from $500,000 to $800,000. "You don't normally get 14 foreigners at one time," she says. "It's not an uptick. It's a surge."

Foreigners have always bought U.S. real estate, particularly when the dollar is weak. But the current buying spree has more steam, thanks to the impact of a single European currency and cheaper airfares, experts say.

The creation of the euro in 1999 is driving European bargain hunters to the U.S. because they can no longer take advantage of weak currencies of countries within the continent to get deals on homes, says Jeremy Siegel, professor of finance at the Wharton School at the University of Pennsylvania.

Another key difference is cheaper airfares. Deregulation of the airline industry fueled competition and led to cheaper flights, making the U.S. more accessible for overseas travelers, says Stuart Gabriel, a professor of finance and economics at the University of Southern California's Marshall School of Business.

Despite the bargain prices, some foreign buyers may not spend much time at these second homes. Ms. Gilhooly and Ms. King plan to vacation at their Florida home three times a year for about two weeks each visit. They'll rent it the rest of the time. Airfares may be cheaper than in previous years, but not throughout the year: fares can cost over $1,000 roundtrip at peak times.

Some foreign buyers aren't content with snagging just one home. Jan Martin, a broker and director at SunSense International Property Ltd. in Kettering, in the United Kingdom, says in the past few months, a number of her customers have purchased multiple properties in the U.S.

One of them, Mike Pethick, a 43-year-old former owner of a leisure-wear clothing line whose primary residence is in Swindon, England, bought four condos in Miami as investments early last year. As the U.S. dollar got weaker, Mr. Pethick couldn't resist buying another condo in September. "It made a hell of a difference. It was a lot cheaper to buy then. In fact, if I had some spare cash, I would definitely buy now," says Mr. Pethick, who plans to sell the condos.

Alvina Labsvirs Smith and her husband, who live in Warwickshire, England, were planning to buy one home to take advantage of the weak dollar but ended up buying two in the Orlando area last year. "You get a lot more for your money than you do in England," says Mrs. Smith, a teacher. "Property prices are very high there." The currency exchange made the $219,000 and $367,000 homes a bargain compared to the U.K., she says. They plan to vacation several times a year in one house and rent it the rest of the time; they will rent the other home.

The activity is by no means limited to Florida. Brokers say Manhattan real estate is increasingly being bought by foreigners. Jonathan J. Miller, president of Miller Samuel Inc., a New York-based real-estate appraisal firm, estimates foreigners made up about one-third of the buyers of apartments in Manhattan in 2004, up from one-fourth in 2003.

Pat O'Brien is one of them. After years of buying vacation homes and condos in Europe and his home country of Ireland, Mr. O'Brien recently bought a two-bedroom apartment in a luxury 78-story condominium building not far from Manhattan's Central Park, his first property purchase in the U.S.

"The [money] I put in was worth up to 40% more than it would have been before," says the 50-year-old tax accountant, who lives in Dublin and plans to use the apartment instead of staying in a hotel when he and his family are in town.

"He's shrewd when it comes to arbitrage," says Mr. O'Brien's broker Max Dobens, who is with Prudential Douglas Elliman. "He timed it perfectly." Mr. O'Brien declined to say how much he paid, but Mr. Dobens says two-bedroom apartments in the building are now on the market for between $2 million and $2.5 million.

Foreign demand is helping to keep home prices lofty, exacerbating concerns about a housing bubble in the U.S. while, at the same time, pushing homes in some areas out of reach of local residents. Mitchell Moss, a professor of urban policy and planning at New York University, says increased demand for residential real estate in Manhattan from foreigners, for instance, has had the indirect effect of increasing demand -- and pushing up prices -- for housing in boroughs such as Brooklyn and Queens.

In some cases, foreigners are dominating sales at entire developments. The majority of pre-sales for the first two phases of a six-phase luxury condominium hotel in Lake Buena Vista, Fla., have been to buyers from the U.K., Ireland and Europe. Sales began early last year and are 71% international and 29% domestic, says Larry Cohen, senior vice president of Lake Buena Vista Resort LLC, the Orlando-based developer of that complex as well as numerous other condominium projects in Florida.

"This is unusual for us. In the past, it's been more like 80% domestic and 20% international," he says. Units in the new condo hotel, set to open in November, range from $349,000 for a two-bedroom to $699,000 for penthouses. Some foreigners bought after seeing pictures of model units on the Internet.

Personal Finance
Home Sales Slip From 2004 Level, But Stay Strong
By James R. Hagerty
318 words
8 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Home sales are slowing from the feverish pace set last year but remain high by historical standards, the National Association of Realtors said.

The assessment is based on a new index of pending home sales, released by the NAR for the first time yesterday. The seasonally adjusted index, which equates the 2001 level of activity to 100, stood at 120.6 in January, down 2.1% from December but up 8.6% from January 2004.

The index is based on pending sales of previously occupied homes. Sales are considered pending when a contract has been signed but the transaction hasn't been completed.

The index "tells us that home-sales activity in the near term is expected to be historically high but is trending off of peak levels recorded in 2004," David Lereah, the NAR's chief economist, said in a news release.

The NAR has long produced a monthly report on completed home sales and will continue to do so. But home sales typically aren't completed until a month or two after contracts are signed. The new index is designed to give a more timely signal of home-sales trends. The index shows the pace at which contracts are being signed and should foreshadow the pace of completions.

The NAR said it derives the index from a national sample representing about 20% of home sales.

Separately, David Berson, the chief economist of mortgage-finance company Fannie Mae, said in a weekly commentary that the rise of home purchases by investors has increased the risk of regional home-price declines. Mr. Berson cited a recent estimate by the NAR that 23% of all home purchases last year were for investment. Demand from investors is more volatile than that from owner-occupiers, Mr. Berson said.

The Home Front:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
The Home Front: Private Properties
By Troy McMullen
796 words
4 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W10
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Athletics and Software

Auction Figures

A STAR ATHLETE and a software tycoon are turning to auctions to unload multimillion-dollar properties. Former National Basketball Association star Karl Malone is putting his Salt Lake City home on the block May 19, according to J.P. King Auction Co., the firm handling the sale.

It's the third time he's tried to sell the log and stone mansion; it first went on the market shortly after he was traded to the Los Angeles Lakers from the Utah Jazz in 2003. The original asking price, $6.1 million, was reduced last year to $5.75 million, according to James Marchant, the broker who had the listing, "There aren't many buyers in this price range in Salt Lake," Mr. Marchant says. "Higher-end buyers either build what they want" or buy property in upscale communities like Deer Valley or Park City, he says.

Mr. Malone, 41, ranks second all-time in points scored and was selected as the NBA's Most Valuable Player after the 1997 and 1999 seasons. The custom mansion was built in 1997. It has eight bedrooms and 11 baths and measures 17,230 square feet. The home has three guest suites, basketball and tennis courts and two swimming pools. The sale will be an absolute auction, which means the property will be sold regardless of price. Mr. Malone couldn't be reached to comment.

McAfee and Molokai

On March 10, software developer John McAfee will put more than 1,000 acres of undeveloped land on the Hawaiian island of Molokai on the block, according to National Auction Group in Gadsden, Ala., the company handling the sale. He paid $1.3 million in 2003 for the coastline property, records show. It stretches from the ocean to the mountains and includes two fish ponds. Mr. McAfee, 60, founded the Santa Clara, Calif., software company McAfee Inc., formerly Network Associates and one of the largest antivirus-software companies.

The 1,046 acres will be sold either in its entirety or in five parcels; three oceanfront lots and two lots on the mountain side of the property. "Sometimes your eyes are bigger than your stomach," says Mr. McAfee, who owns two other properties on the island as well as homes in Colorado, Texas and Arizona. "I can only be in one place at a time," he says.

In 2004, $48 billion in real estate was sold at auction, up from $42.3 billion in 2003 and $37.6 billion in 2002, according to the National Auctioneers Association. Owners who don't want to maintain the high costs often associated with expensive properties often turn to auctions, says National Association of Realtors spokesman Walter Molony. (Property taxes on Mr. Malone's home are nearly $34,000 a year, according to the Salt Lake County assessor's office.)

Does This Capri Fit?

THE CAPRI VILLA owned by Italian actress and movie producer Edwige Fenech is on the market for 10 million euros (about $13.1 million). Last year, Ms. Fenech co-produced "The Merchant of Venice," starring Al Pacino and Jeremy Irons. She starred in a string of Italian comedies and thrillers in the 1960s and 1970s, as well as movies like "The Great Battle" in 1977 with Henry Fonda. Ms. Fenech, 54, bought the villa in 1990. Built in 1931, it has 7,000 square feet of living space and seven bedrooms, each with private bath. The home has a private beach, panoramic views of the Bay of Naples and Porto Grande and a 650-square-foot wine cellar. Raphael T. Harris Jr. of www.worldrealestate.it[http://www.worldrealestate.it] in Messina, Italy, has the listing. Ms. Fenech declined to comment.

Home View / Strong Seconds

SALES OF SECOND HOMES surged 16% in 2004 to 2.82 million, with many of those buyers speculating on the real-estate market, according to new surveys by the National Association of Realtors, a trade group that represents real-estate brokers. The study, released earlier this week, shows that 23% of all homes purchased in 2004 were second homes bought for investment, according to the homeowners, while another 13% were vacation homes.

"With real estate seen as a solid investment the past few years, more people are looking at property investment as a way to diversify their portfolios," says the NAR's vice president of research, Ellen Roche. For properties purchased between mid-2003 and mid-2004, the median price of a vacation home was $190,000 compared with $148,000 for investment homes. The surveys, conducted by mail and e-mail, looked at approximately 8,200 home-buyers and at about 3,300 home transactions.

Politics & Economy
U.S. Manufacturing Growth Slowed but Stayed Solid in February
By Kemba J. Dunham
508 words
2 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. manufacturing sector continued to grow in February, although the pace slowed slightly. Still, an increase in exports hints at strong economic activity in the months ahead.

The Institute for Supply Management, which polls purchasing agents at more than 400 industrial companies, said its index of manufacturing activity slipped to 55.3 in February from 56.4 a month earlier. This was the index's lowest reading since September 2003. Economists had expected a slightly higher February reading of 57.

Still, the index was positive, representing the 21st consecutive month of U.S. manufacturing growth. Readings above 50 indicate the sector is expanding. The ISM index is closely watched by economists and investors because it provides an early look of economic activity in the preceding period and tends to track changes in the health of the U.S. industrial core.

New orders, production and employment all increased less rapidly in February, though economists weren't concerned by the results. "We're moving back to solid output, rather than robust growth, and that's just normal," said Joel Naroff, chief economist at Naroff Economic Advisors in Pennsylvania. "In 2005, the manufacturing sector will be supporting economic growth, rather than powering it."

The report said 13 industries grew, while one had no change and six contracted.

The ISM's export-orders index rose to 57.4 in February, from 56.9 a month earlier, marking its 38th consecutive month of growth. The weaker dollar, economists said, is driving the growth of exports and the rate should remain strong this year. "We need the export business to help drive the manufacturing sector," said Norbert Ore, chairman of the institute's survey committee.

Manufacturers still face rising prices, though the pace cooled off a bit in February. The ISM's prices-paid index fell to 65.5 last month, from 69 in January.

Another report showed that U.S. chief executive officers are confident about strong economic growth in the next six months. The Business Roundtable said its March 2005 CEO Economic Outlook index rose to a record 104.4 from 98.9 in December, the last time the survey was released. The quarterly index combines the responses on projected sales, capital spending and employment into an overall index that shows how chief executives believe the U.S. economy will perform in the six months ahead. The Business Roundtable is a Washington lobbying group comprising 160 chief executives from some of the largest U.S. companies.

Separately, the Commerce Department said total construction spending increased 0.7% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.047 trillion (790.8 billion euros) in January. Spending rose 1.2% in December, revised from the 1.1% gain first reported. Economists had expected a 0.6% increase in January because of bad weather in various parts of the country.

---

By Kemba J. Dunham

(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. manufacturing sector continued to expand in February, although the pace slowed slightly. Still, an increase in exports hints at strong economic activity in the months ahead.

The weaker dollar, economists said, is driving the growth of exports and the rate should remain strong this year. According to Ofheo's quarterly index, home prices rose 1.7% in the fourth quarter from the third quarter, much slower than the 4.8% rise in the third quarter. When compared with a year earlier, home prices remained strong, rising 11% in the fourth quarter.

Family Finance:
Personal Finance
Family Finance: Hot Housing Markets Face New Risks --- Study Identifies Regions Most Likely to Tumble; Average Buyers Outpriced
By Jeff D. Opdyke
704 words
2 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Homeowners in the hottest markets are facing a greater chance that the value of their houses will decline, according to a new study, though risks have lessened slightly for the nation as a whole.

In its recently released winter 2005 Risk Index, mortgage insurer PMI Mortgage Insurance Co., a unit of PMI Group Inc., calculates a 16.1% chance of an overall housing-price decline in the top 50 metropolitan markets during the next two years. That is down from the firm's autumn reading of an 18.6% chance of a price decline.

Risks have heightened substantially for homeowners in many of the major metropolitan areas along the East and West coasts. The culprit: continued housing-price increases that are making the average home increasingly less affordable to the average buyer.

That, PMI notes in its report, "signals a further misalignment of home prices with long-term economic fundamentals," increasing the likelihood that home prices will slide at some point to come back into line with what residents can afford.

The Boston-Cambridge-Quincy, Mass./N.H., metropolitan area tops the list as the region with the highest probability of experiencing a housing-price decline. The area scored a 533 on PMI's Risk Index, indicating a 53.3% probability of weaker home prices in the next two years.

That marks a big increase in risk for the New England hub. A year ago, Boston's probability for home-price declines stood at 23.3%. The area's problem "is that it has had strong home-price increases relative to poor income growth," says Marco Van Akkeren, an economist at PMI.

The PMI Risk Index ranges from one to 1,000, with a higher score indicating a greater likelihood of home-price declines. (A score of 100 represents a 10% chance of falling prices in the next two years.) PMI's proprietary calculus takes into account factors such as home-price appreciation, labor-market statistics and an index that shows how affordable a particular housing market is in relation to local incomes.

California's six major metro areas all rank among the top 10 markets most likely to see prices fall. San Diego, in particular, holds the distinction of also having the nation's most unaffordable housing market in terms of annual income. Based on per-capita income of $36,815 in the area, a 30-year mortgage on the median-priced home -- valued at more than $578,000 -- would consume about 90% of the average resident's income.

Away from the coasts, locales in and around Detroit; Minneapolis and St. Paul, Minn.; and Denver all ranked above the national average.

On the opposite end: Oklahoma City; Buffalo-Niagara Falls, N.Y.; and Pittsburgh all tied as cities least likely to experience a decline in home prices. They each scored 57, meaning they have a 5.7% chance of prices falling.

Results for the top 50 metropolitan statistical areas are available online at www.pmigroup.com/lenders/eret.html[http://www.pmigroup.com/lenders/eret.html] .

Weyerhaeuser Takes a Local Approach to Sales
By Paula L. Stepankowsky Dow Jones Newswires
1041 words
2 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2F
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

FEDERAL WAY, Wash. -- Weyerhaeuser Real Estate Co. could be the largest home builder you've never heard of.

The division of forest-products giant Weyerhaeuser Co. likes it that way.

The size of Weyerhaeuser's real-estate business is masked by the fact that the division operates under different names in each market it serves.

So while many people aren't familiar with Weyerhaeuser Real Estate Co., or Wreco, they are familiar with Pardee Homes in the Southern California and Las Vegas markets, Quadrant Homes in Western Washington, Winchester Homes in the Washington, D.C., area and Trendmaker Homes in Texas.

"By having a separate branding strategy and brand name, you can position yourself in a niche market better than if you are Weyerhaeuser," said Steve Atkinson, a forest-products analyst with BMO Nesbitt Burns.

Though it operates under four different names, the division booked nearly $2.5 billion in revenue in 2004, nearly double its total in 2000 and ranking it the 14th-largest home builder in the U.S. by revenue last year, up from 20th in 2001.

Wreco contributed $595 million in pretax operating earnings to its Federal Way, Wash., parent in 2004, the seventh consecutive year for record earnings growth. In the fourth quarter alone, Wreco contributed $217 million in pretax operating earnings to the parent, 40% of the overall company's total.

On a consolidated net basis, Weyerhaeuser earned $1.3 billion, or $5.43 a share, on net sales of $22.7 billion in 2004.

The division is "just one of the better, if lesser-known, parts of the Weyerhaeuser story," said Steve Chercover, forest-products analyst with D.A. Davidson &Co. "It's not a rounding error; it's making up a meaningful and relatively stable chunk of the earnings."

As a result of its performance, Weyerhaeuser Chief Executive Steve Rogel has said the division may expand at a time when the parent is still focused on reducing debt from its acquisition of Willamette Industries three years ago.

"The business unit has earned the right to contribute a greater share of the company's income," said Steve Hansen, a forest-products analyst with Morningstar.

Wreco has benefited from a booming housing market and low interest rates in the past four years. While the Fed has increased rates steadily in the past 12 months, rates on 30-year fixed-rate mortgages are lower now than they were last year this time.

Last week, the U.S. Commerce Department reported that U.S. housing starts rose 4.7% in January to a seasonally adjusted 2.15 million annual rate, the highest level since February 1984. Housing analysts had anticipated a 3.5% decline in January. In 2004, housing starts totaled 1.95 million.

"We think there is some risk of a slowdown in 2005 with rates increasing, which could translate into somewhat lower margins, but not lower volume," said Daniel Fulton, Wreco president and chief executive, in an interview.

Wreco has been affected by delays caused by the storms that have hammered Southern California, a state that has seen hefty housing price increases that likely will level off in 2005, said Mr. Fulton, who has been at Wreco for 29 years of its 35-year history.

Mr. Fulton said the Las Vegas market may be cooling off, but the Los Angeles market is still very strong, as is the Washington, D.C., market.

While there might be some short-term slowing this year, the housing picture is bright over the long term, Mr. Fulton said. Growth factors include population growth, decreasing household size and an increasing level of homeownership. The mortgage industry also is offering new products that make it easier to get into a first house, he said.

"These all drive the view that the housing business looks very attractive," he said.

Weyerhaeuser is unique among its forest-products peers in its ownership of a real-estate division. The Wreco connection to parent Weyerhaeuser gives it several advantages, he said. "We try to take advantage of the size, stability and financial resources of Weyerhaeuser while encouraging and empowering local managers," he said.

The local managers find the land and get financing through Wreco and Weyerhaeuser, and then they determine what product to build and how to market it, he said. Wreco also benefits from access to its parent's corporate services, benefits administration and human resources, he said.

A lot of Weyerhaeuser wood also ends up in Wreco homes, though Wreco's wood pricing is similar to that of other large builders. When it is working with a contractor, however, it can specify some particular Weyerhaeuser products, such as Trus Joist or Silent Floor System, Mr. Fulton said.

In addition to developing large master-planned communities for single-family homes, Wreco also sells land to other developers and offers capital to builders through its Weyerhaeuser Realty Investors division, a part of Wreco.

"It's a good balance they have," said Mr. Atkinson at BMO Nesbitt Burns. Mr. Atkinson doesn't own Weyerhaeuser stock, but BMO Nesbitt Burns has done banking business for Weyerhaeuser in the past year. "If you only have one product, the earnings become much more volatile, and investors like to have something a bit more stable."

Wreco's growth strategy is to build in existing markets, followed by growth in adjacent markets using the same management team, Mr. Fulton said. It also will continue to tailor its houses to the individual markets. Quadrant Homes, for example, are moderately priced and appeal to value buyers, while Winchester Homes in the Washington, D.C., appeal to high-end custom home buyers.

The company also is evaluating possibilities for entirely new markets, and will move when capital is available, Mr. Fulton said.

The house-building market is still highly fragmented, so acquisitions are likely for Wreco, said Arthur Oduma, a housing-industry analyst with Morningstar. "Size is might in the home-building industry, and the way to do that is to acquire smaller home builders," said Mr. Oduma, who doesn't own Weyerhaeuser stock and whose company doesn't provide banking services.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

International News
U.S. Manufacturing Growth Slows --- Inflation Pressures Retreat, Inventories Meet Demand; CEOs Express Optimism
By Michael S. Derby, John McAuley and Kemba J. Dunham
532 words
2 March 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

U.S. manufacturing activity grew at a slower pace in February, an unexpected development that was leavened by signs that price pressures retreated.

The Institute for Supply Management, a private research group, said its manufacturing-activity index for February stood at 55.3, which represented a slowdown from January's 56.4 reading. The index was 57.3 in December.

Readings above 50 indicate growth and describe the breadth of the gain. The index doesn't capture the size of the change seen by individual companies surveyed by the ISM.

"February was another good month in the manufacturing sector," said Norbert Ore, who directs the survey. "While the overall rate of growth is slowing, the overall picture is improving as price increases and shortages are becoming less of a problem."

February's readings came in below expectations. Forecasters in a survey conducted by Dow Jones Newswires had expected to see a reading of 57.

The ISM said that most aspects of the manufacturing sector grew last month, albeit at a slower pace than in January. The group's new-orders index slipped to 55.8 after 56.5 in January, while the production index fell to 56.7 from 57.8.

On the inflation front, manufacturers faced the 36th straight month of rising prices, although the pace cooled somewhat in February. The group's prices index was 65.5 in February, compared with 69 in January and 72 in December.

Manufacturing payrolls also grew at a softer rate, with that gauge ebbing to 57.4, compared with 58.1 in January. It was the 16th month of growth.

Despite the slower rate of hiring growth, Mr. Ore said, "employment is not really weak. . . . After some very strong months, the index now reflects a more normal level." He added, "I look forward to a good year for manufacturing."

Inventories contracted in February, with a reading of 48.6 versus 52.8 the month before.

"Inventories are doing what they're supposed to do," said Mr. Ore. "Inventory growth is uncommon now that they've caught up with demand, I expect inventories will continue to stay negative."

Separately, the Business Roundtable said its March 2005 CEO Economic Outlook index rose to 104.4, a record high, from 98.9 in December 2004, the last time the survey was released.

The roundtable is an association of chief executives of U.S. corporations. The index combines the responses on projected sales, capital spending and employment into an index that shows how CEOs believe the U.S. economy will perform in the six months ahead. It has been conducted quarterly since the fourth quarter of 2002.

The U.S. Commerce Department said total construction spending increased 0.7% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.047 trillion in January. Spending for December was revised to a 1.2% increase, instead of the 1.1% gain earlier reported.
