The Economy
Revised GDP Picks Up Pace As Business Investment Jumps --- Consumer-Spending Rate Appears to Be a Bit Slower; Snow Points to Momentum
By Michael Schroeder
458 words
28 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- The government's revised estimate of economic growth during the final quarter of last year showed an encouraging pickup for business investment but slower consumer-spending growth.

U.S. gross domestic product increased at a 3.8% annual clip in the fourth quarter, significantly faster than the 3.1% rate that initially was estimated, the U.S. Commerce Department said.

That compares with a growth rate of 4% in the third quarter. For all of 2004, the economy expanded 4.4%, the fastest pace since 1999.

The inflation component gave little reason for alarm, with the price index for personal consumption rising 2.5% during the fourth quarter from a year earlier, compared with a 1.3% increase during the third quarter. Excluding food and energy, prices advanced 1.6% and 0.9%, respectively.

In a prepared statement, U.S. Treasury Secretary John Snow said the robust growth in business spending on capital goods along with other recent positive economic reports shows that the economy's "momentum is continuing into 2005."

Separately, sales of existing homes eased 0.1% in January but maintained a healthy pace as mortgage rates remained relatively low, said the National Association of Realtors, a trade group. Sales were at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 6.8 million last month, the seventh-best rate on record. That compared with a revised December rate of 6.81 million, which originally had been put at 6.7 million.

The upward revision in fourth-quarter GDP was driven by adjustments to business spending, exports and inventory investment.

Economists were cheered by the healthy rate of business fixed investment -- which rose 14%, compared with an initial estimate of 10.3%. The rate was 13% for the third quarter. Investment in structures climbed 1.2% in the fourth quarter, and spending on equipment and software rose 18%.

Consumer-spending growth was 4.2%, slightly slower than the previous estimate of 4.6%. Spending rose 5.1% in the third quarter. Purchases of durable goods increased 3.1% and nondurables rose 6.1% for the latest quarter.

"The economy, as portrayed in aggregate statistics, is a fairly slow-moving creature," said Mat Johnson, an economist at ThinkEquity Partners, an institutional investment firm in San Francisco. "It will take some sizable factors to send these discernible trends in the other direction."

In another bright spot, exports rose 2.4%, while imports increased 11.4%, in the fourth quarter. The Commerce Department originally estimated that exports fell 3.9% and that imports rose 9.1%.

International News
U.S. Data Boost View of Economy --- Revised GDP Figures Show 3.8% Growth in 4th Quarter With Business Spending Up
By Michael Schroeder
497 words
28 February 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal

Investment in structures climbed 1.2%, and equipment and software rose 18%, the government report said.

Consumer-spending growth slowed to 4.2% in the fourth quarter, slightly lower than the previous estimate of 4.6%. Spending rose 5.1% in the third quarter. Purchases of durable goods increased 3.1% and nondurables rose 6.1% for the quarter, the report said.

"The economy, as portrayed in aggregate statistics, is a fairly slow-moving creature," said Mat Johnson, an economist at ThinkEquity Partners, an institutional investment firm in San Francisco. "It will take some sizable factors to send these discernable `trends' in the other direction."

In an additional bright spot, exports rose 2.4%, while imports increased 11.4% during the fourth quarter. The Commerce Department originally estimated that exports fell 3.9% and that imports rose 9.1%. During the third quarter, exports rose 6% and imports climbed 4.6%, the report said.

Industrial Production Turns Flat in the U.S. --- Housing Data Provide A Bright Spot for Economy Amid Low Mortgage Rates
By Jeff Bater Dow Jones Newswires
731 words
17 February 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- U.S. industrial output was unchanged in January after three consecutive months of growth, the Federal Reserve said.

The U.S. central bank also said production grew more slowly in December than previously thought, cutting the initial estimate a tenth of a percentage point to 0.7%.

As a result, industrial-capacity utilization, a measure of economic slack, declined for the first time since September. That measure fell by a tenth of a percentage point to 79%, a level that was two points below average for the past three decades, the Fed said.

Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires and CNBC had called for a 0.4% increase in production and a 79.3% capacity-utilization rate. But they were consistent with the Fed's view that inflation isn't likely to surge anytime soon given the gradual pace of U.S. economic growth.

Factory production increased 0.4% in January, the same rate as in December. That drove the capacity-utilization rate for factories to 78%, the highest level since December 2000. Even so, the Fed said, the utilization rate was 1.8 percentage points below the average for the past three decades.

Production of automobiles and light trucks slowed to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 11.42 million units from 11.69 million units in December, the Fed said. Excluding motor vehicles and parts, industrial production rose 0.2% in January after a 0.7% increase in December.

Separately, new residential construction notched a surprising increase last month as low mortgage rates fueled increased activity in the Southern U.S., helping housing starts climb to the highest mark in nearly 21 years.

The Commerce Department said yesterday that housing starts rose 4.7% last month to a seasonally adjusted 2.159 million annual rate. It was the highest level of starts since February 1984's 2.26 million units. December starts were revised up to show a 14.3% increase to 2.063 million units. Economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires and CNBC predicted starts would decrease, falling by 3.5% to a 1.93 million annual rate.

Stormy, snowy weather in the Northeast and Midwest and heavy rain in the West had been expected to put a damper on building activity -- and indeed, starts did decline in the Northeast and Midwest. Analysts expecting a slowdown pointed to the government's recent report on payroll employment that showed the U.S. construction industry shed 9,000 jobs last month.

But robust activity in the South offset the declines in other regions. Starts climbed 19% in the South to 1.139 million units -- the region's highest level since February 1984's 1.187 million. Housing starts in the South accounted for more than half of the nation's building activity last month. Meanwhile, new construction fell 24% to 150,000 in the Northeast and 13% to 330,000 in the Midwest. Builders in the West broke ground on about 540,000 new homes, a 1.9% increase.

With mortgage rates staying low, economists expect the housing market to continue to be a bright spot for the broader economy. Freddie Mac reported last week that rates on 30-year mortgages had fallen for a sixth consecutive week to 5.57%. Rates have hovered below 6% since July, propelling sales of both new and existing homes to all-time highs in 2004. It was the fourth straight year that sales in both categories have set records.

Another possibly bullish sign: Commerce reported building permits rose by 1.7% to a 2.105 million annual rate. That number also surprised analysts, who projected a 1.5% decline. Permit levels don't always correlate perfectly with actual activity, economists say. Permits fell in December by a revised 0.1%, for example, but building activity has continued at a brisk pace.

The report also showed single-family housing starts rose 2.7% in January to 1.760 million. Apartment-building construction was up by 17.9%. Nationwide, an estimated 140,700 houses were started in January, based on unseasonally adjusted figures.

Politics & Economy
Housing Starts In U.S. Jumped 4.7% in January
By Michael Schroeder
423 words
17 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Low mortgage rates in the U.S. and the strong economy helped push housing construction up in January to the highest level since early 1984.

Separately, industrial output was unchanged in January from the previous month as utilities output fell 3%, largely due to relatively warm weather, the Federal Reserve said yesterday. Industrial capacity utilization was down for the first time since September, dropping 0.1% to 79.0%.

Housing starts rose 4.7% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 2.16 million units, from the upwardly revised 2.06 million units in December.

Building remained strong despite damaging rain in the West and snowstorms in the East. Construction of single-family homes rose 2.7% to 1.76 million units. Construction of apartments rose 14% to 399,000 units.

Robust housing activity is expected to continue. Applications for building permits rose by 1.7% to an annual rate of 2.1 million units in January. The number of single-family home permits was up 0.7%, while apartment permits rose 5.3%.

Historically low mortgage rates have served as an engine for housing activity. Despite the Fed's policy of raising rates in small increments since June to ease concerns over rising inflation, mortgage rates haven't climbed much.

Freddie Mac reported last week that rates on 30-year mortgages had fallen for a sixth consecutive week to 5.57%.

Meanwhile, the industrial output report masked underlying strength. The broad index was held back by a utilities production decline of more than 3% in January, following a 2.8% increase in December. "The lack of growth in January industrial production was due to a weather-related decline in electric and gas utility production," said Daniel Meckstroth, chief economist for the Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI, an Arlington, Virginia, association of manufacturers.

"With economywide inventories at very low levels, and across virtually every industry, continued production activity and business investment look to be the norm in the months and quarters ahead," said Mat Johnson, chief economist at ThinkEquity Partners, a San Francisco investment bank.

Production of autos and light trucks slowed to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 11.42 million units from 11.69 million in December, the Fed said. Excluding motor vehicles and parts, industrial production rose 0.2% in January after a 0.7% increase in December.

The Economy
Greenspan Signals Rates Will Rise --- Uncertainty Over Dollar, Productivity Growth Cited; Fewer Words About Pacing
By Greg Ip
1096 words
17 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan cited uncertainty over productivity growth and the dollar, indicating U.S. interest rates would keep rising in the near future to keep inflation under wraps.

Testifying before Congress, Mr. Greenspan gave fewer signals on the path of interest rates than he has in previous appearances. He didn't tell lawmakers whether interest rates would keep rising at a "measured" pace, as the Fed has said since last spring. "Measured" has come to indicate an increase of no more than a quarter of a percentage point per meeting.

Mr. Greenspan said the Fed's short-term interest rate target has risen "significantly" to 2.5% this month from 1% in June, "but by most measures, it remains fairly low" relative to inflation. That suggests that he thinks it still must rise somewhat before it is no longer stimulating overall spending and thus posing an inflation risk.

The Fed's semi-annual monetary policy report, the subject of Mr. Greenspan's appearance before the Senate Banking Committee yesterday, shows that the central bank remains optimistic on both economic growth and inflation. Most of the 19 voting and nonvoting Federal Open Market Committee members believe the economy will grow 3.75% to 4% this year, with inflation remaining in the 1.5% to 1.75% range -- using their preferred measure. They see growth slowing only slightly to 3.5% next year and inflation in the same 1.5% to 1.75% range.

Still, Mr. Greenspan's testimony suggested he is on the lookout for evidence of inflation pressure. In particular, he noted that growth in productivity, or worker output per hour, has slowed sharply in the past year. Slowing productivity growth means companies must use more labor to increase production, which could drive up costs. Firms would then have to accept narrower profit margins or raise prices.

"Productivity is notoriously difficult to predict," he said. Its rapid growth from early 2003 through mid-2004, he said, reflected companies realizing "delayed efficiencies gains" from the 1990s high-tech investment boom. Its recent slowdown may mean "conceivably the backlog of untapped total efficiencies has run low," Mr. Greenspan said.

The Fed chairman's uncertainty over future productivity growth is noteworthy since, as Goldman Sachs economists told clients, Mr. Greenspan "has been consistently optimistic about productivity growth" and was among the first to spot its acceleration a decade ago. Still, the text of the 25-page monetary-policy report was more upbeat: "Gains in structural productivity should continue, although not necessarily at the pace of recent years."

Mr. Greenspan also suggested, as he first did two weeks ago in London, that further declines in the dollar will be more inflationary than declines to date. The "somewhat quickened" growth in import prices, he said, indicates foreign firms that export to the U.S. have "only limited tolerance" to absorb further dollar depreciation in their profit margins.

The Fed chief dwelt at length on the unusual decline in long-term bond yields since the Fed began raising short-term rates, which "contrasts with most experience." He played down technical explanations such as purchases of Treasury bonds by Asian central banks, mortgage hedging, and even confidence in low inflation.

Mr. Greenspan concluded the drop is "a conundrum," which may prove to be a "short-term aberration." He noted that measures of risk are unusually low across many markets, and, in a remark reminiscent of his warning about "irrational exuberance" in 1996, warned: "People experiencing long periods of relative stability are prone to excess. We must thus remain vigilant against complacency."

As significant as what Mr. Greenspan said is what he didn't say. Since July 2003, he has used his appearances before Congress to give guidance on the course of interest rates. In previous appearances, he has said the Fed would keep rates low for a "considerable period," then be "patient" in raising them, and now it will raise them at a "measured" pace. The paucity of such guidance yesterday may mean the central bank is less sure of what it, if anything, to say about its future actions.

On the one hand, the rise in rates to date means further increases are less urgent. But with the economy stronger and productivity growth slower, the Fed no longer has the sizable buffer against inflation that made it confident it could raise rates slowly.

Some Fed officials have frowned on the use of forward-looking language, worrying that it limits their flexibility. The majority has concluded such guidance has kept markets calm and leveraged their rate actions. Asked by Banking Chairman Richard Shelby (R., Ala.) whether the Fed's post-meeting statements would cease to refer to "measured" rate increases or an "accommodative" stance of policy, Mr. Greenspan said, "We're not going to have the same statement in perpetuity. At some point, it's going to change." But he declined to say when.

Paul Ashworth, an economist at London-based Capital Economics, predicted that the "measured" wording would stay "for a while yet. . . . If the Fed was thinking of dropping it soon, Greenspan would surely have begun to prepare the ground."

Separately, the Commerce Department reported that January housing starts rose to their highest level since early 1984. They rose 4.7% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 2.16 million units from 2.06 million in December. Building remained strong despite damaging rain in the West and snowstorms in the East. Construction of single-family homes rose 2.7% to 1.76 million units. Construction of apartments rose 14% to 399,000 units.

Also, industrial production was unchanged in January from the previous month as utilities output fell 3%, largely because of relatively warm weather, the Federal Reserve said. Industry used 79% of capacity, down from 79.1% in December. Manufacturing production rose 0.4%, and manufacturers operated at 78% of capacity, the highest utilization rate since December 2000.

Personal Finance
Median Price of Home Rose 8.8% To $187,500 in the Fourth Quarter
By Kemba J. Dunham
560 words
16 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Home-price appreciation skyrocketed at the end of 2004, as buyers competed for a tighter supply of homes.

According to the National Association of Realtors, the median price of a single-family home rose 8.8% to $187,500 in the fourth quarter of 2004, compared with the year-earlier quarter. Prices appreciated much faster in the hottest housing markets, and a record number of metropolitan areas experienced double-digit price growth. Of the 129 metro areas that the NAR tracks, 62 saw home prices rise 10% or more. That was the largest number of cities with double-digit price increases since the NAR began publishing such data in 1982.

Home prices have been rising across the nation for several years, but economists at the NAR say the latest price surge reflects tight supply. For all of 2004, there was a 4.3-month supply of homes available for sale, the lowest annual supply on record.

The biggest price increase was in the Las Vegas region, where the median price of a single-family home was $281,400 in the fourth quarter, up 47.3% from a year earlier. The second-strongest increase occurred in the Riverside-San Bernardino, Calif., area, where the median price was $322,400, up 34.7%. In third place was the West Palm Beach-Boca Raton-Delray Beach area of Florida, with a fourth-quarter median price of $338,800, a 34% increase over the previous year. The median is the midway price, with half of all homes selling for more than the median and half selling for less. In the third quarter of 2004, the national annual rate of home-price appreciation was 7.5%

Prices declined in four metropolitan areas: Indianapolis; Austin, Texas; Beaumont-Port Arthur, Texas; and Charleston, W.Va. NAR economist David Lereah said these areas currently are facing either local economic weakness or have a large supply of homes on the market.

WEEKEND JOURNAL
Home Prices: Our Towns
By June Fletcher
2716 words
11 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

[As home prices soar, we survey more than 1,000 neighborhoods, finding big gains from Miami to Wayzata, Minn. June Fletcher on unexpected hot spots -- and whether the boom can last.]

ROBERT ALBERTSON put his five-bedroom lakeside house in tiny Wayzata, Minn., up for sale last year at $1.4 million -- and, almost immediately, pulled it off the market. Calls from eager buyers convinced Mr. Albertson, who helped invent Mr. Coffee, that it was worth waiting to see if prices would rise.

They did: A few months' delay added another $100,000 to the value of the Tudor-style home, according to two recent appraisals, and he plans to put it back on the market at $1.5 million. Says Mr. Albertson, who bought the house in 1963 for $38,000: "Prices here have just gone crazy."

What home-price bubble? So far, predictions of the U.S. housing market's demise have been greatly exaggerated. Despite some dark clouds -- war, a looming deficit, the weak dollar -- this market has continued to rise, with existing-home prices up 8.3% through the end of 2004, according to the National Association of Realtors, compared with 7.5% the year before. As low interest rates fueled sales, dozens of cities saw double-digit gains: In Glendale, Calif., where prices had already doubled in the past four years, the median home price rose 29.8% last year, to $440,000. In the old whaling town Marion, Mass., prices rose 13.5%, to an average of $550,000.

No one can say for sure if this can continue. But at least some homeowners can brag that their neighborhood did even better than most. In a town-by-town survey conducted for Weekend Journal by housing specialists Fiserv CSW, we analyzed home-price changes in more than 1,200 ZIP codes for the year ending September 2004. To get a sense of the longer-term phenomenon, we also looked back at the change in these areas compared with five years ago.

Some of the results were striking. From Florida's Gulf Coast to the Jersey Shore, some of the fastest-growing neighborhoods are ones that used to be second choices. Some people are relocating less for jobs than for views: Waterfront access was the feature many of our fastest-appreciating towns shared, along with new commuter lines or magnet schools. Spillover was a factor in some booming areas, as many of our "hot" neighborhoods turned out to be right next door to longtime enclaves of wealth (see: Montclair, N.J.; south Orange County). Meanwhile, only 10 neighborhoods we surveyed showed price declines; four of those were in Salt Lake City suburbs, which saw heavy building ahead of the 2002 Winter Olympics.

The housing market's continued growth has surprised some economists who thought, last year at this time, that home prices were headed for a fall. Many of these people still believe prices will drop this year, as expected mortgage-rate increases kick in. "It's like the stock market in the late '90s," says economist Dean Baker, co-director of the Center for Economic and Policy Research in Washington, D.C. He points out that overall, housing prices have grown by 40% since 1995, adjusted for inflation, the steepest rise in five decades. "It's too overheated," he says.

However, other economists say they don't see home prices falling anytime soon -- though they do expect gains to be more modest. Lawrence Yun, senior forecast economist for the National Association of Realtors, expects growth to slow to 5% over the next year based in part on anticipated interest-rate rises. However, he doesn't see prices falling, he says, because job creation is expected to rise, boomers are still in their peak buying years and the inventory of homes available for purchase is currently at a historic low.

Even economists who believe the market is still primed for growth say some areas are overheated. They point to Las Vegas and Southern California, for example, where speculators are "flipping" homes for profit shortly after buying them. In Las Vegas, 16.1% of all housing loans last year went to investors rather than those buying a primary residence, nearly double the national average, according to San Francisco research firm Loan Performance Inc.

For our survey, Fiserv CSW looked at price changes for the five years ended in September for 1,237 ZIP codes across the country -- areas where median home sales were higher than in surrounding areas, and where 100 or more home transactions had typically been recorded. Overall, home prices in these areas were up 14.7% in the year ended September 2004.

In general, neighborhoods in the West showed the strongest growth, but grew only half as fast in the Northwest, still reeling from the tech-industry slowdown. In the South, coastal towns performed the best. Price gains in the Midwest and Southwest, with the exception of bright spots like Scottsdale, Ariz., and Oak Park, Ill., were sluggish. And the priciest town in America? In our survey, it's Santa Monica, Calif., with a median home price of $1.928 million -- up nearly 30% in the last year alone.

In Rockville, Md., homeowner Bryan Hubbard says local home prices have grown "much faster than I anticipated." Mr. Hubbard, a spokesman for the U.S. Defense Department, says he's now asking $990,000 for his seven-bedroom Victorian home in the Washington, D.C., suburb, double what he paid for it four years ago, and is marketing the home without the help of a real-estate agent. If his family moved into town today, Mr. Hubbard adds, "I don't think we'd be able to afford the house we have."

Here is our region-by-region look at the market -- including ZIP codes with median home prices above $400,000 and particularly strong price growth over the past five years:

Southeast

St. Pete Beach, Fla. 33706

ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 22.7%

FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 105.6%

SIDEWALK VIEW: Sleepy beach town wakes up

"Life is short -- max out your credit cards," reads the sign in a jeweler in this seaside town, situated on a two-mile barrier island in the Gulf of Mexico. Once a sleepy retirement mecca and vacation retreat for the likes of Clarence Darrow and Al Capone, the 17,000-resident town is benefiting from the growing local tech industry.

Median home prices here have more than doubled in five years, to $410,000, as foreign investors take advantage of a weak dollar and bidding wars are common. New condominium projects are pulling median prices up even more, with starting prices of around $700,000.

Kathleen Johnson, formerly a real-estate investor in San Francisco, scouted areas along the Gulf of Florida before settling in St. Pete Beach five years ago. She bought two properties in 2002 -- a waterfront condo for $197,000 and an inland home for $239,000 -- and sold them last year for a combined $164,000 gain. Many neighbors, she says, are also speculating in real estate. "It's easy to find the buyers," she says, but "harder and harder to find the homes."

Northwest

Lake Oswego, Ore. 97034

ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 11.1%

FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 41.8%

SIDEWALK VIEW: Hobbit village by a lake

This bedroom community eight miles south of downtown Portland is one of the best-performing areas in the Northwest, which has seen slowdowns in the software, aerospace and lumber industries. Once a steel town, Lake Oswego now has a series of sloped-roof, English-style cottages surrounded by a dense pine forest. Construction crews are everywhere, adding new turrets to Tudor-style mansions or demolishing cottages. A downtown renovation is transforming old industrial areas into parks and offices. "There's some big money out here, and they don't want to look" at auto shops and machinery, says local architect Ray Totten.

Last year, 54 homes sold for more than $1 million here -- but the only people with access to the lake are homeowners with frontage property. Most of the buyers for the area's homes (median price last year: $525,000) are year-round residents who instead say they're attracted to the area's top-ranked schools.

Physician Arthur Rott wants to sell the 3,100-square-foot home where he's lived for 15 years, but he's not sure if he should bother to update the kitchen and baths. Looking out his bay window at a home being torn down across the street, he says, "My house is a perfect `Leave It To Beaver' kind of place -- but that's not enough around here anymore."

Midwest

Wayzata, Minn. 55391

ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 9.6%

FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 67.5%

SIDEWALK VIEW: Construction hits Mayberry

There's not a McDonald's or Burger King anywhere along the 10-block stretch of road bordering the lake in Wayzata, Minn., and "two cars at a stop sign count as a traffic jam," says real-estate broker Keith Anderson. Just 12 miles west of Minneapolis, Wayzata is the gateway to Lake Minnetonka, one of the most popular recreation spots in the state. Residents spend their summers sailing, riding the red-and-gold trolley to the town center or cruising on a restored 1906 steamboat, the Minnehaha.

This quaintness comes at a price. The 4,100-resident town had one of the highest rates of appreciation in the state over the last five years. At $522,000, the median home price is almost 2 1/2 times that of the surrounding area, and brick and Tudor-style mansions here have sold for up to $12 million. Some critics worry about young professionals buying up remaining buildable lots to put up big homes that lack small-town character. Says architect Sarah Susanka: "They're grander, but blander."

Mid-Atlantic

Rockville, Md. 20850

ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 20.5%

FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 105.4%

SIDEWALK VIEW: Historic town, with gridlock

Just 20 miles northwest of the White House, Rockville is one of the newer stops on the Washington, D.C., Metro line and one of the capital's major bedroom communities. The 200-year-old town includes Victorian-era homes and a historic cemetery -- F. Scott Fitzgerald and his wife, Zelda, are buried there -- but the town's overall feel is more modern. The main thoroughfare is lined with strip malls, and known for its near-daily traffic jams.

(MORE)

Many of Rockville's 55,000 residents work for the federal government, but technology is a growing presence. In the last year, the area added 615,000 square feet of office space, more than twice the amount of neighboring Gaithersburg. Planners are also demanding some more pedestrian-friendly projects: In the town's center, a $353 million redevelopment is under way to create a leafy plaza -- the plan is to hold a farmer's market there -- surrounded by red-brick buildings for a library and shops. Another developer has hired architect Andres Duany, founder of the New Urbanist movement, to design a center that will mix residential lofts, offices and stores.

On average, homes in Rockville (median price in our survey: $485,000) are selling for 99.7% of their asking price, says real-estate agent Wendy Banner -- well above the more typical national level of 90% to 95%.

Northeast

Montclair, N.J. 07042

ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 18.6%

FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 104.3%

SIDEWALK VIEW: Small-town English shire, with commuters

Long considered the poor cousin of tonier New Jersey suburbs such as nearby Short Hills, Montclair is now one of the fastest-appreciating places in the state -- with a median home price of $410,000 in this ZIP code. Brokers say one big draw is its magnet schools: Residents can choose to send their children to any of the town's schools, which specialize in subjects like international studies and science. Last year's addition of a direct-line train to midtown Manhattan also boosted housing demand.

Despite New York's proximity and some famous residents -- Hall of Fame Yankee catcher Yogi Berra, cosmetics-company founder Bobbi Brown -- Montclair's tone is small-town retro, with wide sidewalks, streetlights and stores like the Soda Pop Shop. Come spring, shop owners compete to create the prettiest flower basket.

Since Montclair's boom has been relatively recent, many of its dated houses need considerable work. Sybil Eng, an epidemiologist who commutes to New York, paid $811,000 for a six-bedroom, 1927 colonial last summer, even though it needed renovations -- starting with the cracked bathroom tiles, white linoleum kitchen floors and oven too small for a Thanksgiving turkey.

West

Capistrano Beach, Calif. 92624

ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 39.2%

FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 161.2%

SIDEWALK VIEW: Luxury living creeps into low-key town

As the old song suggests, every spring the swallows return to Capistrano. Homeowners have flocked here for a different reason: While the 7,445-population town has only a handful of businesses and few local jobs, the beachfront area has become increasingly desirable as prices have grown in better-known neighboring towns. One of the most expensive homes in Orange County is next door in Monarch Beach, a $30 million mansion owned by Stephen Udvar-Hazy, founder of International Lease Finance Corp. A few miles to the north in picturesque Laguna Niguel, six properties are currently for sale for more than $10 million. But an oceanfront home in Capistrano Beach recently sold for $6 million. "Around here, that's relatively affordable," says real-estate agent Joe Smith.

In downtown "Capo Beach," as the locals call it, the dominant business is the thatched Coconuts restaurant, and many of the homes are custom-made, with touches like navy blue tile trim, leaded windows or widow's walks. Even with the price spikes, most homes sell swiftly, agents say. But there's some evidence that the speculative froth may be subsiding. Contractor Larry Sackett, who says he "flipped" about 43 houses here during Southern California's boom of 1988-1993, says he isn't buying anything now. "I think the market is past its peak," he says. "I don't want to get burned."

Personal Finance
Some Housing Markets Overheat --- More Than a Dozen Cities, Eight in California, Have Excessive Home Prices
By James R. Hagerty
788 words
10 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Home prices appear to be excessive in more than a dozen U.S. cities, including San Francisco, Miami and Los Angeles, according to a study by one of the nation's largest mortgage lenders.

At the same time, home prices are at what seem to be bargain levels in cities such as Albuquerque, N.M.; Memphis, Tenn.; and Salt Lake City, says the study by economists at National City Corp., a Cleveland banking company.

There is no sign of a national "bubble" in home prices, says Richard DeKaser, chief economist at National City, but "there is a growing risk of `bubblettes' in certain places."

Mr. DeKaser studied data for the past 25 years on 99 metropolitan areas to determine price levels that would be expected for each city based on such factors as population density, incomes, interest rates and past premiums or discounts compared with other parts of the country. He then looked at the difference between those expected levels and actual house prices in 2004.

Chico, Calif., a small city in an almond-growing area about 90 miles north of Sacramento, is the most overvalued area, with house prices 43% above the expected price level, the study found. Eight of the top 10 cities deemed overvalued were in California.

Some other cities where prices have been rising fast were nonetheless closer to expected levels. New York was 16% above the expected level, while Boston was 14% above; Chicago, 11%; and Washington, D.C., 10%.

Markets with an overvaluation of 20% or more account for about a fifth of the total U.S. housing stock, according to the study. In most of the country, Mr. DeKaser says, houses have remained affordable, largely because low interest rates have held down monthly mortgage payments. On a national basis, 22% of the median household income was needed to meet payments on the typical single-family home last year, he says. That's down from an average of about 35% in the 1980s, when interest rates were much higher.

Metropolitan areas where prices appear excessive face the risk of falling prices, Mr. DeKaser says, but that doesn't mean prices necessarily will decline. "The true test of today's premiums in these markets will be the economic environment, especially incomes and interest rates, in the years ahead," he says.

Mr. DeKaser acknowledges that his research couldn't include all factors that may explain differences in price appreciation. For instance, house buyers in California have been faster than those elsewhere to embrace "interest only" loans that let borrowers delay paying back the principal, allowing them to buy more expensive homes than would otherwise be possible.

Home prices in Chico have risen fast partly because the city has attracted people from the coastal areas of California looking for a more "laid-back lifestyle," says Rodney Krebs, a broker associate at Coldwell Banker DuFour Realty, a local brokerage firm. Land available for development is scarce, and builders haven't been able to keep up with demand, he says. Locals with relatively modest incomes have trouble competing for houses with newcomers flush with cash from selling homes in high-cost areas.

In Salt Lake City, a loss of technology jobs and a slowdown of business activity after the 2002 Olympics helped hold down house price gains in recent years, says Jaren L. Davis, president of the Utah Association of Realtors. He predicts that affordable housing will draw more employers to Utah.

John Gellhausen, an executive vice president responsible for consumer lending at National City, says the banking company is using the data in analyzing its portfolio of loans but doesn't plan to reduce lending in the areas deemed overvalued. He says most mortgage loans made by National City are sold to Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and other investors. National City's First Franklin subprime-lending unit has a big presence in California.

Personal Finance
Condos Exceed Single-Family Homes in Price
By Ray A. Smith
474 words
10 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Sales of existing condominiums and co-ops rose 8% in 2004, the ninth consecutive year of record sales -- and the median price for a condo exceeded the median price of a single-family home for the first time since such records were kept.

A total of 970,000 existing condos and co-ops were sold in 2004, according to the National Association of Realtors, up from the previous record of 898,000 units in 2003. The median condo price rose 17% to $193,600, a record high, from $164,100 in 2003. In contrast, the resale price for a single-family home was $184,100.

Last year marked the first time since such numbers were tracked beginning in the 1980s that the median sales price of a condo in the U.S. surpassed that of a median home price. The median price of a condo also rose faster than the price of a home in 2004 -- 17% compared with 8.3%.

The existing condo market is about one-seventh the size of the existing home market, and existing home sales actually grew slightly faster than existing condo sales last year. One reason condos have grown more expensive is that they tend to be located in more-expensive urban areas.

David Lereah, the National Association of Realtors' chief economist, said the sales performance reflects the significance of condo sales in the overall housing market.

"The condo market has clearly matured over the last decade, accounting for a market share almost as big as the new-home market," he said.

Celia Chen, a senior economist with forecasting and consulting firm Economy.com of West Chester, Pa., said she was surprised to see the 2004 median existing home price was lower than the existing condo price. "This says condo demand is very strong in downtown urban areas, and some of that may be due to empty nesters returning to urban cores and driving up demand for luxury apartment buildings," she said.

Ms. Chen and others, including the National Association of Realtors, stress that strong national prices may be skewed somewhat by condo-heavy markets, such as New York and Chicago.

The fourth quarter of 2004 marked the second quarter since the early 1980s that the price of a condo exceeded the price of a home. The median price of a condo rose 16.7% in the fourth quarter to $203,200. The typical single-family home cost $187,500 in the fourth quarter, 8.8% higher than a year earlier, according to the National Association of Realtors.

In the fourth quarter of 2003, the median price of a condo surpassed the price of single-family home but just in that quarter, not for the full year.

Manufacturing Sector Expands, With a Brightening Job Picture
By Michael Schroeder
424 words
2 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B3
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. manufacturing sector continued to expand in January and manufacturing employment appears to be on the rise, according to a survey.

The Institute for Supply Management, which polls purchasing agents at more than 400 industrial companies, said its index of manufacturing activity slipped to 56.4 in January from a revised reading of 57.3 in December.

Still, the index remained in positive territory, representing the 20th consecutive month of U.S. manufacturing growth. Readings above 50 indicate the sector is expanding. The ISM index is closely watched by economists and financial markets because it provides an early look at economic activity in the preceding period and has a track record of corresponding to changes in the health of the nation's industrial core.

Norbert Ore, chairman of the institute's survey committee, said the January index reflects continued strength in manufacturing, following robust growth in 2004. Among the month's positive readings are relatively strong production growth, less concern about price inflation and a strong willingness by manufacturers to add jobs, Mr. Ore said.

"January sets the tone for a strong first quarter," he said. "Demand for exports continues to be quite strong, with a number of industries reporting significant growth."

The ISM's new-order index fell to 56.5 in January from 62.6 in December, but the production index rose to 57.8 last month from 56.7.

The employment index jumped to 58.1 in January from 53.3 the month before. "The tally ranks as among the best, raising the prospect of a solid gain in the manufacturing component of payroll employment," said Michael Moran, an economist at Daiwa Securities America Inc.

Of the 20 industries tracked by the ISM, 12 reported growth in January, including primary metals, furniture and rubber and plastics.

Separately, the U.S. Commerce Department said total construction spending rose 1.1% in December to a seasonally adjusted rate of $1.032 trillion. November spending was revised upward to a 0.3% gain from the 0.4% decline originally reported.

Private residential spending rebounded 0.7% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $567.6 billion, after a revised flat performance in November.

Public construction spending increased 1.8% to $242.8 billion after a 2.3% advance the prior month. Federal government construction outlays rose 6.6%.

International News
U.S. Manufacturing Expanded At Slower Pace During January
Associated Press
426 words
2 February 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

NEW YORK -- The U.S. manufacturing sector grew for the 20th consecutive month in January, though at a moderated pace, a private research group reported, affirming the rebound in activity at the nation's factories.

The Institute for Supply Management said its index measuring manufacturing activity declined to 56.4 in January, from a revised reading of 57.3 in December. The January figure was slightly below the reading of 57 expected by analysts. A reading of 50 or above in the index means the manufacturing sector is expanding, while a figure below 50 represents a contraction.

The institute's manufacturing data offer the first comprehensive look at U.S. factory activity for the prior month.

Norbert J. Ore, chairman of the institute's survey committee, said the January index reflects strength in manufacturing, with production and employment picking up. "January sets the tone for a strong first quarter. Even though the [index] is slightly lower, the month-over-month growth is still quite strong, and will provide significant momentum for the remainder" of the quarter, Mr. Ore said. "Demand for exports continues to be quite strong, with a number of industries reporting significant growth."

In other economic news, the U.S. Commerce Department reported that construction spending in the U.S. rose 1.1% in December, making last year the best for building activity since 1996. The December increase was the sector's best showing in eight months.

For the year, building activity increased 9% to $998.4 billion, the largest increase since a 10.4% rise in 1996.

The ISM, based in Tempe, Arizona, said its index measuring production increased to 57.8 in January from 56.7. A separate index gauging employment rose to 58.1 from 53.3 in December.

The group's measure of new orders declined to 56.5 in January from 62.6 the previous month. In addition, ISM's index of prices paid by manufacturers declined to 69 from 72 in December, showing some easing in the pace of increases in prices for the materials they use.

Overall, of the 20 manufacturing sectors surveyed by the group, 12 reported growth. They include primary metals, furniture, rubber and plastic products, transportation and equipment, industrial and commercial equipment and computers, instruments and photographic equipment, miscellaneous, textiles, chemicals, electronic components and equipment, apparel and food.

The Economy
Personal Income Climbs Steeply --- Microsoft Payout Credited With Contributing to Rise; Holiday Sales Saw Boost
By Kemba J. Dunham
658 words
1 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Americans' personal income shot up in December, boosted by a large dividend payment to shareholders of computer-software titan Microsoft Corp. that arrived just in time to stoke holiday spending.

The U.S. Commerce Department said the $32 billion dividend payment caused total personal income to rise 3.7% in December from the previous month -- the largest monthly gain since the government began tracking the data in 1959. Excluding the dividend, personal income rose 0.6% in December, up from a revised 0.4% gain in November.

Even without the dividend, economists said, income growth was strong in December, pushing up retail sales to a hefty 8.7% month-to-month rise -- the strongest holiday sales gains since 1999.

"The consumer ended the year spending like crazy, and with the help of Microsoft, they had the money to spend," Joel Naroff of Naroff Economic Advisors Inc. said in an e-mail to clients.

The Commerce report found personal-consumption expenditures rose 0.8% in December, twice the previous month's pace. Incentive-driven gains in motor-vehicle sales fueled the December rise.

Economists said it's rare for one company's dividend payment to have such a huge impact on national income figures. Michael Moran, an economist at Daiwa Securities America Inc. in New York, says there are several instances of large dividend payments having an impact on personal income in the 1970s, "but the magnitude was nowhere close to what we saw in December," he adds.

The Microsoft payment, which affected 4.6 million shareholders, was nearly as large as the $38 billion federal income-tax rebate paid in the summer of 2001. But unlike the rebate, which many Americans spent, economists said the dividend will have a smaller impact on spending because much of it will be reinvested in stocks or left in brokerage accounts.

Separately, the Commerce Department's Bureau of Economic Analysis said that due to a miscalculation by Canada's statistics agency, over $1 billion of Canadian imports from the U.S. were undercounted in November. That means the recently reported estimate of the U.S. exports and of U.S. gross domestic product may have been understated. The government agency said the correction will have an impact on calculations of the November trade balance and fourth-quarter GDP, but it didn't provide details on the nature or size of the change. On Friday, the BEA said real GDP grew at annual rate of 3.1% in the fourth quarter, weaker than many economists expected.

For the year, personal income rose 5.4% in 2004, up from 3.2% a year earlier. Consumer spending rose 6.1% for the year, up from 5.2% a year earlier. Wages and salaries grew 0.4% in December, up from 0.2% a month earlier.

The inflation news in the report was also encouraging. A price index for personal-consumption expenditures excluding food and energy was flat during December, compared with the prior month. The rate climbed 0.2% in November.

Separately, the Census Bureau said newly constructed single-family homes sold at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.098 million units in December. That was up 0.1% from November and down 2% when compared with December 2003.

The average price of a new home continued to rise. In December, the average price was $276,600 and the median price was $222,000. The median is the midway price, half of all homes sell for more than the median and half sell for less.

The personal income of Americans shot up during December in its biggest increase ever, elevated by the payment of a special dividend to shareholders by Microsoft Corp.

Personal income increased 3.7%, after rising a revised 0.4% in November, the U.S. Commerce Department said yesterday. Income was originally estimated to rise 0.3%. Excluding the Microsoft dividend, personal income rose 0.6%, which still "suggests that income growth was strong in December," said John Ryding, chief U.S. economist for the investment bank Bear Stearns Cos.

The huge increase was the largest gain since monthly records began in 1959. The report showed personal dividend income was boosted $298.2 billion at an annual rate -- or $24.8 billion at a monthly rate -- by the special dividend. Dividend income climbed 301.7% at a seasonally adjusted annual rate in December, after rising 3.4% in November.

For the year, personal income -- also skewed by the Microsoft dividend -- rose 5.4%, up from 3.2% in 2003.

Personal consumption rose 0.8%, after climbing a revised 0.4%. Spending was initially seen going up 0.2% in November. The December gain was driven by an incentive-induced jump in motor-vehicle sales. Consumer spending rose 6.1% for the year, up from 5.2% the previous year.

Wages and salaries grew 0.4% in December, up from 0.2% a month earlier. The increase reflected the modest rise in jobs, a longer workweek for the holidays and soft earnings growth during the month, some economists say. For the year, wages and salaries grew 5.2%, up from 3.6% the previous year.

The inflation news in the report was also encouraging. A price index for personal-consumption expenditures excluding food and energy was flat during December, compared with the prior month.

The rate climbed 0.2% in November.

Year-to-year, personal-consumption expenditures minus food and energy rose 1.5% in December, compared with December 2003.

The year-to-year rise in November was 1.6%.

Separately, the Commerce Department also said sales of new single-family homes increased 0.1% in December to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.098 million.

November home sales fell 13.1% as the government lowered the annual rate to 1.097 million from an earlier estimate of 1.125 million.

Economists predicted a bigger increase for the month. While wet weather damped sales in November, "the absence of a December rebound is puzzling," said Steve Wood, chief economist at Insight Economics in Danville, California.

For the year, new-home sales reached an unadjusted 1.183 million annual rate, 8.9% higher than 2003's 1.086 million.

By Kemba J. Dunham Dow Jones Newswires

U.S. consumer confidence rose in January for the second month in a row, but there remain concerns about economic conditions in the months ahead, the Conference Board said.

The private research group said its consumer-confidence index rose 0.7% to 103.4 from an upwardly revised 102.7 in December. The gain came as a surprise; the median finding of economists in a survey conducted by was for a reading of 101.5, a decline from the previous month. The Conference Board report is based on a mail-in survey of 5,000 U.S. households.

Despite recent improvements, "confidence seems fragile, driven by shifting opinions about the labor market and gasoline prices," said Steve Wood, chief economist at Insight Economics in Danville, California. "For there to be a sustained rise in confidence, job creation will have to accelerate."

Meanwhile, U.S. sales of previously occupied homes in December fell 3.3% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of nearly 6.7 million units, the National Association of Realtors said. The revised November level of 6.9 million equaled the record set in June 2004.

"Our sense was that November sales were the peak for the current housing cycle, but activity remains strong," said David Lereah, the NAR's chief economist.

The inventory of homes on the market declined 12% in December to nearly 2.2 million, partly reflecting the normal seasonal slowdown of listings. At the current rate of sales, the NAR said, that supply is enough to last 3.9 months, the lowest since the association began tracking the number in 1982.

For the full year, the NAR reported a record 6.7 million home sales, up 9% from a year earlier.

The Economy
U.S. Consumers Remain Upbeat About Economy
By Kemba J. Dunham Dow Jones Newswires
643 words
26 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A measure of U.S. consumer confidence rose in January for the second month in a row, despite lingering worries about whether the economy will lose steam in the months ahead.

The Conference Board, a private research group, said its consumer-confidence index rose 0.7% to 103.4 from an upwardly revised 102.7 in December. The median finding of economists in a survey conducted by was for a reading of 101.5. The Conference Board report is based on a mail-in survey of 5,000 U.S. households.

"It's a good sign in terms of consumer spending," said Lynn Franco, director of the board's consumer-research center. "Consumers remain optimistic that the economy is going to continue to grow in the first half of 2005."

The board said its present-situation index, a gauge of consumers' assessment of current economic activity, rose to 110.9 from 105.7 in the previous month. The index is at its highest level since May 2002. The employment picture was also favorable, with the percentage of consumers saying jobs are plentiful increasing to 20.7% from 19.4%. Those claiming jobs are hard to get fell to 24.7% to 26.4%.

Consumers are feeling slightly cautious about the next six months, however. The expectation index, an assessment of the state of economic activity during the next six months, dropped to 98.4 from 100.7. Consumers believed the outlook for jobs, business activity and income growth had deteriorated.

Some economists says consumers may be hedging because they already have loaded up on credit-card debt, realize that interest rates are rising and know that the job market isn't as strong as it was in the late 1990s.

"We can count on the consumer to keep the economy growing this year, but we can't count on the consumer to accelerate that growth," said Anthony Chan, an economist at JP Morgan Fleming Asset Management, a unit of J.P. Morgan Chase &Co.

Meanwhile, U.S. sales of previously occupied homes in December fell 3.3% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of nearly 6.7 million units, the National Association of Realtors said. The revised November level of 6.9 million equaled the record set in June 2004.

"Our sense was that November sales were the peak for the current housing cycle, but activity remains strong," said David Lereah, the NAR's chief economist. For the full year, the NAR reported a record 6.7 million home sales, up 9.4% from a year earlier. The NAR's Mr. Lereah predicted that sales will slow to about 6.5 million this year.

The national median price for previously owned homes was $188,900 in December, up 8.1% from a year earlier, the NAR said.

The inventory of previously occupied homes on the market declined 12% in December to about 2.2 million. At the current rate of sales, the NAR said, that supply is enough to last only 3.9 months, the lowest since the association began tracking the number in 1982.

At the end of last year's third quarter, vacant homes available for sale stood at 1.08% of the nation's total housing stock, up from a range of about 0.8% to 1% in most of the 1990s. If the pace of sales slows as interest rates rise, the backlog of unsold homes could become a drag on the market, said Rick T. Murray, an analyst at Raymond James &Associates Inc., a securities firm in St. Petersburg, Fla.

Personal Finance
Home Construction Continues at Robust Pace --- Builders Say the Market Will Remain Solid in 2005 Despite Chorus of Warnings
By James R. Hagerty
702 words
20 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. housing starts jumped 11% in December from the prior month, underscoring builders' confidence that the market will remain solid in 2005 despite a growing chorus of warnings that prices have risen too fast in some parts of the country.

"Builders seem quite upbeat," said David Seiders, chief economist for the National Association of Home Builders, adding that the latest data show there has been no "fundamental weakening" in the market.

The Commerce Department estimated that builders began work on privately owned homes at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of two million units in December, up 11% from 1.81 million in November but down 3% from a year earlier. In November, starts plunged 12% from a month earlier, but Mr. Seiders said that was partly due to heavy rain in much of the country. He described the December performance as a "snapback."

Builders are poised to keep firing their nail guns at a rapid clip: Building permits for privately owned homes were recorded at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 2.02 million in December, down 0.3% from November but up 2.1% from a year before.

For all of 2004, builders broke ground on an estimated 1.95 million housing units, up 5.7% from 2003, the department said. Last year's total was the highest since 1978. For 2005, Mr. Seiders forecast a decline of about 3.6% in starts, to 1.88 million. That would still leave them at the second highest level since 1978.

Mr. Seiders and other housing-industry economists who took part in a news briefing yesterday said they expect modest increases in interest rates this year. The rate on 30-year fixed-rate mortgages is likely to rise to 6.75% by year end from about 5.75% now, Mr. Seiders said. David Berson, chief economist at Fannie Mae, forecast a rise to just 6.3% -- far below the averages of 8.2% in the 1990s and 12.7% in the 1980s.

While higher rates will make monthly payments steeper, the economists said they expect fairly strong growth in jobs and wages to offset the rising cost of borrowing.

Sales of new and previously occupied homes are likely to decline 7% to 8% this year from last year's record 7.75 million, Mr. Berson said. He also forecast that the national average house price will rise 3% to 4% in 2005, down from an estimated 10% to 11% in 2004. In some areas, he said, prices could decline.

The forecasts for a cooling market are remarkably similar to those of a year ago. Those forecasts proved wrong when interest rates stayed surprisingly low and helped keep home sales brisk.

One reason to be more cautious about home prices this year, Mr. Berson said, is that more investors have jumped into the market. LoanPerformance Inc., a San Francisco-based research firm, says 8.4% of purchase-mortgages in the first 10 months of 2004 went to people who didn't plan to occupy the homes, mostly investors. That was up from 7.6% for all of 2003 and 5.8% in 2000. Mr. Berson expects fewer investor purchases in 2005 and says some investors may seek to cash out of the market.

Another reason for caution is that the supply of homes has been growing. The number of new single-family homes for sale in November was 424,000, up 16% from a year earlier, according to the Commerce Department. Mr. Seiders said he has been warning builders about the risk of overbuilding.

Some economists outside the industry are less confident that the market will remain firm. In a research note earlier this month, Andrew Tilton, an economist at Goldman, Sachs &Co. in New York, said the expected rise in interest rates this year "would have a chilling effect on home prices. This would likely take the form of an extended period of flattish prices in many regions, with outright declines more likely in the hottest coastal markets."

The Economy
CPI Rose at Fast Clip, Erasing Many Pay Gains
By Kemba J. Dunham
674 words
20 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Consumer-price inflation during 2004 rose at the fastest pace in four years, essentially wiping out wage gains for many American workers.

The Bureau of Labor Statistics reported yesterday that its consumer-price index -- the most widely followed barometer of inflation -- rose 3.3% last year, the largest gain since 2000. The core rate of inflation, which excludes the volatile food and energy categories, rose 2.2% last year. While those rates of inflation remain mild by historical standards, they are up sharply from 2003 when the overall CPI rose 1.9% and the core rate was up 1.1%. For the month of December, inflation eased as the CPI declined 0.1% compared with the previous month.

The year-over-year numbers "were not wildly surprising given the way things ended in 2003, when the rate of inflation was unsustainably low," says Joshua Shapiro, an economist with MFR Inc., a New York-based economic-advisory firm.

Even though inflation remains low, it was high enough last year to offset the gains in worker wages. According to a separate Labor Department report, when adjusted for inflation, hourly earnings for private nonfarm workers declined 0.7% last month from a year earlier. Average weekly earnings in December were $537.08, down 0.1% from a year earlier when adjusted for inflation.

"We're three years into a recovery with strong productivity growth, yet many in the working class are falling behind, which suggests that inequalities are embedded in the recovery at this stage," said Jared Bernstein, a senior economist at the Economic Policy Institute, a progressive Washington-based think tank.

The slow rise in earnings didn't stop consumers from spending, but economists say this could eventually change. The Federal Reserve, in its latest report of economic conditions across the country, said consumer spending was generally higher in December when compared with November but that inflation remained in check. "Labor markets firmed in a number of districts, but wage pressures generally remained modest," the Fed said in its "beige book" report, adding that most districts "reported steady or only slightly higher overall prices." Economic growth continued in 11 of the 12 Federal Reserve districts.

The CPI report showed that consumer prices rose sharply during 2004 for a number of products. Energy prices, for example, rose 16.6%, led largely by oil prices, which were up 26.7%. Transportation prices were up 6.5%, partly due to rising costs for new and used cars. Prices for medical costs rose 4.2% and hotel costs were up 5.1%. The only major category to show a decline for the year was apparel.

But the December CPI suggests that inflation has eased after surging for much of last year. The small decline last month was the first dip in five months, which mostly reflected dropping energy prices and stable food prices. The core index rose 0.2% for a third month in a row. December declines were broad-based.

Separately, the Labor Department said the number of U.S. workers filing first-time applications for unemployment benefits for the week ended Saturday decreased 48,000 to 319,000 from the previous week's unrevised figure of 367,000. The four-week moving average was 341,000, a decrease of 3,000 from the previous week's unrevised average of 344,000.

In another report, the Commerce Department said December housing starts increased 10.9% to a seasonally adjusted 2.004 million annual rate. For the entire year, an estimated 1.953 million housing units were started, 5.7% above 2003's 1.847 million. Building permits decreased 0.3% in December to a 2.021 million annual rate. For 2004, an estimated 2.018 million housing units were authorized by building permits, a 6.8% increase from the previous year.

International News
U.S. Consumer Prices Declined in December --- First Drop in Five Months Signals Inflation Is Easing, Reflects Drop in Cost of Oil
By Joseph Rebello and Paulette Chu Dow Jones Newswires
520 words
20 January 2005
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- U.S. consumer prices fell for the first time in five months in December as oil prices dropped, suggesting that inflation has eased after surging for much of 2004.

The consumer-price index fell 0.05% last month, the Labor Department said. The decline mostly reflected falling energy prices and stable food prices. The core index, which excludes those items, rose by 0.15% for the third month in a row.

Wall Street had expected overall consumer prices to be stable in December, although forecasters accurately predicted the 0.2% increase in the core index. The numbers were consistent with the Federal Reserve's view that inflation is "well contained."

Over the course of 2004, consumer prices rose 3.3%, the fastest pace in four years, the Labor Department said. Fed policy makers have said much of that increase reflected surging oil prices in the first 10 months of the year. But core prices also showed a big gain in annual terms -- a 2.2% increase that was the largest since 2001.

Fed policy makers recently have debated whether inflation is likely to heat up because of the dollar's decline against other major currencies. But most Fed officials say they don't think a further surge in inflation is likely.

"I expect inflation will remain subdued in 2005," Gary Stern, a member of the policy-making Federal Open Market Committee said Tuesday. But Fed officials also say they intend to keep raising interest rates gradually to make sure inflation does stay tame.

Fed policy makers meet Feb. 1 and Feb. 2 to decide the course of interest rates. Wall Street widely expects them to raise the key federal funds rate a quarter percentage point to 2.5%. Many economists expect at least four more increases of that magnitude during 2005.

In its report, the Labor Department said energy prices fell 1.8% in December, marking the biggest decline since July. Gasoline prices fell 3.7% in the biggest decline since October 2003. Prices of natural gas, however, rose 1%. Food prices were steady for the first time in three months.

Separately, the Labor Department said the number of U.S. workers filing first-time applications for unemployment benefits for the week ended Jan. 15 decreased 48,000 to 319,000 from the previous week's unrevised figure of 367,000. The four-week moving average was 341,000 -- a decrease of 3,000 from the previous week's unrevised average of 344,000.

In another report, the Commerce Department said December housing starts increased 10.9% to a seasonally adjusted two million annual rate. For the entire year, an estimated 1.953 million housing units were started, 5.7% above 2003's 1.847 million; the annual figures are not seasonally adjusted.

Family Finance:
Personal Finance
Family Finance: The Nation's Least-Affordable Housing Markets
Rising Home Prices Outpace Incomes in Many Areas; Interest-Only Loans Surge ---- By Ruth Simon
1494 words
12 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Rising home prices during the past year have made it substantially harder for buyers to get their foot in the door in half of the nation's 30 largest metro areas.

That is the conclusion of a study that ranked 325 metropolitan areas by their affordability. The study, by forecasting and consulting firm Economy.com, found that in nearly 30 metro areas nationwide, prices are so high that someone earning the median income can't afford a median-price home, based on traditional lending standards. That is a remarkable finding given that mortgage rates remain near historical lows.

Other measures also suggest affordability is dropping. Just more than half of the homes sold in the U.S. during the third quarter were affordable to families earning the median income, according to the National Association of Home Builders/Wells Fargo Housing Opportunity Index, which looks at sales of both new and existing homes. That's down from about 61% for the first quarter of 2004.

Affordability -- a measure of the ability of the average household to buy a home at current mortgage rates -- has long been an issue for low-income buyers. But in some markets, such as Salinas, Calif., New York City and Honolulu, prices have increased so rapidly that buyers with healthier incomes are feeling the squeeze as well. And the number of such markets could climb if mortgage rates rise this year, as many economists expect.

The decline in affordability helps explain the rise of lending programs -- such as interest-only mortgages and 100% financing -- that help buyers get into a home they couldn't otherwise afford. In some of the least-affordable markets, including Salinas and Santa Cruz, Calif., interest-only mortgages accounted for more than 60% of mortgages taken out to buy homes last year, according to an analysis done for The Wall Street Journal by LoanPerformance in San Francisco, which tracks 46 million mortgages monthly. Nationwide, interest-only loans accounted for about one-third of home-purchase mortgages in 2004.

If interest rates rise, some buyers who stretched their purchasing power with adjustable-rate loans and interest-only mortgages could face financial problems.

In many parts of the country, affordability isn't much of a problem. The National Association of Realtors' Affordability Index stood at 131.7 in November, a slight decrease from the previous month. (When the index is at 100, it means that the median income family has the exact amount needed to purchase the median-price existing home, with 20% down and a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage.)

"By and large, when you look at the affordability index it is still very healthy," says David Lereah, chief economist for the National Association of Realtors. In many areas where affordability is an issue, the supply of homes for sale isn't large enough to meet demand, he adds.

Rising interest rates could make affordability an issue in more areas. Sixteen metro areas, including Bergen County, N.J., Providence, R.I., Boulder, Colo., and Seattle, would become unaffordable for someone earning the median income if rates on 30-year fixed rate mortgages rise soon to 7%, according to Economy.com. If mortgage rates jump to 8%, the list would expand to 18 more metro areas, including Portland, Maine, Baltimore, and Charleston, S.C.

For now, the pain is greatest for entry-level buyers. In the Miami area, "the first-time home for many people will be a condominium," says Ron Shuffield, president of Esslinger-Wooten-Maxwell Realtors.

As home prices rise, many first-time buyers are finding they don't have enough cash for a down payment. Some turn to relatives for help. Many others are opting for 100% financing. In Jersey City, N.J., roughly three-quarters of first-time buyers put down 5% or less of the purchase price, says Laura Skolar, manager of Century 21 Plaza Realty Corp. "If we get someone coming in with 10% or 20% down, we feel like we've hit pay dirt," she says.

Jean Manner Schwimmer, of Coldwell Banker Gay Dales in Salinas, Calif., says that more than 90% of her first-time home buyers put no money down. In Salinas, a household earning the median income can afford to buy just 49% of the median-price home, down from 61% during the first quarter of 2003, according to Economy.com.

Len Filippoff opted for 100% financing when he purchased a $509,000 three-bedroom home in Salinas last summer. "When prices started going up, we started to panic," says Mr. Filippoff, a manager for Costco Wholesale. "We thought we were going to be priced out of the market. We had some money saved, but we didn't have the 10% most lenders were looking for." To help keep his monthly payments in check, Mr. Filippoff also chose an interest-only mortgage.

In some high-cost markets, buyers trading up are also feeling the pinch. "We're seeing an awful lot of well-qualified move-up buyers who are stretching," says John Karevoll, a housing analyst for DataQuick Information Systems in La Jolla, Calif. "Instead of putting 20% down, they are putting 10% down. Instead of a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage, they're taking a seven-year adjustable."

There are other creative solutions. Robert Hale, a fixed-income portfolio analyst in Boston, figured he would be able to afford only a 1,200-square-foot condo on his $350,000 budget. So Mr. Hale and his wife teamed up with a friend and purchased a 3,500-square-foot, two-family house in Brighton, Mass., for $685,000 and are splitting it into two condominiums. Such conversions are becoming more popular in the Boston area, says Mr. Hale's broker, Jeffrey Heighton of Coldwell Banker Residential Brokerage in Brookline.

In Las Vegas, declining affordability has spurred a wave of condominium conversions as developers seek to appeal to entry-level buyers who have been priced out of the market. At least 8,000 apartment units will be converted into condos this year, up from roughly 3,000 last year, says Dennis Smith, president of Home Builders Research Inc., which tracks the Las Vegas housing market. "Until 2004, apartment conversions to condos were . . . basically nonexistent," he adds.

Building Value:
The Property Report
Building Value: West Coast Leads Apartment-Markets Rankings
By Ray A. Smith
628 words
12 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B12
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

THE WEST COAST, and California in particular, dominate the rankings of the apartment markets with the best growth prospects for this year, according to a new report that ranks 42 markets throughout the country.

Four of the top five markets are in Southern California. A total of six Western markets are in the top 10. Riverside-San Bernardino topped the list, moving up three spots from its 2004 ranking, followed by San Diego, Orange County (which dropped from No. 1), Las Vegas and Los Angeles. Oakland took the 10th spot.

Washington, D.C.; Boston; Fort Lauderdale, Fla.; and New York City ranked sixth, seventh, eighth and ninth, respectively. Markets in the South and Midwest mostly ranked toward the bottom of the list, largely because they continued to struggle with supply issues, a weak labor market or both.

These findings are included in Marcus &Millichap Real Estate Investment Brokerage Co.'s 2005 National Apartment Index. The Encino, Calif., brokerage firm's research division ranks markets based on a series of 12-month forward-looking supply and demand indicators. Markets are ranked based on their cumulative weighted-average scores for a number of things, including forecast employment growth, vacancy, construction, housing affordability and rent growth.

Southern California's showing in the ranking isn't surprising, as apartment markets there held up well during the economic downturn. The region had high home prices, which tend to lead to strong renting activity, as many people in those areas find buying out of reach. The markets also have strong demographic trends and constraints on new construction, according to the index. Washington, D.C., also has shared many of these traits in recent years.

But other markets had more significant moves. New York City, for example, rose to ninth place from 12th largely because of its exorbitant housing prices, which the index says should elevate rental demand and allow for strong rental growth. Seattle made the biggest positive move, rising eight places to 18.

Tim Campbell, a broker with Pinnacle Management AMS LLC in Seattle, says that city's rental market is poised for a recovery, as hiring is picking up in the burgeoning biotech industry and as Microsoft Corp. and Boeing Co., which have long had presences there, have started hiring again.

Miami, however, slipped 10 spots, the biggest drop, in part because of competition from affordable condos, which pushes people to buy rather than rent. Lyle Chariff, president of brokerage firm Chariff Realty Group in Miami, says investors should expect to lose money on apartment properties for the next year or two, with the hope they will make it back as the housing market slows. "They'll be taking a loss on the properties now but they'll be gaining appreciation later," he says.
