Students at Glendover Elementary school[http://www.wkyt.com/Global/story.asp?S=2655051target=_blank] in Lexington, Ky., are putting together boxes with schools supplies for their Iraqi peers. And students from Franklin Middle School[http://www.kpvi.com/index.cfm?page=nbcheadlines.cfm&ID=22666target=_blank] in Pocatello, Idaho, are participating in Operation Iraqi Children, raising finds to buy school kits for Iraqi students.

Students from the University of Maryland Baltimore County[http://trw.umbc.edu/article.php?page=ne03target=_blank] are also doing their bit:

Students in the combined Lutheran and Episcopal Ministry at UMBC have joined together with students from the Johns Hopkins University Interfaith Center in collecting and donating school supplies to elementary school children in war-ravaged Iraq. Crayons, colored and regular pencils, paper, markers, notebooks, glue sticks and binders from UMBC were delivered to the Hopkins campus on September 27. The project was originated by Hopkins alum Alexander Kuhns, who graduated in 1997 and who is currently serving in Mosul as an Arabic interpreter for the U.S. Army.

First Lt. Timothy Jones has inspired Delaware County, Pa.'s Brownie Troop No. 9[http://www.zwire.com/site/news.cfm?newsid=13561243&BRD=1725&PAG=461&dept_id=45529&rfi=6target=_blank], of which his daughter Raven is a member, to also help Iraqi children:

Spurred by UNICEF figures showing the toll war is taking on Iraqi children, the Brownies are collecting clothing, blankets and soccer balls to mail to the children. . . .

"They have already collected between 200 and 300 boxes and it continues to go on. I just thought maybe it's going to be 9, 10, 11 boxes something like that. I would never have imagined hundreds of boxes. It's great though. That's more children that's going to be able to receive something, some clothing or maybe a soccer ball," [Jones] said.

Humanitarian aid also continues to reach Iraq from its neighbors in the region. A fifth Red Crescent Society[http://news.xinhuanet.com/english/2004-12/06/content_2298309.htmtarget=_blank] relief ship from the United Arab Emirates has reached the Iraqi port of Umm Qasr with 400 tons of relief aid, including 20 containers of medical supplies.

Coalition troops. The U.S. and its allies contributing in a significant way to the reconstruction effort[http://www.defenselink.mil/releases/2004/nr20041203-1772.htmltarget=_blank], alongside numerous government and private initiatives currently in place:

The Iraq Project and Contracting Office (PCO) with the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers announced . . . that the U.S. government has surpassed the 1,000 construction start mark one month ahead of their year-end goal. The reconstruction team has delivered 1,051 construction starts to-date. "We're thrilled to have achieved this goal in spite of insurgent activity," stated PCO Director Charles Hess. "At the same time, while it is an important mark, it's just a mark on the wall. There are going to be many more. As of today, we have 1,051 projects that have actually started construction, or 'turned dirt.' Our new goal is 1,200 by the end of the year."

The 1,050 projects include schools (363), public health clinics (41), hospitals (14), railroad stations (58), border posts (88), port of entry (6), fire stations (20), police stations (17), military bases (16), water (67), electricity (58), oil (19), sewer (24), roads (66) and various others (194).

The U.S. Army Corps of Engineers[http://www.defenselink.mil/news/Dec2004/n12032004_2004120303.htmltarget=_blank] is specifically involved in a substantial number of security-related reconstruction projects:

Iraq's border-post project is one of the first steps to protecting the country from the influx of insurgents. Construction continues on the borders between Iraq and Iran, Kuwait and Saudi Arabia. More than 90 border posts are either under construction or complete. . . .

Refurbishing police stations also remains a big focus of the reconstruction. . . . At least 27 stations and 10 vehicle checkpoints have been identified for refurbishment in a province in southern Iraq. Police cadet training has started on a military base in south central Iraq, and work continues on the many police stations in the southern district. Teams have started to assess the needs in different areas, and a direct funding apparatus has been put in place to enable rapid construction of the facilities. Five fire station sites have been identified, and 13 more are being assessed in both the Maysan and Basrah provinces.

Troops continue to work to provide vital infrastructure throughout Iraq. Soldiers from Task Force First Battalion, 77th Armor recently renovated the Al-Ahabab water treatment plant[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_06/Article_06.htmtarget=_blank] that provides water for 2,000 local people. The plant hasn't been operational for years. In Sheik Jamil village, near Balad, soldiers from the 13th Corps Support Command Special Troops Battalion have open a new water filtration plant[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041211.txttarget=_blank] to serve 500 residents. In Gazaliya[http://www.portaliraq.com/shownews.php?id=679], a city of some 250,000 residents, the First Cavalry Division's 91st Engineer Battalion has so far spent $1 million on fixing the town's run-down sewer system. The effort also helps to stimulate the local economy: "One of the stipulations in all our projects is that 90% of all unskilled labor must be hired from the local area because we try to give jobs to as many people as we can," says Lt. Col. Chris Martin, the battalion's commander.

Meanwhile, a valuable project involving paving a major section of Iraqi transport network[http://www4.army.mil/news/article.php?story=6648target=_blank] has been recently completed:

Iraq's first national highway running from its northern borders to the Persian Gulf in the south was completed Dec. 5 with the driving of a golden spike.

Main Supply Route Tampa, also called Highway or Expressway One, was completed by a combined effort of coalition and Iraqi forces led by the 115th Engineer Group of the Utah National Guard.

The golden spike ceremony was patterned after one in Utah 135 years ago when the world's first transcontinental railroad was joined in Promontory Point. The ceremonial driving of a golden spike completed the final link of the railroad on May 10, 1869, joining the U.S. Pacific and Atlantic coasts.

The golden spike ceremony for the Iraqi highway was performed by representatives of both Iraq and the coalition. Maj. Gen. Walter Natynczyk, deputy commander of the Multi-National Corps-Iraq, hammered the railroad spike into the center of the pavement with a representative of Iraq's Ministry of Housing and Construction.

The report explains by way of background:

Prior to the paving project, 143 kilometers of the 1,020-kilometer road were unpaved. The U.S. Army adopted the project in cooperation with Iraq in November 2003. Now, at least one lane of asphalt covers the center of Iraq from the northern borders of Turkey and Syria to the southern border of the Persian Gulf. The road construction had never been completed due to difficulties such as the war with Iran, the Gulf War, and issues Saddam had with people of the area, said the Iraqi ministry representative. . . .

Since there was no working asphalt plants to produce the materials needed for the road, Iraqis and Coalition Forces shipped bitumen, the naturally occurring tar used successfully for more than 6,000 years in the fertile crescent, and processed it and worked together to pave the road.

A considerable amount of humanitarian work in Iraq is done through Civil Affairs Battalions attached the U.S. forces. Here, for example, you can get a brief snapshot of all the good work that the 411th Civil Affairs Battalion[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_08/Article_08.htmtarget=_blank], an Army Reserve unit based in Danbury, Conn., is doing in support of the First Infantry Division in Diyala province.

Coalition troops are continuing to support the Iraqi health system, from providing valuable supplies to training for medical personnel. In a typical action[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_22/Article_22.htmtarget=_blank], "874th Forward Surgical Team (FST) of the 225th Forward Support Battalion, 2nd BCT received 13 Air Force 463L pallets of humanitarian supplies for local hospitals in the Kirkuk Province. The donated equipment came from a civilian hospital in Columbia, SC."

The Public Health Team of the 411th Civil Affairs Battalion has been distributing supplies, including textbooks and medical equipment at the Kirkuk Teaching Hospital[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_19/Article_19.htmtarget=_blank]. Soldiers from Task Force 1-21 Infantry recently delivered medical equipment to the Kirkuk General Hospital[http://www.noticias.info/asp/aspComunicados.asp?nid=42148&src=0target=_blank].

Soldiers of the Task Force Tacoma, meanwhile, have been helping a doctor in Balad[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_16/Article_16.htmtarget=_blank]:

"We delivered the medical supplies to a local doctor who's not affiliated with the Government," said Capt. John Ramirez, TF Tacoma, 81st Brigade Physician Assistant. "He cares for a lot of the local people in the community right out of his house." Ramirez said donating to this doctor was particularly important because he takes care of many Iraqis who can't afford to go to a local government hospital either because of distance or lack of money.

In Baquba[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_36/Article_36.htmtarget=_blank], thanks to the teamwork between the local community and the 411th Civil Affairs Battalion publics works team, the local health clinic has been thoroughly renovated and is now operational.

The troops also continued to support Iraqi schools and schoolchildren. Near Baqubah[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_10/Article_10.htmtarget=_blank], soldiers of the C Company, the 141st Engineer Combat Battalion, First Infantry Division distributed 200 backpacks filled with school supplies donated by family and friends back in the U.S. Soldiers from the 601st Aviation Support Battalion visited the Al Salam School and the Hemrin School near Tikrit[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_43/Article_43.htmtarget=_blank] with school supplies and other gifts for students.

In villages outside Dibbis[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_33/Article_33.htmtarget=_blank], soldiers from Battery C, Task Force 2-11 Field Artillery, along with Iraqi police and airmen, distributed 165 donated school supply bags and refurbished desks at the Qaradara and Baihassan elementary schools: "The school supplies were part of an ongoing Operation Crayon mission that 2nd Brigade Combat Team has been carrying out since September." The Al Shebebea School in Owja[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_31/Article_31.htmtarget=_blank] has been renovated, thanks to the efforts of Alpha Company, Task Force 1-18 Infantry. And Hazaban Primary School in Suleymaniyah[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_28/Article_28.htmtarget=_blank] has received new kerosene heaters from the soldiers of the civil affairs unit of Task Force Danger.

Soldiers are also engaging in humanitarian work to bring immediately relief to people's lives. In the Shia section of Baghdad[http://www4.army.mil/ocpa/read.php?story_id_key=6633target=_blank]:

Every day since the Mahdi Militia turned in their weapons in October, there have been dramatic decreases of hostile activity in Sadr City allowing the Soldiers of Task Force Lancer to assist with re-building the neighborhood. Day in and day out, Task Force Lancer has been conducting missions such as: giving out sheep, frozen chickens and humanitarian-aid bags; protecting kerosene and propane stations and escorting sewage trucks to remove pools of sewage from the streets.

Says Staff Sgt. Chad Sandoe, 443rd Civil Affairs Battalion: "[Food items] are very well received. . . . We have no problems with giving them out, because we get large crowds when we arrive. Soldiers also get into it by passing out candy, toys and school supplies they receive from donations back home to the kids. The big thing about it is that since hostilities have subsided, we are trying to give something that makes an immediate impact on the people. The major projects we are undertaking are not as visible and take time."

Also in Sadr City[http://www.defenselink.mil/news/Dec2004/n12082004_2004120802.htmltarget=_blank], soldiers from the First Cavalry Division's First Brigade Combat Team "teamed up with local children and cleaned a trash-covered area in the middle of an eastern Baghdad neighborhood, converting the land into a park. Infrequent visits from the city's trash trucks and no trash bins for people to use had resulted in what was turning into a trash dump, threatening the health of those living nearby":

Seeing U.S. vehicles entering the area, local children ran to meet the soldiers and ask for candy, which soldiers often bring for them. This time, however, the soldiers distributed tools that the children could use to help in the cleanup. "Kids always approach us and want candy, but this time we had something for them to do. We wanted to encourage the local children to help themselves by using the rakes and shovels we provided to help clean up," [First Lt. Gerald] Kubicek said. "Since there were a lot of kids out there, we gave school supplies as a token of our appreciation after the work was done."

Kubicek said getting the neighborhood energized was essential to the success of the project. "We tried to draw the community together by getting different families to help us with this project," he said. Since trash disposal had been the problem that made the field unusable in the first place, a trash pit was constructed so families have a centralized place for refuse.

The following day, a local contractor delivered two swing sets and other playground equipment, giving local kids a place to hang out and play soccer, the Iraqi national pastime. "Now we have a nice, open park, two swing sets, and a place for them to deposit their garbage, instead of having it laying all over the place," Kubicek said. "We have a vested interest in giving something back to the Iraqi people. Projects like this one are a reminder that the American people are here to help."

But it's not all big projects and mass actions, as individual soldiers keep helping individual Iraqis. These are people like Doug Stenberg[http://www.concordmonitor.com/apps/pbcs.dll/article?AID=/20041213/REPOSITORY/412130305/1031target=_blank], a staff sergeant with the 744th Transportation Company, a Hillsboro, N.H., resident who never took no for an answer in his attempts to obtain proper medical attention to 9-year-old Zaharra, who suffered from a cleft palate and a tumor on her upper jaw. Stenberg's persistence finally paid off after his superiors agreed for military doctors to perform free an operation that would cost Zaharra's family $1,000. Stenberg is not alone:

In a similar story last month, soldiers from the 197th Field Artillery Brigade, headquartered in Manchester, helped 14-year-old Lamia Kareem, an Iraqi citizen, get the surgery she needed to have a tumor removed from the side of her liver.

In another, Stephanie Riley, of Penacook, a chief nurse and major with the 157th Air Refueling Wing of the New Hampshire Air National Guard, spent extra time caring for a toddler who Marines found abandoned and injured in a field in Fallujah. Her husband, Shawn, volunteered to adopt the child but said the couple later found out "it just would be a near impossibility" because there is no government structure in Iraq through which they could do it legally. Stephanie Riley helped transport the little girl to Germany for care. "She was the sweetest little thing that just liked to be held, not afraid of anyone," she said in an e-mail to friends and family. "One of the many heartbreaking stories that has come out of the war."

Other coalition partners are continuing their humanitarian and reconstruction work throughout Iraq.

The Japanese Cabinet[http://www.portaliraq.com/shownews.php?id=675target=_blank] has made the decision to continue the activities of the Self Defense Forces (SDF) centering on humanitarian and reconstruction assistance by revising the Basic Plan on the measures based on the Special Measures Law on Humanitarian and Reconstruction Assistance in Iraq. Activities of the SDF such as water supply, medical services, as well as rehabilitation and maintenance of schools and other public facilities, have restored and enhanced the basic infrastructure for the life of local residents and also contributed to their job creation.

The Japanese forces are based in the Al Samawah region.

Diplomacy and security. Turkey and Iraq are increasing cooperation[http://www.turkishpress.com/news.asp?ID=34589target=_blank] to deny a northern Iraq sanctuary to Kurdish rebels from the Kurdistan Workers Party (PKK), a terrorist organization.

While everyone's attention continues to focus on the Sunni Triangle, the rest of Iraq is full of dogs that didn't bark. Kirkuk[http://www.kurdishmedia.com/news.asp?id=5922target=_blank] is one of them:

When the government of Saddam Hussein fell after the U.S.-led invasion in March 2003, Kirkuk was predicted to be ground zero in an Iraqi civil war. Nearly equal parts Arab Muslim, Kurd and Turkmen, with a number of Iraqi Christians as well, the groups were expected to clash viciously for power, prestige and wealth, setting the stage for a battle that could quickly spread nationwide.

But instead, even as violence and insurgency rages all around it, Kirkuk has defied the odds, becoming one of the most peaceful cities in Iraq. Its economy is growing; its police force is considered a model for the rest of the nation; its City Council meetings are lively and attended by the public.

Read how it all happened and the role U.S. troops are playing in the city:

Unlike in most Iraqi cities, where soldiers live on outlying posts and enter city limits predominantly for patrols, several hundred troops in Kirkuk live in "safe houses" in the city center. Neighborhood kids swim in their pool; the soldiers send their local interpreters to pick up food from restaurants.

Bravo Company of the 1st Battalion of the 21st Infantry Regiment is one such example. The soldiers live in a sprawling compound in eastern Kirkuk. In nearly a year of living there, they have not taken a single mortar round or attack from a rocket-propelled grenade.

The soldiers know their quadrant of the city as well as any local. Every day they visit schools, talk with religious leaders, visit clinics to check that medical supplies are coming in.

Throughout the country, Iraq's own new security forces are playing an increasingly prominent role in maintaining order and fighting the insurgents and terrorists. Foreign assistance in training and equipping continues to be essential for the development of these domestic forces. During his recent visit to Iraq, NATO's Secretary-General Jaap de Hoop Scheffer has officially opened the NATO headquarters in Baghdad[http://www.iraq.net/displayarticle6044.htmltarget=_blank]:

Several hundred instructors due in the country will be protected by a NATO force, with the aim of training 1,000 Iraqi officers a year. NATO agreed to send a military training mission to Iraq in principle in June, but struggled for several months to agree the details. It is now rushing to deploy up to 400 instructors in the country ahead of the crucial elections. Lindqvist said NATO at present had 20 instructors in the country and the rest were being trained for the job.

At Forward Operating Base Paliwoda, Capt. Christian Cosner reports on the progress of the training of the 203rd Iraqi National Guard Battalion[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_18/Article_18.htmtarget=_blank]:

The three companies we're responsible for had just graduated basic training when we first got here and since then, they've become a lot more competent at conducting their missions. . . . They've made our presence here much easier in that they're able to go into the cities and converse with populace because they identify with the ING. . . . They know the people and can gain their trust, which is a great way of gathering intelligence.

Training of Iraqi police continues, too. Sixty-six officers[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041203.txttarget=_blank] graduated from special courses ran at the Adnan Training Facility, in the International Zone. "Officers spent two to three weeks negotiating the specialty skill courses intended to augment the standard eight-week police training all officers undergo prior to service and three-week integration training for prior service officers."

Another 140 officers[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041219.txttarget=_blank] graduated from the same facility more recently. And 914 officers[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041220.txttarget=_blank]--including 52 women--graduated from Basic Police Training Courses at the Baghdad Public Safety Academy. In other police training news, FATS Inc.[http://www.portaliraq.com/shownews.php?id=660target=_blank] has been awarded a contract to provide high-level training to Iraqi police involving advanced firearm simulation.

Iraqi authorities have asked Australia to extend its training of police and military personnel to also cover intelligence and security services[http://www.chinadaily.com.cn/english/doc/2004-12/06/content_397667.htmtarget=_blank]. According to Australia's defense minister, Sen. Robert Hill, "[Security] Minister [Qassim] Da'ud wants to train a group of new young leaders, possibly within Australia, in the field of intelligence and security, in order to subject them to alternative cultures and a new way of law enforcement that is different to the brutal techniques used by the Saddam Hussein regime. . . . Minister Da'ud said one of Iraq's big challenges was to develop a new psychology within the intelligence and security units after three decades of dictatorship."

Australia is also considering providing additional resources for training of Iraqi security forces. These will take the form of a 50-strong defense team[http://www.theage.com.au/news/Iraq/More-Australian-trainers-for-Iraq/2004/12/13/1102787017167.html?oneclick=truetarget=_blank] to help train supply and transport personnel in the new Iraqi armed forces:

Hill said this marked a shift in the Australian Defence Force's training role in Iraq from combat-operations training to providing specialist logistics skills. Senator Hill said the new 3rd Australian Army Training Team Iraq would replace Australians who have been training members of the new Iraqi army and navy and who will be back in Australia by February.

Meanwhile, the equipping of the new Iraqi security forces continues. In November[http://www.portaliraq.com/shownews.php?id=668target=_blank], the rollout consisted of:

More than 2 million RPK/PKM machine gun rounds; 1.2 million 9mm pistol rounds; 2.8 million AK-47 rounds; 450,000 12 gauge shotgun rounds including 200,000 slug rounds; 999,000 5.6mm rounds; 48 shotguns; 1,000 various-make 9mm pistols; nearly 1,000 RPK and PKM machineguns; 1,120 smoke and riot grenades; roughly 1,900 9mm Glock pistols; 5,400 AK-47 assault rifles; 20 Walther pistols; 78 rocket propelled grenade launchers; 16,000 sets of body armor; more than 7,400 helmets including 150 riot helmets; 44 French-designed Panhard M3 armored personnel carriers; four T-55 Russian-designed heavy tanks; 18 multi-purposed armored vehicles; and four Comp Air 7SL light reconnaissance aircraft.

The rollout also included nearly 200 vehicle and handheld radios; 150 night vision goggles; some 11,000 field jackets; 3,000 cold weather jackets; 2,200 mattresses and beds; 40,000 desert combat uniforms; 11,000 pairs of running shoes; 300 kneepads; 600 tactical vests; 1,000 holsters; 9,500 t-shirts; 1,200 binocular pairs; 1,000 handcuff sets; 20 blunt trauma suits; 1,450 compasses; 132 GPS positioning systems; 800 'MAG' lights; 750 whistles; 4,150 hats; 344 first aid kits; 2,000 canteens; 1,500 police shirts; 2,000 police uniforms; two 2-ton trucks; 14 ambulances; 10 Russian-made GAZ heavy transport trucks; 15 Chevy trucks; 20 Chevy Trailblazers; four dodge Durangos; and 52 Chevy Lumina police sedans.

The British Defense Ministry also announced a 3.6 million pounds ($7 million) assistance package[http://news.scotsman.com/latest.cfm?id=3891822target=_blank], which will include "438 AK47 grenade launchers with 18,400 rounds of ammunition, 5,666 9mm pistols with 850,000 rounds, just under one million assorted blank rounds for training and radio equipment."

To increase security along the main highway linking Baghdad with Amman, Jordan, four airplanes[http://washingtontimes.com/upi-breaking/20041202-064432-5935r.htmtarget=_blank] will be used for surveillance, giving the security forces eyes in the sky. On the ground[http://www.1id.army.mil/1ID/News/December/Article_14/Article_14.htmtarget=_blank], meanwhile, "in an effort to discourage insurgents from re-entering this former rebel stronghold, Soldiers from the 216th and 9th Engineer Battalions constructed three traffic control points around Ad Duluiyah in November."

Among recent security successes of the Iraqi and the coalition security forces: the arrest of a bomb maker[http://www.defenselink.mil/news/Dec2004/n12022004_2004120202.htmltarget=_blank] and two accomplices at Al Duluiyah; the detention of 56 suspected insurgents[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041204.txttarget=_blank] in a joint U.S.-Iraqi action at the village of Bi'aj near Syrian border, and another 66[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041207.txttarget=_blank] in the town of Avgani near Tal Afar; the capture in a raid on a sports complex in the east of Baghdad of "several suspected senior level transnational terrorists[http://reuters.com/newsArticle.jhtml?type=topNews&storyID=7019518target=_blank], including key leaders, operatives, and financiers"; the arrest of 20 suspected insurgents[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041215.txttarget=_blank] in three Al Rashid District neighborhoods in Baghdad; the discovery in in palm groves near Baquba of weapons caches[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041214.txttarget=_blank] consisting of "10 mortar tubes, 50 artillery rounds, 193 mortar rounds, 162 rocket propelled grenades, 21 blocks of plastic explosive, 3,840 rounds of 7.62 mm ammunition, and boxes of mortar, RPG and mine fuses and propellants"; the detention of 22 suspects[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041210.txttarget=_blank] during a joint American-Iraqi operation in northern Iraq; the uncovering by the Danish troops[http://news.xinhuanet.com/english/2004-12/10/content_2315180.htmtarget=_blank] near Basra of the largest arms cache found by them so far; the killing of Hassan Ibrahim Farhan Zyda, an aide to Abu Musab al-Zarqawi[http://sify.com/news/fullstory.php?id=13629489target=_blank], and capture of Zyda's deputies; and further arrests[http://www.noticias.info/asp/aspComunicados.asp?nid=42237&src=0target=_blank] of suspected insurgents at Rasheed, south of Baghdad, following an unsuccessful attack on a police station. And the Iraqi and the coalition forces continue to successfully foil insurgent[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041227.txttarget=_blank]attacks[http://www.centcom.mil/CENTCOMNews/News_Release.asp?NewsRelease=20041229.txttarget=_blank].

Terrorism, violence and insecurity continue to plague Iraq, and the pace of reconstruction and the economic reform try the patience of people who, having suffered so much, are starved for a better life. Yet among all this, there is good news coming out of Iraq. Soon the Iraqis will have the opportunity to elect their government and thus institutionalize the process of reform taking place over the past year and a half. A few among them want to turn back the clock to before March 2003, while others want to turn it back by hundreds of years, and they will stop at nothing to achieve their goal. Let us hope that the silent majority of Iraqis will in the end prevail.

The Economy
Current-Account Deficit Widened In Third Quarter
By Greg Hitt and Michael Schroeder
708 words
17 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- The U.S. current-account deficit, the government's broadest measure of trade, widened slightly in the third quarter, swelled by demand for imported oil and petroleum products, as well as foreign goods, including autos.

Treasury Secretary John Snow, in an interview with The Wall Street Journal, also sought to shift some of the blame to other countries, saying: "Higher growth rates on the part of our trading partners would help a great deal." He added,"We'd like to see them grow faster so we can sell more . . . of the things that Americans are good at producing to them."

The third-quarter current-account deficit of $164.70 billion equaled 5.7% of gross domestic product, the broadest measure of the nation's output of goods and services. In the second quarter, the deficit was $164.39 billion.

Still, there was some good news in the report. The current account measures the ebb and flow not only of goods and services, but of investment as well, and the report showed that foreign direct investment in the U.S. took a positive turn in the third quarter -- good news for future growth at home.

The Commerce Department said net FDI flows swung sharply in the July-September period, as the U.S. attracted $9.59 billion more in direct investments than was sent abroad. In the second quarter, by comparison, U.S. direct investment abroad outstripped FDI in the U.S. by $22.68 billion.

"We're back in the business of attracting global investment," said economist Dan Griswold of the Cato Institute, a libertarian-oriented think tank. "That's a very good thing for the U.S. economy. That shows confidence for the long run."

Ian Shepherdson, chief economist at High Frequency Economics Ltd., in Valhalla, N.Y., suggested the swing in investment -- the first net inflow since mid-2001 -- is just the beginning of a longer-term turnaround. He said the change is fueled by a brighter assessment by foreign investors of the U.S. economy, compared with Europe and Japan. "We now think everything is in place for a strong and sustained upswing in net direct-investment inflows," he said.

The Commerce Department also reported that housing starts fell 13.1% last month, as bad weather stymied new construction in several areas of the country. Housing starts slid to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.8 million units in November, compared with an upwardly revised level of 2.0 million a month earlier.

Some analysts played down the decline. "Despite the steep drop in start activity, housing fundamentals appear sound: new-home sales were near record levels in October, while mortgage applications for purchase, fueled by continued low mortgage rates, have remained red-hot," said Peter Kretzmer, senior economist at Bank of America Corp.

Low mortgage-lending rates, which averaged around 5.7% in November, have helped buoy the housing market. So far, the Federal Reserve's five short-term interest-rate increases haven't hurt the sector.

Furthermore, permits authorizing new construction fell only 1.5% in November to 2.0 million, and remain 3.5% above year-ago levels. But economists expect new housing activity to flatten in the quarters ahead as rates edge higher.

In a separate report, seasonally adjusted initial jobless claims fell by 43,000 to a five-month low of 317,000 in the week that ended Dec. 11, the Labor Department said. The decline was the largest since the week of Dec. 8, 2001.

The four-week average, which smooths out weekly fluctuations, declined last week to 337,750 -- below the 350,000 benchmark that economists say indicates an improving labor market.

More broadly, workers filing continuing claims for unemployment benefits fell by 50,000 to 2,737,000 in the week that ended Dec. 4, the latest week for which data are available. The unemployment rate for workers with unemployment insurance held steady at 2.2% in that week.

---

Politics & Economy
U.S. Savings Rate Nears a Low, As Spending, Incomes Increase
By Greg Ip
528 words
2 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. personal savings rate fell to its second lowest level on record in October, a development that makes it harder for the U.S. to shrink its huge trade deficit -- a gap that is putting downward pressure on the dollar.

The drop in savings came as personal consumption and income growth remained brisk, and other reports showed continued optimism among manufacturers and blue-chip companies for the months ahead.

Households saved 0.2% of their disposable income in October, down from 0.3% in September, the Commerce Department said yesterday. It was the lowest savings rate recorded since the U.S. government began compiling savings data in 1959, with the exception of October 2001 when statistical quirks related to the prior month's terrorist attacks produced a small negative savings rate.

Drew Matus, an economist at Lehman Brothers, said the savings rate implies someone with take-home pay of $40,000, or about 30,096 euros, a year is on average saving $1.50 a week.

The latest drop in savings came as consumer spending rose faster than income. Personal consumption rose 0.7% in October from September, an acceleration from September's 0.6% gain. Spending on nondurable goods jumped 1.5%, likely reflecting much higher prices for gasoline. Indeed, adjusted for inflation, consumer spending only rose 0.3% in October, a deceleration from September's 0.5% gain.

Personal income rose 0.6%, a pickup from a 0.2% gain in September when hurricanes disrupted workplaces and chopped pay checks. After taxes, disposable income also rose 0.6%.

The falling household savings rate is making it more difficult for the U.S. to rein in its current-account deficit, the shortfall on all trade and investment income between the U.S. and the rest of the world. That gap is running at more than 5% of gross domestic product, meaning that for every $100 the U.S. earns as a nation, it spends about $105, with the difference borrowed from foreigners.

Policy makers primarily have blamed the large U.S. budget deficit for the gap, but the drop in the household savings rate also is a major factor.

Separately, the Institute for Supply Management said its index of manufacturing activity rose to 57.8 in November from 56.8 in October, suggesting growth in the factory sector accelerated. The index is based on a survey of several hundred factory purchasing managers. A reading above 50 suggests manufacturing activity is growing, and below 50 indicates it is contracting. The subindex of new orders, an indicator for factory activity in coming months, rose to 61.5 from 58.3, and the subindex of employment rose to 57.6 from 54.8, suggesting the recent revival in long-battered manufacturing payrolls will continue.

Meanwhile, the Commerce Department said construction spending was unchanged in October from September. Residential construction fell 0.3%, private nonresidential construction rose 0.4%, and public construction increased 1.2%.

International News
Consumer Spending Rises in U.S. --- Incomes Increase Sharply; Manufacturing Index Gain Indicates Sector Expansion
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
744 words
2 December 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- U.S. consumers spent briskly in October, an encouraging sign that the final quarter of 2004 is getting off to a decent start. Incomes rose sharply.

The Commerce Department reported yesterday that consumer spending went up by 0.7% in October, up from a 0.6% rise in September and the best showing since July.

In manufacturing, U.S. factories continued to flex their muscles in November, as a private survey of manufacturers found that the sector booked its eighteenth-straight month of expansion as new orders increased and price pressures remained high.

The Institute for Supply Management reported yesterday that its manufacturing business index rose to a reading of 57.8 from 56.8 in October. That result bucked economists' expectations that the index would idle at its October mark. Any reading above 50 indicates expansion in the sector.

Growth in Americans' incomes -- the fuel for future spending -- jumped by 0.6% in October as employers added significantly to payrolls, according to the Commerce Department's consumer report. That compared with a 0.2% advance in September and marked the biggest increase since May.

The latest snapshot of consumers was better than economists were expecting. They were forecasting a 0.4% rise in consumer spending and a 0.5% increase in income growth.

"Consumers were not deterred by higher oil prices and by consumer confidence numbers that keep on going lower," said Ken Mayland, president of ClearView Economics. "I got to believe this gets the fourth quarter off on very solid footings."

The spending and income figures are not adjusted for price changes.

When adjusted for inflation, consumer spending rose by a more moderate -- but still healthy -- 0.3% in October.

The Federal Reserve, wanting to make sure inflation doesn't become a problem for the economy, has boosted short-term interest rates four times this year. Economists expect another increase on Dec. 14.

An inflation gauge tied to the consumer spending and income report showed that inflation is picking up. Prices rose by 0.4% in October, up from a tiny 0.1% increase in September.

In other economic news, construction spending was virtually unchanged in October from the previous month at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $1.01 trillion, a record. Analysts were expecting a 0.7% rise. The report suggested that construction activity, which has been seeing slower growth since July, still remains vibrant.

The economy grew at a solid 3.9% pace in the third quarter as consumers and businesses spent more freely. Some analysts believe growth in the current October to December quarter could slow a bit, while others believe it could be slightly stronger.

Consumers' resiliency has amazed economists. Analysts said they'll be watching consumers closely to see the extent to which sinking consumer confidence, high energy prices and a job market still recovering from the 2001 recession affect their buying behavior.

Spending by consumers accounts for roughly two-thirds of all economic activity in the U.S. Thus their buying appetite is an important factor in determining the strength of economic growth.

Yesterday's report showed that consumer spending on big-ticket "durable" goods such as cars, rose by 0.2% in October, down from a sizable 1.2% gain in September.

Spending on nondurable goods, such as food and clothing, jumped by 1.5% in October, compared with a 0.8% increase in the prior month. Spending on services rose 0.5% in October, up from a 0.4% gain. The figures are not adjusted for prices changes.

The Bush administration credited the president's tax cuts with helping the economy rebound. But Democrats counter that the tax cuts mainly helped the wealthy and plunged the government's balance sheets deeper into the red.

With consumer spending outpacing income growth in October, the personal savings rate -- savings as a percentage of after-tax income -- dipped to 0.2%, from 0.3% in September. October's savings rate was the lowest in three years.

Economists say the savings rate doesn't provide a complete picture of household finances because it doesn't capture gains realized from such things as higher real-estate values or from financial investments.

Investors Buy Real Estate At Record Pace --- Wary of Stocks, More Individuals Buy Condos and Rental Property; REITs vs. Owning It Yourself
By Ruth Simon
1536 words
30 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A GROWING NUMBER of Americans are buying real estate as an investment, driven by the combination of low interest rates, rising property values and tepid stock-market returns.

Investors accounted for 8% of mortgages used to purchase homes in the first eight months of this year, up from 7.5% in 2003 and 5.7% in 2000, according to an analysis prepared for The Wall Street Journal by LoanPerformance, a San Francisco-based firm that tracks the performance of 46 million mortgages monthly. It's the largest investor share since at least 1986, according to the firm. The number of mortgages used to buy investment property may actually be higher since those figures don't include loans taken out to buy second homes that are used by the owner but may also provide rental income.

The demand for investment property is particularly apparent in rapidly appreciating markets, including Las Vegas, Arizona and parts of California, according to LoanPerformance. But it's evident in other markets as well: In Miami, speculators account for as much as 80% of the preconstruction purchases of luxury condominium units, according to Mark Zilbert, an associate with Esslinger-Wooten-Maxwell Realtors. In one new South Beach condo building, ICON, roughly half of the apartments were bought by investors and then resold before the building's official opening this week, according to the developer, the Related Group of Florida.

The steady upward appreciation of housing prices has provided a sharp contrast to the stock market's performance during recent years. Median home prices climbed 7.7% during the third quarter from a year earlier, with many coastal markets posting double-digit gains, according to the National Association of Realtors. For the five years ended Sept. 30, median home prices rose 39%, versus a loss of 13% for the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index.

Those gains have attracted many individual investors. "It seems like investing in real estate is a wiser decision than putting [the money] into a stock market or savings account or savings bonds," says Kelly McDonnell, a recruiter for an educational testing company in Amherst, Mass. Ms. McDonnell is currently shopping for a two-family duplex she can rent out and then, she hopes, sell for a profit in a few years.

But investors betting on double-digit gains could end up disappointed. Falling interest rates have helped fuel the recent rise in home prices, but rates are more likely to rise than fall going forward. Already, there are signs that the housing market is cooling. The 7.7% increase in median home prices during the third quarter, while still above historical levels, represents a decline from the 8.9% annual gain posted in the second quarter, according to the National Association of Realtors.

One alternative: real-estate investment trusts, which buy and then rent out shopping malls, apartments, office buildings and other real estate.

REITs give investors access to a diversified mix of properties and a hefty dividend without the management headaches. Already this year, investors have poured a record $5.17 billion into funds that invest in real estate, according to AMG Data Services in Arcata, Calif. The inflows, which cover the period through a week ago today, exceed the record $4.75 billion that poured into real-estate funds in all of 2003.

Though considered a relatively safe investment, REITs can also be volatile. REIT prices fell roughly 20% during a six-week period this spring after a strong jobs report raised fears that the Federal Reserve would be more aggressive about raising interest rates. That would have made yields on REITs less attractive.

Prices have since rebounded, but whether REITs can maintain their upward trajectory is a matter of debate. They currently trade at roughly 19 times adjusted funds from operations -- a commonly used measure of REIT earnings -- well above their historical average of 12 times adjusted FFO, according to Mike Kirby, a principal with Green Street Advisors, an independent REIT research firm. The average yield on a real-estate fund is now 2.8%, according to Morningstar Inc., down from about 4% three years ago. "On a pure valuation level, it's hard to make the case that real-estate funds are a good buy now," says Morningstar senior analyst Dan McNeela.

Still, some analysts say that REITs remain attractive for investors with a long-term time horizon. Mr. Kirby expects REITs to generate long-term returns of 9% a year.

Investors interested in owning hard real-estate assets range from first-time speculators to wealthy individuals. Erik Friis, a San Diego attorney, started buying San Diego real estate in the late 1990s, then set his sights on Arizona as prices in California climbed. "It came to a point where there weren't any properties that made sense to buy," says Mr. Friis, who recently sold a small apartment building in San Diego and used the proceeds to buy a 24,000-square-foot shopping center in Tucson.

In Long Beach, Calif., there's "more of a demand than ever for investment property," says Richard Gaylord of Re/Max Real Estate Specialists. "The problem is that inventory is so low that I'm pulling my hair out trying to find them stuff."

Many buyers are new to real-estate investing. In Tucson, "people who never looked at real estate as being part of their investment portfolio . . . are either acquiring residential income property or participating in partnerships," says Rosey Koberlein, president of Long Realty Co.

In Warwick, R.I., broker Ron Phipps says many investors are opting for properties with good locations, even if a high purchase price means they won't cover their costs for the next few years, Mr. Phipps says. Others are buying investment properties that may eventually be used by a child or aging parent.

Real-estate purchases are becoming increasingly popular among people with $1 million to $10 million in assets, says Kevin Ruth, a senior vice president and director of financial planning at UBS AG's UBS Financial Services unit. In the Wisconsin Dells resort area, condos that double as second homes and rental properties "are going like hotcakes," says Michael Dubis, a financial planner in Madison, Wis.

The rise in investment purchases has caught the attention of many who track the housing market, including Federal Reserve Board Chairman Alan Greenspan. In a speech before America's Community Bankers in October, Mr. Greenspan noted that purchases of single-family homes for rental or use as second homes have grown in recent years, though he said they still accounted for less than 11% of total home-mortgage originations in 2003.

Some housing experts worry that investor demand has helped fuel the recent gains in home prices and could accentuate any downturn as interest rates rise and demand slows. The country is experiencing "investor-driven price increases in certain markets. . . . And these markets could soften as investor demand wanes, as it inevitably does," Fannie Mae Chairman Franklin Raines said in a speech last month.

The perils of betting on short-term profits have been particularly apparent in Las Vegas, where speculators helped push prices up nearly 54% in the past year, the highest growth rate ever measured in a metropolitan area, according to the National Association of Realtors. When a falloff in sales and traffic led a handful of builders to cut prices this fall, the reductions "hit investors hardest," says Larry Murphy, president of SalesTraq, which tracks the Las Vegas real-estate market.

Owning real estate, meanwhile, isn't as simple as purchasing a stock or a bond. Investors need to be ready to handle late-night calls about broken water pipes and faulty heating systems -- or hire someone to manage these and other headaches. Management fees often run 5% to 10% of gross rents. Moreover, rising housing prices are, in many cases, making it tough for investors to cover expenses -- such as mortgage payments and property taxes -- through rental payments.

---

The Economy
Holiday Spirits Lifted by New Data --- Consumers Seen as Jollier, Jobless Claims Are Scarcer As New-Home Sales Persist
By Constance Mitchell Ford
751 words
26 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The economy entered the holiday-shopping season on a high note, as an improving job market, strong consumer sentiment and gains in home sales indicate that consumers are still willing and able to spend.

Economic conditions "look extremely good going into the Christmas season," says Susan Sterne, president of Economic Analysis Associates in Greenwich, Conn., which is forecasting a 7.5% rise in nonauto general merchandise sales in the fourth quarter compared with a year earlier. "There is every reason to believe that holiday sales will be better than last year."

Like other economists, Ms. Sterne's optimism about the season was reinforced by a slew of economic data released Wednesday, some of which suggested that the economy is growing faster than previously projected.

The University of Michigan's report on consumer sentiment for November, which is released only to subscribers, was said by those who had seen it to have increased to a reading of 92.8 for the month. While that was lower than the preliminary reading for November that was released a few weeks ago, it's still higher than October's reading and a sign that sentiment improved.

"The sentiment reading was good, not great, but good," says David Resler, chief economist at Nomura Securities. He said the sentiment reading might have been higher had it not been for changing buying attitudes about autos, which he said were the least favorable in three years. That, says Mr. Resler, may indicate that spending is shifting away from big-ticket items toward smaller items, a trend that could help sales of general merchandise and apparel.

Economists were also heartened by a Department of Labor report that showed new claims for unemployment insurance fell last week to 323,000, a three-month low. The four-week moving average of claims, which smooths out weekly fluctuations, dropped last week to 332,000, the lowest level since Nov. 18, 2000.

Meanwhile, the Census Bureau reported that sales of new homes increased in October to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.23 million units, the third-highest level on record and a much stronger performance than economists had expected.

A report on the industrial sector was more sobering, yet some economists found reasons to be optimistic. The Commerce Department reported that orders for durable goods -- high-price manufactured products expected to last at least three years -- declined 0.4% in October. Factory activity was weighed down by weakness in new bookings placed for automobiles, computers, electrical equipment and primary metals, which include steel.

But while the overall number was a disappointment, as many economists were expecting a moderate gain, components of the report were more upbeat.

First of all, orders in September were revised to show a 0.9% gain, much stronger than the government previously estimated. And shipments of durable goods for October -- a good barometer of current demand -- rose 0.6%. According to economists at J.P. Morgan Chase, when excluding defense goods and aircraft, "core" durable goods shipments are up an annualized 20% over the third quarter.

On balance, Wednesday's economic reports were "unambiguously good" said Ted Weisman, an economist at Morgan Stanley. Based on the latest data, Morgan Stanley revised up its fourth-quarter estimate of real gross domestic product -- the value of the nation's output, adjusted for inflation -- to an annualized growth rate of 4.4% from its previous estimate of 4%.

Morgan Stanley's estimate is much higher than the consensus forecast of economists surveyed earlier this month by The Wall Street Journal Online, which called for fourth-quarter GDP growth of 3.7%, the same as the pace of growth clocked in the third quarter.

Merrill Lynch raised its fourth-quarter estimate of GDP growth to 3.5% from 2.5%, but the firm's economists say they are sticking with a forecast for slower growth of 2.5% in the first quarter of 2005. In written comments to clients, David Rosenberg, Merrill's chief North American economist, said the forecast of slowing growth reflects "five consecutive down months on the leading economic indicator and the sharp slowing" of new orders for technology products.

---

Fixed Mortgage Rates Show Little Change, While ARMs Increase
Dow Jones Newswires
233 words
26 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

NEW YORK -- Long-term fixed home-mortgage rates were flat to a bit lower this week, while Treasury-indexed adjustable-rate mortgages rose slightly, Freddie Mac, the housing-finance agency, said Wednesday.

The average for 30-year fixed-rate mortgages for the week ended Wednesday fell to 5.72% from 5.74% a week earlier. A year ago, the 30-year fixed-rate mortgage was at 5.83%.

The average for 15-year fixed-rate mortgages this week was 5.15%, unchanged from one week ago and down from 5.17% a year ago.

The average rate for one-year Treasury-indexed adjustable-rate mortgages rose to 4.27% from last week's average of 4.17%, and a year-earlier 3.72%.

"At this time last year, our forecast called for interest rates for 30-year fixed-rate mortgages to exceed 6% by this time this year," said Frank Nothaft, Freddie Mac vice president and chief economist. "Today's annual mortgage rates are below even that projection thanks to the spring `soft patch' in economic growth. Nevertheless, our outlook is that long-term rates are destined to rise to a still home-buyer friendly range that will most likely cause home sales to cool relative to their current record highs," he said.

Existing-Home Sales Rate Slowed a Bit Last Month
Associated Press
359 words
24 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Sales of previously owned homes eased in October but still clocked in at the fourth-highest pace on record, a fresh sign that the housing market is in solid shape.

The National Association of Realtors reported that sales of existing homes totaled a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 6.75 million units in October, a 0.1% decline from September.

Despite the decline, analysts say home sales remain on course to set a record for 2004, aided by low mortgage rates. "The ongoing stimulus of lower-than-expected mortgage interest rates was the primary driver of strong home sales in October," said David Lereah, the association's chief economist.

Although the Federal Reserve has been pushing short-term interest rates higher, mortgage rates have actually drifted lower in recent months. The average monthly rate on a 30-year mortgage in October was 5.72%. That was down from 5.75% in September and considerably below 5.95% in October 2003.

Some economists say an improving employment climate also is helping to underpin home sales. In October, the economy added a sizable 337,000 jobs, the most since March. If payroll gains are sustained going forward, says Mr. Lereah, that also should help support the housing market in the months ahead.

The median price of a previously owned home was $187,000 in October, up 8.8% from the same month last year. The median is the midpoint price; half of all homes sell for more than the median and half sell for less.

Yesterday's report showed some sharp differences by region. Sales fell in all parts of the country except the South, where they rose by 3.7% in October from September to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 2.77 million. In the Northeast, sales dropped 1.3% to a rate of 750,000. In the Midwest, sales clocked in at a rate of 1.37 million, a 2.8% decline from September. Sales in the West fell 3.6% to a rate of 1.86 million.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Justin Lahart
450 words
23 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

[Today's Market Forecast]

House of Cards?

Many economists see a slowdown in housing as a "when," not an "if." But the "when" keeps getting pushed further into the future.

Today's report on existing-home sales from the National Association of Realtors looks likely to show another month of strength. Economists expect that October existing-home sales -- which might otherwise be termed "used-home sales" -- came in at an annualized clip of 6.7 million. That is 4.5% above the year-ago level and not far off of September's 6.75 million.

Hang the spry housing market on mortgage rates, which most people reckoned would rise through the course of the year but instead are slightly lower than they were at 2004's outset, according to Freddie Mac. Falling interest rates and an attendant rising rate of home ownership have been the main factors behind the housing boom.

Still, some chinks have appeared lately in the housing market's armor. Some hot markets like Southern California and, especially, Las Vegas have cooled and prices in general appear to have moderated.

At the same time, the Federal Reserve appears set to raise its target federal-funds interest rate by another quarter point when it next meets in December, and recent comments from Fed officials suggest they expect to continue raising rates through much of next year. That would probably push long-term Treasury yields up, as well, leading to higher mortgage rates.

With U.S. household debt levels near all-time highs, by any common measure, a rise in rates could damp consumer spending, points out Merrill Lynch economist Jose Rasco. First, a greater share of household income would need to be earmarked toward debt financing. Second, if the rise in rates also made home prices falter, it would mean that the asset that households had used as collateral had fallen.

Merrill's strategy group has pointed out that just as an investor who uses leverage to buy a stock will see outsized gains or losses, someone who has, say, made a down payment of only $10,000 on a $100,000 home may be more affected by price swings than someone who has put down $50,000 on an identical home. If the homes' value fell to $90,000 and they both sell, the first will have lost all the money he put down, while the second will have seen a loss of just 20%. Even if the first buyer hangs onto his home, his spending behavior might be affected.

The Economy
Inflation Perks Up as the Fed Weighs Pace of Rate Increases
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
769 words
18 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Consumer-price inflation picked up in October, a development that is likely to get the attention of Federal Reserve policy makers as they weigh whether to alter the pace of increases in short-term interest rates during the months ahead.

The Labor Department reported yesterday that its consumer-price index rose 0.6% in October on a seasonally adjusted basis from the month before, the largest increase in five months. Rising energy and food prices drove the increase, but other goods and services played a role. Medical-care prices accelerated after slowing earlier in the year and apparel prices rose for the first time since June.

Excluding food and energy, consumer prices were up 0.2% from the month before, which is faster than the pace of increases between June and August, though down slightly from September. The report, taken together with large increases in producer prices reported earlier in the week, suggested the country's inflation rate, while still low, might have perked up after a lull between June and September.

Some of these trends could reverse. Crude-oil prices have receded in recent weeks and there are signs that food-price increases have tapered off. Moreover, apparel prices could recede next year as textile import quotas are removed. But the aftereffects of price increases already seen still are reverberating through some segments of the economy.

At Samantha's Finefoods-Catering, an Italian delicatessen in lower Manhattan, food- and energy-price increases are pinching. Co-owner Dominick Liuzzi says delivery trucks for the past year have been demanding $3 to $4 surcharges to compensate for costlier gasoline. Prices for green peppers, tomatoes and high-end provolone cheese have moved higher.

"You'll probably see an increase in the [price of] heroes," Mr. Liuzzi says.

While inflation perked up, other reports pointed to plenty of momentum in the underlying economy. Industrial production rose 0.7% last month, after a 0.1% increase in September, the Fed said. Housing starts increased 6.4% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of about two million units, the fastest pace of new construction since December 2003, the Commerce Department said.

Industrial output and housing construction probably were nudged higher by the resumption of activity following hurricanes in the South. Nevertheless, economists said the reports sounded a common theme.

"The economy is showing a lot of resilience in the face of higher energy prices," said David Greenlaw, an economist with Morgan Stanley. This leaves the Fed "the ammunition to keep moving" short-term interest rates higher.

Last week, the Fed raised the target for the federal funds rate -- charged on overnight loans between banks -- to 2% from 1.75%, the fourth increase this year.

Minutes of the Fed's Sept. 21 policy meeting showed policy makers were open to slowing the pace of rate increases if they spotted signs of anemic economic growth or low inflation. In comments this week, Fed officials have voiced little concern about inflation. At the same time, they have given little indication they are prepared to alter the gradual pace of rate increases just yet.

Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland President Sandra Pianalto said yesterday that oil-price gains haven't had a major inflationary impact, while other Fed officials have spoken pointedly about more rate increases. "Our work is not done," Fed Governor Mark Olson said Monday. On Tuesday, Michael Moskow, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago, echoed that sentiment, saying "there is certainly more ground to cover" to shift interest rates to a less stimulative level.

Most of the data this month are pointing to sustained expansion. As a result, the financial futures markets are pricing in a high likelihood that the Fed will move the federal-funds rate to 2.25% in December and 2.5% in February.

In its report yesterday, the Labor Department said consumer prices were up 3.2% in the 12 months through October, up from a 2.5% rate in the 12 months through September. The core consumer-price index was up 2% in the 12 months through October, the same as in September.

Energy prices rose 4.2% in October alone from the month before, the largest monthly increase since May. However, recent declines in crude-petroleum prices suggest this index could see downward pressure in November.

Home &Family: First-Time Home Buyers Kept Housing Boom Going This Year
By Raymund Flandez
701 words
18 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D7
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

First-time home buyers have played an important role in keeping the housing boom going, according to a recent report.

Purchases by first-time buyers accounted for four out of 10 home purchases, and were a major driving force of the year's housing-market surge, a study released early this month by the National Association of Realtors found. The dollars pouring into the market by first-time buyers also helped homes sales by providing existing homeowners the capital to buy new homes.

Coupled with historic lows in interest rates and an improving economy, existing-home sales are expected to shoot up to a record high of 6.55 million this year, up 7.4% from 6.1 million in 2003, the Realtors association says. The group predicts existing-home sales will hold steady around six-million-plus for the next couple of years.

First-time home buyers accounted for 3.23 million home-sale transactions overall, including new and existing family homes and existing condos. They purchased more single-family homes, about 3.09 million, up 7.4% from 2.87 million last year.

"It's all due to entry-level buyers," says Walt Molony, an National Association of Realtors spokesman. "Without the liquidity that first-time buyers are providing to the marketplace, we wouldn't be having record sales." The study, which was based on a national sample of more than 8,000 recent home buyers, is the latest in a series of annual surveys by the Realtors association evaluating the demographics and other aspects of the housing market.

The trend is expected to continue in the next decade as children of baby boomers age into their prime years for buying a home. These "echo-boomers," as well as minorities and immigrants, make up an increasingly bigger part of the pool of new home buyers, and are expected to keep the real-estate sales bustling as they become "tomorrow's repeat buyers," the study says.

The impact of baby boomers on the housing market was most evident in the late-1970s, when they became first-time buyers themselves. In 1978, the baby-boom generation took home sales to a record-breaking 3.98 million. The housing market didn't take off again until the mid-90s, when 4.02 million existing homes changed hands in 1996. The housing market then was buoyed from declining interest rates that increased consumer-buying power. Home sales have set records each year since then, aside from a brief dip in 2000 because of a recession.

The report found that the average first-time buyer is 32 years old and has a household income of $54,500. In contrast, a repeat buyer is 45 years old with a household income of $79,100.

According to the survey, the majority of first-time home buyers are white, while one in 10 is black, although the data may be skewed because many of those surveyed declined to describe their race. First-time buyers also are somewhat more likely to speak a language other than English compared with repeat buyers, "reflecting a higher share of recent immigrants among first-time buyers," the study says.

As for the types of homes they buy, first-timers are more likely to buy townhouses or row houses in urban areas than the detached single-family homes in suburban areas preferred by repeat buyers.

Today, first-time home buyers have more options in financing their homes than 20 years ago. And with expectations of rising incomes over time, these buyers are making good use of financing plans. The study says first-time home buyers financed a median of 97% of the purchase price, with 42% reported financing the entire purchase price of the home. Meanwhile, repeaters, who often have equity in their previous home, financed a median of only 78% of the purchase price.

The National Association of Realtors estimates that the interest rate for a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage will stay under 6% for the rest of this year and will average 6.5% next year.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

International News
U.S. Inflation Perks Up, Driven by Energy Prices --- Reports on Output, Housing Suggest Economy Carried Momentum Into 4th Period
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
608 words
18 November 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

U.S. Federal Reserve officials have signaled they would be taking a very close look at incoming economic data before deciding how aggressively to raise short-term interest rates in the months ahead. Well, the data are coming in, and they suggest the modest pace of rate increases will continue for now.

The Labor Department reported yesterday that its consumer-price index -- a closely watched gauge of inflation -- rose by a larger-than-expected 0.6% in October, the largest increase in five months. The increase was driven by surging energy and food prices, which seem on course to reverse in November. But other goods and services also played a role, including medical-care prices, which showed signs of some acceleration after slowing earlier in the year, and apparel prices, which rose for the first time since June.

While inflation was perking up after a lull in the past few months, other reports suggested the underlying economy carried plenty of momentum going into the fourth quarter, with industrial production and housing construction both rising. Industrial production grew 0.7% last month after a 0.1% increase in September and a 0.1% decline in August. Housing starts last month increased 6.4% to a seasonally adjusted 2,027,000 annual rate, the highest level since December 2003, the Commerce Department said.

The production and housing reports may have been nudged higher by the resumption of activity following hurricanes in the South. Yet economists said the reports sounded a common theme.

"The economy is showing a lot of resilience in the face of higher energy prices," said David Greenlaw, a fixed-income strategist with Morgan Stanley. This leaves the Fed "the ammunition to keep moving" short-term interest rates higher, he said.

Last week, Fed policy makers raised the target for the federal-funds rate -- charged on overnight loans between banks -- to 2% from 1.75%, the fourth increase this year. The markets are now pricing in a high likelihood that the Fed will move the rate to 2.25% in December.

Despite the latest pickup in consumer prices, economists said inflation wasn't accelerating at an alarming pace. In its report yesterday, the Labor Department said consumer prices were up 3.2% in the 12 months ended October, up from a 2.5% rate in the 12 months ended September. But the core consumer-price index -- which excludes volatile food and energy prices -- showed no acceleration in the growth of prices. It was up 2% in the 12 months through October, the same as in September. It rose 0.2% from the month before on a seasonally adjusted basis.

Energy prices rose 4.2% in October, the largest increase since May. Gasoline prices registered an 8.6% increase, while fuel-oil prices rose 9.4%, both the biggest increases in 20 months. However, the recent fall in crude petroleum prices suggests these indexes could see downward pressure in November.

Food prices also surged in October, climbing 0.6%, the biggest increase since May. Prices of fresh fruits rose 6.3%, the biggest increase in more than 20 years. Prices of fresh vegetables rose 8.8%, the biggest increase since early 1997. Patrick Jackman, a Labor Department economist, said some of those increases were "associated with the hurricanes in Florida."

Opinion Journal; Extra
Black and White and Red All Over? A look at the past two weeks' good news from Iraq.
ARTHUR CHRENKOFF
11862 words
7 November 2004
23:01
The Wall Street Journal (Online and Print)
WSJO
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Bruce Chapman[http://www.opinionjournal.com/extra/?id=110005783target=_blank] of the Discovery Institute recounted on this Web site not long ago how "basking in the sun by the Al Hamra Hotel swimming pool, a Spanish journalist complained to me that 'all my editors want is blood, blood, blood. No context. No politics.' "

There certainly has been enough carnage in Iraq to satisfy even the most bloodthirsty editor. Although not as bloody as, say, the Darfur region in Sudan, which has few hotels with swimming pools, Iraq keeps providing plenty of beheadings, suicide bombings, ambushes and firefights to color red the newspaper pages and nightly news bulletins.

If your regular reading and viewing habits have fulfilled your fortnightly quota of "blood, blood, blood," read on for some "context" and "politics." Yes, there is more to Iraq than gore.

Society. The Iraqi Interim Government is now online[http://www.iraqigovernment.org/test/index.htmtarget=_blank].

Powerline blog, via one of its readers, brings to our attention the results of an opinion poll[http://powerlineblog.com/archives/008379.phptarget=_blank] that is not getting any publicity outside Iraq. "[The] poll taken in Baghdad, Mosul and Dehok and published in Iraq on October 25. The poll probably over-sampled Sunnis, which makes its results even more striking":

63% of Iraqis say that the withdrawal of American and allied forces will not be in the best interest of Iraq, it will undermine the work towards security and control of the country. 27% say that it would be in the best interest of Iraq. 9% had no opinion.

58% say that terrorists do the kidnappings and assassination of police and soldiers. 9% say that patriots fighting for Iraq carry them out. 32% say ignorant Iraqis who have been brain washed & misled carry them out.

89% said that the terrorism, kidnapping, beheadings and assassination of police and security forces do not help the freeing of Iraq and the building of a stable country. 6% said that it would help free Iraq and build stability. 4% had no opinion.

It seems that insurgents are failing not only to win popular support but also to slow the march toward democracy. Iraq's Shia religious establishment has now thrown its weight and moral authority behind the election[http://iraqthemodel.blogspot.com/archives/2004_10_01_iraqthemodel_archive.html#109847865029537130target=_blank]:

Ahmed Al Safi, a senior aide of Ayatollah Sistani announced . . . that "Those who don't participate in the elections will end up in hell" and he added in his speech "We must bear the responsibility and we must all participate in the elections because it's a patriotic duty and not doing so is like treason." He also denied the news that spread about Sistani preparing or supporting a particular list of candidates.

Iraqi blogger Zeyad has more[http://healingiraq.blogspot.com/archives/2004_10_01_healingiraq_archive.html#109874394986128505target=_blank]. Buoyed by the religious imprimatur, the country's Shia majority[http://www.iwpr.net/index.pl?archive/irq/irq_86_1_eng.txt] is increasingly looking forward to exercising their democratic rights:

Ayatollah Sistani has . . . asked his representatives to form committees--comprising members of all faiths--to provide information and guidance to Iraqis in the run-up to the 2005 elections, which he regards as vitally important to the future of Iraq.

Sheik Adel al-Ramahi, Sistani's representative in east Baghdad and a committee supervisor, has called for the last day of January--voting day--to be declared a public holiday to reduce the chances of people abstaining for work reasons.

Kurdish blogger Kurdo, meanwhile, posts on how the political parties in his part of Iraq[http://kurdo.blogspot.com/2004/10/good-news-from-kurdistan-i-have-some.htmltarget=_blank] are preparing for the elections.

Iraq's free press[http://www.rferl.org/featuresarticle/2004/10/d7a3c71a-e5a3-4bf9-a263-9a4fcc8908a6.htmltarget=_blank] has certainly been making it difficult for everyone to overlook the coming of democracy:

Newspapers in Iraq have been offering up a barrage of daily reports and opinion pieces over the past month on a variety of election-related subjects. Politicians and religious leaders "in the know" have commented on election developments, as the official Electoral Commission has detailed information on the mechanisms established to become a candidate and on voting. Articles have appeared on voter-education seminars that are being offered by political parties and organizations; the likelihood of whether or not expatriates will be allowed to vote from abroad, whether Sunnis will participate in the elections, as well as the political maneuverings as the parties work to forge alliances and place their candidates on election lists that will meet the stringent requirements established by the commission.

But perhaps the most salient barometer of the "mood" in Iraq can be found on the editorial pages of Iraq's dailies. Commentaries overwhelmingly support the elections and offer intelligent and well-constructed viewpoints on a variety of election-related topics. Writers regularly demand that the Electoral Commission provide more information on the election process, and call on the Iraqi people to cast their ballots on election day.

Writers publishing in a variety of newspapers supporting divergent political positions appear to agree on one fact: elections should not be derailed by terrorism and instability. Most contributors have stressed the necessity of holding nationwide polling. But some writers support the idea that partial elections in stable areas would be better than no elections. "Attaining half or three-quarters of legitimacy, so to speak, is better than no legitimacy at all in order to respond to the doubters and silence the loud voices that keep accusing the government of treason and illegitimacy. They act as if the whole Arab world enjoys legitimacy and as if Iraq is the only exception in the region that has no legitimacy in the middle of [an] ocean of Arab legitimacy," Latif al-Subayhawi wrote in the 18 October edition of "Al-Dustur."

The report notes that "news of Iraq's upcoming January elections has dominated the pages of Iraq's major dailies in recent weeks, to some extent crowding out the more detailed coverage of the growing insurgency, the presence of multinational forces, and even the workings of the interim administration." Which arguably demonstrates that the Iraqis are fully aware of how crucial the elections are to the future of their country.

The newspapers may be doing their best, but clearly there is no such thing as too much civic education in a country that had suffered under a brutal dictatorship for some three decades. The Wall Street Journal's Daniel Henninger reports on the latest cooperative initiative between the Spirit of America and enthusiastic Iraqis. The project, Friends of Democracy[http://www.opinionjournal.com/columnists/dhenninger/?id=110005794target=_blank], aims "to educate the Iraqi people about the meaning and purpose of democracy before that January election date." Read the whole story and see if you can assist this very valuable project.

With the democratic steamroller gaining speed, even United Nations officials[http://abcnews.go.com/International/wireStory?id=187984target=_blank] in Baghdad are increasingly optimistic:

Preparations for the crucial January election are "on track" and the absence of international observers due to the country's tenuous security should not detract from the vote's credibility, the top U.N. electoral expert here said. . . .

"International observation is important only in that it's symbolic," Carlos Valenzuela told The Associated Press. . . . "I don't think that the process will be less credible without observers, absolutely not. They are not the essence. They are not essential. They are not important. If they can come, fine, of course."
