The Economy
Durable-Goods Orders Climb; New-Home Sales Leap by 3.5% --- Data Boost Growth Picture, With Business Spending Showing Signs of Picking Up
By Michael Schroeder and Elizabeth Price
512 words
28 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- U.S. factory orders for big-ticket durable goods rose modestly last month, boosted by increased demand for machinery and computers.

Orders for durable goods -- items expected to last three or more years -- increased 0.2% to $195.7 billion in September, following a revised decline of 0.6% in August, the Commerce Department said.

Separately, the department reported that new-home sales rose 3.5% last month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.21 million units, the third-highest level on record. Total U.S. construction activity for 2004 is poised to register its largest gain since the late 1990s, according to a report by McGraw-Hill Construction.

Adding to this growth picture, the Federal Reserve said business spending appeared to pick up in most regions of the country, with modest gains in capital spending and hiring.

The U.S. economy continued to steam ahead in September and early October, but several districts reported slower growth amid rising energy costs, according to a survey of regional economic activity -- the so-called beige book -- prepared for use at the central bank's next meeting of the Federal Open Market Committee on Nov. 10.

Of the 12 Fed districts, four reported slower growth, five cited continued economic expansion, two reported faster growth and one said activity was mixed.

Some economists viewed the durable-goods data as a positive sign for continued economic growth. "Machinery orders were up 19% this year versus one year ago and demonstrate that business is confident enough about the outlook to replace and expand their productive capacity," said Daniel J. Meckstroth, chief economist for Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI, an Arlington, Va., association of manufacturers.

Orders for nondefense capital goods excluding aircraft -- a barometer of business spending -- rose 2.6% last month, after a 0.3% increase in August.

The September report showed rising demand for machinery, up 2.9%, and for computers and electronics, up 9.3%.

Low mortgage rates have continued to support the housing sector. September new-home sales were up in all U.S. regions except the West, where sales fell 0.8%. Sales were up 6% in the Northeast, 12.3% in the Midwest and 2.7% in the South.

The value of construction activity is expected to rise 9% this year to $576.7 billion, according to McGraw-Hill Construction.

This year's activity has been stronger than expected as mortgage rates have stayed low. McGraw-Hill Construction expects single-family housing construction to increase 14% to $276.6 billion in 2004 and construction starts to grow to 1.53 million units from 1.42 million units in 2003.

"What really pushed the construction figures upward was single-family housing achieving a record high," said Robert Murray, vice president of economic affairs at McGraw-Hill Construction.

WASHINGTON -- The U.S. economy continued to steam ahead in September and early October, but several districts reported slower growth amid rising energy costs, the Federal Reserve said.

The Beige Book is a summary of economic activity prepared for use at the central bank's next Federal Open Market Committee on Nov. 10.

The most recent report was prepared by the Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago and reflects data gathered before Oct. 18.

In its previous Beige Book report Sept. 8, the Fed said the U.S. economy remained buoyant in late July and August, but the pace of growth slowed in some districts amid weaker consumer spending and softness in the housing market.

Orders for durable goods -- items expected to last three or more years -- increased 0.2% to $195.7 billion (153.5 billion euros), following a revised decline of 0.6% in August, the U.S. Commerce Department said.

Meckstroth, chief economist for the Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI, an Arlington, Virginia, association of manufacturers.

Orders for nondefense capital goods excluding aircraft -- a barometer of business spending -- rose 2.6% last month, after a 0.3% increase in August.

The September report showed rising demand for machinery, up 2.9%, and computers and electronics, up 9.3%. Most of the weakness of the past couple of months has resulted from a $7 billion drop in the level of transportation orders, following a July surge.

Existing-home sales among the regions were strong, except for the South. In the Northeast, sales were up 4.1%, and in the Midwest, sales rose 3.7%. Home resales in the South slipped 0.7% while the West region saw sales advance 7.8%.

The Economy
Greenspan Again Plays Down Fear Of Housing Bubble
By Greg Ip
598 words
20 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan again played down worries of a bubble in the housing market, and argued that speculative buying -- while rising by some measures -- remains low.

Mr. Greenspan, in a speech to a banking group here, also said U.S. households shouldn't have any trouble handling record debt levels, though he acknowledged that worries over such debt "cannot be readily dismissed."

Houses aren't as prone to bubbles as stocks because high transaction costs and a seller's need of shelter are "significant impediments to speculative trading," the Fed chief said. While some buyers have bid through offering prices, they have "contributed only modestly to overall house price speculation," he added.

Speculators are most likely to be investors in second homes and rental properties, Mr. Greenspan told the group America's Community Bankers, but those categories accounted for less than 11% of the value of single-family home mortgage originations last year. Still, Fed estimates show that's up substantially from less than 6% in 1998.

Since the housing market took off a few years ago following the Fed's deep interest-rate cuts, concerns that the housing market is a bubble have mounted. Fed officials have usually dismissed such worries, but as housing-price appreciation accelerated this year, some began to fret.

Prices were up 9.4% in the second quarter from a year earlier, the Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight has reported, the fastest in 25 years. In an August letter to Congress, Mr. Greenspan acknowledged the possibility that "real-estate prices, at least in some markets, could be out of alignment with the fundamentals."

Last week, economists at Goldman Sachs said houses are almost 10% overvalued, there are "signs of excess supply" in housing and "home buyers seem to have developed a speculative mindset."

Meanwhile, the Commerce Department said housing starts fell 6% last month from the previous one, to a still-healthy 1.898 million annual rate. Single-family starts fell 8.2%. Economists had expected a drop because of hurricanes, but the South, which felt the brunt of the storms, actually experienced the smallest drop of the four regions surveyed, suggesting a weak underlying trend. But permits for new units rose 1.8% to an annual rate of two million.

In his speech yesterday, Mr. Greenspan reiterated his longstanding view that while "local economies may experience significant speculative price imbalances, a national severe price distortion seems most unlikely in the United States, given its size and diversity."

Were prices to decline, he said they wouldn't likely fall enough to endanger households' finances. The down payment equals or exceeds 20% in about three-quarters of outstanding first mortgages, he said, though some people borrow for their down payment as well. And in aggregate, mortgage debt equals about 45% of home values. "Many of those who purchased their residences more than a year ago have equity buffers in their homes adequate to withstand any price decline other than a very deep one."

The ratio of household debt to income has risen in recent years, to a record. But Mr. Greenspan noted that ratio has risen steadily for at least half a century, reflecting rising homeownership and financial innovation that has allowed more consumers to use credit, and for more purposes.

"To be sure, some households are stretched to their limits," he said. "But the vast majority appear able to calibrate their borrowing and spending to minimize financial difficulties."

International News
Greenspan Plays Down Debt Fears --- Fed Chief Says Rise in Rates Won't Capsize Households; Consumer Prices Rise 0.2%
By Joseph Rebello and Michael Schroeder
794 words
20 October 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

Corrections & Amplifications

U.S. FEDERAL RESERVE Chairman Alan Greenspan said Friday that the rise in the value of imported oil so far this year equaled about 0.75% of gross domestic product. Page-one articles Monday and yesterday incorrectly said Mr. Greenspan said higher oil prices had reduced growth by three-quarters of a percentage point.

(AWSJ Oct. 21, 2004)

(END)

WASHINGTON -- U.S. Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan played down fears that the rapid growth of U.S. household debt could trigger a crash in housing prices.

Meanwhile, U.S. consumer prices rose a modest 0.2% in September, while housing starts dropped 6% to a seasonally adjusted 1.9 million annual rate last month, according to separate government reports.

In remarks prepared for a speech to bankers, Mr. Greenspan acknowledged that "some households are stretched to their limits," but he said that most are in "reasonably good shape" and can absorb gradual increases in interest rates.

Most Americans who bought houses more than a year ago have enough equity to "withstand any price decline other than a very deep one," Mr. Greenspan said. In addition, he noted that the ratio of household net worth to income has risen recently.

U.S. household debt has climbed rapidly during the past five years, raising debt to a record 1.2 times disposable income. That trend worries some economists who say households will find it increasingly hard to service their debt as the Fed raises interest rates.

The U.S. central bank has raised its federal-funds rate, charged on overnight loans between banks, by three-quarters percentage point to 1.75% since June and is expected to make another quarter-point increase next month.

But Mr. Greenspan said the debt-to-income ratio paints an unduly gloomy picture of the state of household finances. A more appropriate measure, he said, is the ratio of household net worth to income. Net worth is now five times household income, he said, up from 4.5 for most of the past 60 years.

"Some of the rise in the ratios of household debt to income may not be evidence of stress," Mr. Greenspan said.

Separately, a sharp jump in lodging costs helped push up U.S. core inflation, which doesn't include the volatile food and energy sectors -- by 0.3%, the fastest pace in five months, the U.S. Labor Department said.

The department said a 2.9% jump in the cost of lodging -- such as hotels and college housing -- accounted for about three-quarters of the September acceleration in the core consumer-price index, which had risen only 0.1% in each of the prior three months.

A 0.4% drop in energy prices in September, the third straight monthly decline, helped temper the rise in the consumer-price index, the most widely used gauge of U.S. inflation.

In September, gasoline prices rose a mild 0.1%, the report said. Fuel-oil costs shot up 2.1%, but natural-gas prices slid 3.1% and electricity costs held steady.

"This confirms inflation remains steadfastly under control despite rising oil prices," said Peter Morici, a business professor at the University of Maryland.

But experts said the energy figures for last month don't reflect the recent sharp rise in oil prices, which have pushed the price of a barrel of crude to well above $50. Each $1 increase in the price of crude oil translates into 2 1/2 cents per gallon of gasoline. Crude-oil prices were at about their August levels in late September and have been rising since.

"Going forward, we can expect gasoline and home heating-oil prices to consistently follow crude-oil prices up," Mr. Morici said.

Meanwhile, the Social Security Administration reported that Americans receiving Social Security benefits will get a 2.7% increase -- an extra $25, on average -- in their monthly checks starting in January.

The drop in housing starts may not be sign of a weakening market. Building permits, an indicator of future building activity, increased by 1.8% last month to a two million annual rate from August's rate of 1.97 million, the government said.

The healthy housing market has benefited from relatively low mortgage rates. Last month, the average rate on 30-year loans held below 6% after drifting lower in midsummer, according to mortgage financer Freddie Mac.

Single-family housing starts took a dive, falling by 8.2% in September.

Leading the News
Economy Improves, But Oil Prices Carry Risk --- Construction, Auto Sales Signal Faster U.S. Growth; A Wary Eye on Consumers
By Greg Ip and Neil King Jr. in Washington and Lee Hawkins Jr. in Detroit
1097 words
4 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. economy appears to have put its summer soft spot behind it, but the threat of another soft spot looms.

A rebound in construction and auto sales and continued robust manufacturing activity suggest economic growth bounced back to an annual rate of 4% to 5% in the just-ended third quarter after slipping in the second quarter to a 3.3% rate.

"It does look as if the soft patch is over, but there are certainly risks, particularly with oil closing over $50," said Nancy Lazar, economist at ISI Group, a New York economic-research firm.

Indeed, although car manufacturers Friday reported a 5.5% boost in September sales from August, it was driven by steep discounting. Some manufacturers predicted sales would slip in the current quarter, and made no significant changes to already-trimmed fourth-quarter production schedules.

Consumer spending slowed sharply in May and June when oil topped $40 a barrel. Then, as gasoline prices dropped and a decline in long-term interest rates reignited home sales, the consumer revived from an early-summer slump that contributed to employers' reluctance to hire. Now with crude oil prices above $50 a barrel, that rebound is threatened.

Meeting Friday in Washington, finance ministers and central bankers from the world's seven major industrial economies said that "economic growth is strong and the outlook for 2005 remains favorable," but "this is not the time for complacency. . . . Oil prices remain high and are a risk." U.S. Treasury Secretary John Snow said the cost of oil has "created some headwinds for the economies of the world." Costlier oil can hurt the economy by acting as a "tax" on consumers, who must shell out more for products such as gasoline and heating oil, and by denting the profit margins of companies that consume fuel.

Crude oil for near-term delivery rose 48 cents to $50.12 a barrel Friday on the New York Mercantile Exchange on concerns about production losses in the Gulf of Mexico due to Hurricane Ivan. Adjusted for inflation, though, oil is cheaper than in 1981. Some economists said retail-gasoline prices, which have fallen from May's highs, could rise again.

"We could be cycling into another soft patch," warned Morgan Stanley economist Ted Wieseman.

The presidential campaign is shifting back to the economy, the theme of much of the Democrats' new ad blitz and of next week's third presidential debate. President Bush emphasizes the improvements in the economy; Sen. John Kerry the anxiety about jobs, health care and oil prices. The University of Michigan said its index of consumer sentiment eased slightly in September to 94.2 from 95.9 in August because of worries about energy prices and jobs.

Warnings about the remainder of the year came as economists raised estimates of growth for the just-ended third quarter. Ms. Lazar lifted hers to 4% from 3.5%; Mr. Wieseman raised his to 4.5% from 4.3%. Economists generally agree that it takes growth of at least 4% to create enough jobs to keep up with population growth and put some unemployed back to work.

The new estimates were driven primarily by signs of strong consumer and construction spending in early summer. The Commerce Department said Friday that construction spending rose 0.8% in August from July, unadjusted for price changes, to a record annual rate of $1.015 trillion, after July's upwardly revised increase of 1.1%. Still, much of the third quarter's strength was concentrated in July. Growth was spottier in August. Only a smattering of data on September is available, so it isn't clear how much momentum the economy has now.

Manufacturers, however, remain upbeat. The Institute for Supply Management's index of manufacturing activity, based on a survey of purchasing managers, eased slightly to 58.5 in September from 59 in August. A reading above 50 means the manufacturing sector is expanding. The survey's jobs measure jumped to 58.1 from 55.7. "Manufacturers are willing to hire as long as manufacturing production continues to grow," said Norbert Ore, who oversees the survey. One troubling sign was a continuing drop in the index of export orders.

U.S. sales of light vehicles rose to an estimated annual selling rate of 17.5 million from 16.6 million in August, according to reports from manufacturers. Driven by heavily promoted rebates and six-year, zero-percent loan offers from General Motors Corp. and Ford Motor Co., overall sales were up 10% in September from the same month a year earlier, paced by a 25% gain at GM. Ford's sales fell 4.2%.

GM and Ford, which trimmed fourth-quarter production plans in the face of bulging inventories last month, made no significant changes to those plans. Auto makers and dealers have mixed views of the economy, largely depending on whether they have hot new models. Toyota Motor Corp. saw September U.S. sales rise 10.3% to a record from the previous year.

Ford economist Jarlath Costello said the U.S. economy could be weaker in the fourth quarter if consumers remain concerned about weakness in the labor market and high oil prices. GM's executive director for market and industry analysis, Paul Ballew, said GM expects to "see some payback" -- or a decline in sales -- in October and November after September's spurt. He said GM will have to continue to offer aggressive deals to hold inventories in line. GM dealers are carrying inventories of 1.1 million unsold vehicles, down 30,000 from August, but up about 50,000 from this time last year.

Higher oil prices are likely to worry Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan, who has blamed the second-quarter weakness in the U.S. economy on energy costs. While the Fed looks certain to raise its key short-term interest rate to 2% from 1.75% at its Nov. 10 meeting, any sign that growth is being hurt by oil prices raises the odds the Fed will pause in its rate-raising campaign shortly thereafter.

U.S. Economy Appears To Get Past Soft Patch --- Rebound in Construction, Auto Sales Signal Growth But Oil Prices Augur Risks
By Greg Ip in Washington and Lee Hawkins Jr. in Detroit
1303 words
4 October 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A1
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

The U.S. economy appears to have put its summer soft spot behind it, but the threat of another looms.

A rebound in construction and auto sales and continued robust manufacturing activity suggest that U.S. economic growth, which slipped in the second quarter, rose to a robust 4% to 5%, at an annual rate, in the third quarter.

"It does look as if the soft patch is over, but there are certainly risks, particularly with oil closing over $50," said Nancy Lazar, economist at ISI Group, an economic-research firm in New York. Indeed, though U.S. car manufacturers reported a 5.5% boost in September sales from August, that was driven by steep discounting. Some manufacturers predicted sales would slip again in the current quarter.

U.S. consumer spending slowed sharply in May and June, as oil topped $40 a barrel and the stimulus faded from last year's U.S. tax cuts and from the mortgage-refinancing boom. That, in turn, prompted firms to cut back hiring. Then U.S. gasoline prices dropped, and with that as well as a decline in long-term interest rates which reignited home sales, the consumer bounced back. But with crude oil closing above $50 a barrel for the first time Friday, that rebound is threatened.

Moreover, the threat is global. Meeting in Washington Friday, finance ministers and central bankers from the world's seven major industrial economies said "economic growth is strong and the outlook for 2005 remains favorable," but "this is not the time for complacency. . . . Oil prices remain high and are a risk."

Crude oil for near-term delivery rose 48 cents to $50.12 a barrel Friday on the New York Mercantile Exchange on concerns about production losses in the Gulf of Mexico due to Hurricane Ivan. Adjusted for inflation, crude is still cheaper than in 1981.

Some economists said U.S. retail gasoline prices, which rose to a nationwide average of $2.06 a gallon in May and then fell to $1.85 in mid-September, could top those previous peaks. Gasoline averaged $1.92 last week, according to the U.S. Energy Department. "We could be cycling into another soft patch," warned Morgan Stanley economist Ted Wieseman.

Indeed, the University of Michigan said its index of U.S. consumer sentiment eased slightly in September to 94.2 from 95.9 level in August because of higher energy prices and job worries.

These warnings came even as economists boosted estimates of how fast the U.S. economy grew in the July-to-September quarter. Ms. Lazar has boosted her estimate of third-quarter growth to 4% from 3.5%, and Mr. Wieseman raised his to 4.5% from 4.3%. Economists generally agree that growth of 4% or more creates enough jobs both to keep up with population growth and to put some of the unemployed back to work. Growth slowed to 3.3% in the second quarter from 4.5% in the first.

The latest upward revisions were driven primarily by data showing strong consumer and construction spending in July. The U.S. Commerce Department said Friday that construction spending rose 0.8% in August from July, unadjusted for price changes, to a record annual rate of $1.015 trillion, after July's upwardly revised increase of 1.1%. The recent growth was centered in housing and commercial construction, reflecting continued low mortgage rates and a fading drag from still-high office vacancy rates.

Still, much of the third quarter's strength was concentrated in July, and growth was spottier in August. Only a smattering of U.S. data on September is available, so it's not clear how much momentum the economy has now. Ms. Lazar said her firm's weekly surveys of business contacts found a recent deterioration in business conditions, led by retailers.

Manufacturers, however, remain upbeat. The Institute for Supply Management said its index of manufacturing activity, based on a survey of U.S. purchasing managers at several hundred major industrial firms, eased only slightly to 58.5 from 59 in August. A reading above 50 means the manufacturing sector is expanding. Its subindex of employment jumped to 58.1 from 55.7. "Manufacturers are willing to hire as long as manufacturing production continues to grow," said Norbert Ore, who heads the survey for the institute. One troubling sign, however, was a continuing drop in the index of export orders.

U.S. sales of light vehicles rose to an estimated annual selling rate of 17.5 million from 16.6 million in August, according to reports from manufacturers. Driven by heavily promoted rebates and six-year, zero percent loan offers from General Motors Corp. and Ford Motor Co., sales were up 10% from the same month a year earlier, paced by a 25% gain at GM. Ford's sales rose 3%.

Still, GM and Ford, which trimmed fourth-quarter production plans in the face of bulging inventories last month, made no significant changes to those plans. U.S. auto makers and dealers have mixed views of the economy, largely depending on whether they have hot new models. Toyota Motor Corp. saw September U.S. sales rise 10.3% to a new record from the previous year. In a statement, Jim Press, executive vice president of Toyota's U.S. sales arm, said that "despite continuing fluctuations in oil prices and destructive weather in the Southeast, the industry held tight."

But Ford economist Jarlath Costello said there is a chance that the U.S. economy could be weaker in the fourth quarter if consumers remain concerned about softness in the labor market and high oil prices. GM's executive director for market and industry analysis, Paul Ballew, said that following the strong September showing, he expects GM to "see some payback" -- meaning a decline in sales -- in October and November. He said GM will have to continue to offer aggressive deals to hold inventories in line. GM dealers are carrying inventories of 1.1 million unsold vehicles, down 30,000 from August, but up about 50,000 from this time last year.

GM's U.S. dealers share the manufacturer's uncertainty. Many said the zero-interest promotion kept traffic bustling, but stopped short of characterizing the improved results as a broader indicator of an improved economy and consumer confidence.

Tom Shellworth, owner and president of Shellworth Chevrolet, a dealership in Vacaville, California, said sales are typically robust at his dealership during September and October, as consumers rush in to score deals on leftover 2004 models.

"The big question is whether what's happening now is a sign of things to come," Mr. Shellworth said. "And my answer is, `I don't know.' When I'm all out of what I'm selling from 2004, I'm going to have to sell 2005 models. Are the buyers going to be as eager to buy the 2005s? We'll have to wait and see."

Higher oil prices are likely to worry U.S. Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan, who first referred to June's weakness as a "soft patch" driven by energy costs. While the Fed looks likely to raise its short-term lending rate another notch at its November meeting, from 1.75% to 2%, any sign that growth is being hurt by the elevated oil prices will raise the odds it pauses in its rate increases shortly thereafter.

At present, world central bankers see little risk higher oil prices will fuel continuing inflation. "Inflation and inflation expectations remain low in our economies," G-7 officials said.

New-Home Sales In U.S. Rose 9.4% In August Data
292 words
28 September 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

New-home sales in the U.S. bounced back in August after declining in three of the previous four months, the Commerce Department said.

Behind the monthly volatility, sales show little sign of accelerating. But falling long-term interest rates are helping to stabilize the sector at a relatively brisk sales pace.

Sales of new-single-family homes rose 9.4% in August from the month before to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.184 million, the U.S. agency said Monday. The increase was the largest since sales shot up 11.7% in December 2000, but it came after large declines in the previous months. July home sales fell 7.3% to a rate of 1.082 million units and June sales were revised down to 1.167 million. The government's estimate of May sales was also lowered.

"The picture is still of a robust new-home-sales sector, only not nearly as hot as previously thought," economists at Maria Fiorini Ramirez Inc., a Wall Street research firm, said in a note to clients after the report.

A mixed housing picture shows up in other indicators. While new-home demand rose amid dropping mortgages rates, sales of existing homes declined last month. The National Association of Realtors Friday reported resales slipped 2.7% to a 6.54 million annual rate from July's 6.72 million. New-home-sales data are reported at contract signings and tend to show a more immediate response to interest-rate movements than existing-home-resale data.

New-home sales in the U.S. bounced back in August after declining in three of the previous four months, the Commerce Department said.

Behind the monthly volatility, sales show little sign of accelerating. But falling long-term interest rates are helping to stabilize the sector at a relatively brisk sales pace.

New-Home Sales Rose 9.4% in U.S. During August
By Jeff Bater and Jon E. Hilsenrath
356 words
28 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New-home sales in the U.S. bounced back in August after declining in three of the previous four months, the Commerce Department said.

Behind the monthly volatility, sales show little sign of accelerating. But falling long-term interest rates are helping to stabilize the sector at a relatively brisk pace.

Sales of new single-family homes rose 9.4% in August from the month before to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.184 million, the Commerce Department said. The increase was the largest since sales shot up 12% in December 2000, but it came after large declines in the previous months. July home sales fell 7.3% to a rate of 1.082 million units, and June sales were revised down to 1.167 million. The government's estimate of May sales also was lowered.

"The picture is still of a robust new-home-sales sector, only not nearly as hot as previously thought," economists at Maria Fiorini Ramirez Inc., a Wall Street research firm, said in a note to clients after the report.

While new-home demand rose amid dropping mortgage rates, sales of existing homes declined last month. The National Association of Realtors Friday reported resales slipped 2.7% to a 6.54 million annual rate from July's 6.72 million.

New-home-sales data are reported at contract signings and tend to show a more-immediate response to interest-rate movements than existing-home resale data.

The average 30-year mortgage rate has moved below the 6% mark, after rising earlier in the year on worries about inflation and Federal Reserve efforts to raise shorter-term rates.

Inventories of unsold, new single-family homes rose 2% to 404,000 in August, the largest amount since October 1979. But the supply of homes available at the current sales pace fell to 4.2 months, which is considered relatively tight supply.

The Economy
Durable-Goods Orders Fall 0.5% And Existing-Home Sales Soften
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
480 words
27 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Orders for long-lasting manufactured goods and sales of existing homes declined in August after posting large increases in earlier months, evidence that the economic recovery remained on track last month, but that the pace of activity wasn't especially vigorous.

Durable-goods orders for products such as cars, computers and aircraft fell 0.5% in August to a seasonally adjusted level of $195.4 billion after jumping by 1.8% in July and 1.3% in June, the Commerce Department said.

The key to the August decline was a drop in aircraft orders, which tend to move in large blocks from month to month. In July, Boeing Co.'s orders for commercial aircraft surged to 75 from 14 the month before, because of a large order for 7E7 aircraft from All Nippon Airways. But in August, Boeing's orders retreated to 20. Excluding the volatile transportation sector, new orders nationwide rose 2.3% in August from July, though this measure of industrial activity was below levels reached in March.

Economists keep close track of durable-goods orders because they are considered a leading indicator of economic activity. When a manufacturer receives a new order, it generally leads to additional production and employment can increase. Orders have rebounded sharply since early 2003 after tumbling during the economic downturn. But there are other signs that the pace of ordering activity has slowed in the past few months. Orders for capital equipment excluding defense products and aircraft, which are considered to be a good indicator of business-investment activity, fell 0.5% in August and were below levels reached in March after the decline.

"The economy is good but not great," said Michael Moran, an economist with Daiwa Securities America Inc. "It is not strong enough to put a meaningful dent in unemployment. It is not strong enough to lead to vigorous demand for labor and vigorous growth of new jobs. But at the same time, we're not deteriorating."

The National Association of Realtors said sales of existing homes declined 2.7% in August from the month before to an annual selling rate of 6.54 million units. This represented a cooling after the pace of home resales surged by more than 15% during the first half. With the August decline, the inventory of homes on the market amounted to about 4.6 months in August, compared with a backlog of 4.4 months in July and 4.2 months in June, the association said.

"We're at a more sustainable level now, but long term there should be some additional easing toward the end of the year," said David Lereah, the association's chief economist.

U.S. Home Construction Picks Up Pace
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
560 words
22 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A21
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The pace of U.S. home construction advanced slightly in August, despite hurricanes in the South and signs that home sales slowed in recent months.

The Commerce Department reported that the pace of new projects launched by home builders advanced 0.6% in August from July to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of two million units, after rising 9.4% in July to 1.988 million units. Activity was stronger than economists had expected.

Economists have worried that the housing market could falter as the Federal Reserve raises short-term interest rates and consumers react to ever-rising home prices. Yesterday the Fed raised the target for its key short-term rate, the federal-funds rate -- charged on overnight loans between banks -- a quarter of a percentage point to 1.75%.

Yesterday's Commerce Department report suggested that construction has instead leveled off at a relatively high pace. Construction has been helped in part by low long-term interest rates, which have declined in recent months even as the Fed has pushed short-term rates higher.

"We continue to see a healthy home-building market, particularly in our land-constrained markets, even though some of these markets are experiencing slightly slower sales as they absorb recent significant price appreciation," said Stuart Miller, chief executive of Lennar Corp., a Miami-based builder, in a profit announcement Monday. The company said the impact of hurricanes in Florida in August was small and it revised its profit guidance upward for 2004 and 2005.

Housing starts in the South rose by 1%, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 907,000 units. The pace of activity also rose in the Northeast and Midwest, but declined in the West.

There were some signs of a slowdown in the Commerce Department report. It showed that the pace of approvals for new-building activity slowed in August. Permits issued for new building declined by 5.5% to a 1.952-million-unit pace after jumping in July. Such permit activity is considered a leading indicator of future construction.

In a separate report, Financial Executives International, an association of corporate finance officers, said a September survey of 221 of its members showed executives plan to use high levels of cash to pay down debt and invest in new projects in the months ahead. Rising profits have left many companies flush with historically high levels of cash and other liquid assets. But many executives have been reluctant to put that cash to work aggressively.

The association said 40% of those surveyed from Sept. 8 to Sept. 13 said cash levels were above average and 19% described cash as below average. When asked what they planned to do with excess cash, 46% said they planned to invest in new equipment or expansion, 42% planned to pay down debt, and 20% had no immediate plans.

About a third of respondents were from public companies. Among public companies, 57% planned to invest or expand, 34% planned to pay down debt, 24% planned to buy back stock, 16% planned to start or increase dividends, and 17% had no immediate plans. More than one answer was allowed for the question.

House Play: After Big Run-Up in Real Estate, Some on Coasts Are Cashing Out --- Lower Prices Inland Create Opportunity for Arbitrage; A Slowdown or a Peak? --- Selling Loft for 9 Acres Upstate
By James R. Hagerty
2189 words
22 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Jo Anne Zliczewski cried when her husband proposed uprooting their family from Pompton Plains, N.J., about 30 miles from New York City. But she had to agree that the numbers looked good: By selling their suburban ranch house for a profit of nearly $300,000, they could buy a much larger home in Pennsylvania and live mortgage-free.

So began a debate that is rumbling across many kitchen tables on the East and West Coasts: Is it time to take profits on the real-estate boom? The huge rise in prices in thriving cities on or near the coasts has created an arbitrage opportunity for people who have the flexibility to move: Sell Manhattan, buy Montana.

Over the past five years, raging real-estate markets in some coastal areas have more than doubled housing prices, while farther inland prices have risen more moderately. That has stretched the price gap between the middle of the country and the coasts far beyond the norm. The typical home price for the 10 American metropolitan areas with the highest housing prices has jumped to 230% of the national median from 155% five years ago, according to an analysis by Economy.com for The Wall Street Journal.

Americans have been arguing for years about whether the surge in home prices on the coasts represents a bubble -- like Internet mania or the 1980s real-estate boom -- or merely an adjustment dictated by supply and demand. But anxiety over the question has been rising lately amid signs that some markets are finally cooling off. Inventories of unsold houses have begun growing in many parts of the nation, including Orange County, Calif., where the median home price has surged to $655,000, up 84% since 2001. Some owners are rushing to sell before it's too late.

Diane Saatchi, a regional vice president for the New York-based Corcoran Group, is advising customers to take their time looking. With war in Iraq, the economy looking wobbly and interest rates expected to rise, "who knows what could happen between now and November?" she says. For those inclined to sell, Ms. Saatchi says, now "seems like a great time."

One reason for caution about the market's long-term prospects: It's getting hard for ordinary people to afford housing near the coasts. The median house price in San Diego has climbed to 4.7 times average annual household income in that city from 2.8 times a decade ago, according to Economy.com. On a national basis, the ratio has risen only modestly, to two times income from 1.8.

For those approaching retirement, the price gap can be too tempting to resist. Jeanne Rowe, 67 years old, had expected to work for several more years as a technical writer for a mortgage company before selling her home in Costa Mesa, Calif., and retiring elsewhere with her husband, Fred, a former manager at a coffee-roasting company.

But Mrs. Rowe says her four decades of working in the mortgage business taught her that there is a cycle in housing: After seven to 10 boom years, she says, "the bottom can drop out." As for the latest boom, she figures, "there has to be an end because people can't afford to buy houses anymore."

So Mrs. Rowe accelerated her retirement plan. In March, the Rowes put a "for sale" sign in front of the four-bedroom house they bought in 1974 for $39,900. On the opening day of the sale, the first couple who viewed the home made an offer, matching the asking price: $672,000. The Rowes accepted rather than waiting for higher bids. "We didn't want to be greedy," Mrs. Rowe says. The profits will help fund their retirement in Tullahoma, Tenn., where they recently paid cash for a $145,000 home.

"They timed it perfectly," says Valerie Torelli, the real-estate broker who handled the Rowes' sale. About a dozen of her clients have made similar moves to cash out and move to cheaper areas inland, she says: "It changes their life. They never have to work again. Isn't that cool?"

The gap between the most expensive housing regions and the rest of the country has widened partly because there is a shortage of land available for home building in the high-cost areas. Those areas already are crowded, and local officials often block residential developments that would add to burdens on roads and schools.

So when the lowest mortgage interest rates in more than four decades spurred demand for housing, builders in many coastal areas couldn't quickly find space to put up more houses. Watching prices shoot up, buyers panicked. In the suburbs of Washington, many dispensed with the usual precaution of making their bids contingent on a professional inspection of the home's soundness.

By contrast, builders can respond much more quickly to increased demand in freely sprawling cities like Atlanta or Dallas, where land is plentiful. Median home prices have risen just 27% in Atlanta and 19% in Dallas over the past five years.

Economists hotly debate whether the housing boom is leading to another bust. "The boom continues," declares David Lereah, chief economist of the National Association of Realtors. While Mr. Lereah forecasts a slowing of price increases, he argues that strong demand from immigrants and the children of baby boomers will keep the market firm over the next decade.

But Robert J. Shiller, an economist at Yale University whose book "Irrational Exuberance" correctly predicted the bursting of the stock-market bubble in 2000, now is increasingly bearish on houses. Noting that prices in Los Angeles fell nearly 30% in the early 1990s, Prof. Shiller says such local or regional busts could happen again in the coastal areas where prices have risen most spectacularly. "These markets are riskier than people realize," he says.

After they had their first child last year, Diana and Bob Pailthorpe knew they would eventually outgrow their 1,250-square-foot home in San Jose, Calif., purchased in 2000 for $415,000. They started to look around in their area. "We realized we'd have to spend at least $800,000 to get a house that was even livable," Ms. Pailthorpe says.

Despite the high costs, they wanted to remain in California a few more years because they both had good jobs at technology companies. But they fretted: What if they bought an expensive new house in San Jose just before prices dipped? They could easily suffer a big loss when it came time to sell in a few years, especially after subtracting 5% or 6% in real-estate commissions. In the end, they unloaded their San Jose house for $593,000 in May and bought a new one, nearly three times as large, for $425,000 in Bozeman, Mont.

Mr. Pailthorpe found a job as a marketing manager for RightNow Technologies Inc., a software supplier based in Bozeman. His wife has found public-relations work there. Their household income has dropped by about a third, and Ms. Pailthorpe has been surprised to find that some things, such as medical and auto insurance, cost more in Montana than they did in California. Even so, the real-estate savings trumped all other financial considerations.

Ben T. Smith IV, chief executive of Spoke Software Inc., Palo Alto, Calif., says he has lost some promising young employees who decided to move to areas with lower housing costs. "When you begin to see young kids -- ones who are the future of the business -- opt out, that definitely has an effect on how you develop the business," Mr. Smith says. He has moved some work to India and allowed one of his software engineers to work from home in Vermont, visiting California just once a month. "For both sides, the economics worked out better," Mr. Smith says.

In Manhattan, Michael Quinn and his partner, John Soroka, decided to cash out on their two-bedroom condo in Manhattan's Hell's Kitchen neighborhood, agreeing to sell it for around $625,000 in mid-September. The proceeds will be enough to let them buy two homes -- one in Bronxville, a 30-minute commute from the gift and clothing shops they own in Manhattan, and another in Fort Lauderdale, Fla. The real-estate windfall makes it possible "to have two places and not be financially strapped," Mr. Quinn says.

Karen Davidson, a 50-year-old graphic designer, recently sold her loft in Manhattan's trendy Tribeca district for more than $1.5 million and bought a cabin and barn on nine acres in Lake Placid, N.Y., for $65,000. She found that she could do her freelance work there as easily as she could back in Manhattan. One of her main motivations was that she didn't want her life savings to be wrapped up in Manhattan real estate. By moving, she has been able to create a retirement fund for the first time.

Some owners of vacation homes also are deciding this is a good time to cash out. The inventory of unsold homes in Great Barrington, Mass., a popular spot for vacation homes in the Berkshires, is up about 40% from a year earlier. Richard Jackson, a real-estate agent at Wheeler &Taylor Realty in Great Barrington, says a local home-listings publication "is getting to be more and more like a telephone book." Dick Krzynowek, president of Isgood Realty in Great Barrington, says many sellers are seeking fancy prices for their homes but some buyers are "digging in their heels."

For the Zliczewskis (pronounced zluh-CHEF-skis) of Pompton Plains, N.J., real-estate profits were a lifeline. John Zliczewski, 51, who holds a bachelor's degree in business administration, last year was laid off from his job as a manager for a software company in New Jersey. It was his third layoff in four years. "Three strikes and you're out," he told his wife.

Jo Anne Zliczewski, 45, a mother of four children age 7 to 13, went to work folding clothes at a discount store so the family would still have medical insurance, but their financial situation looked shaky. Their four-bedroom house, purchased a decade earlier for $175,000, was their major asset. After his layoffs, Mr. Zliczewski wanted to ensure his family would have food and shelter no matter how he fared in the job market.

Friends suggested they consider Pennsylvania, where the typical house price is about half the New Jersey level. Mrs. Zliczewski hated the idea of moving away from family in New York City and doubted the schools there would be as good.

(MORE)

Then Mrs. Zliczewski began looking at Pennsylvania home prices on the Internet. "I was amazed at the size of house you could get for the price you'd pay for the handyman's special in a flood zone in New Jersey," she says.

In March, the Zliczewskis sold their house in New Jersey for $459,000. They used the proceeds to pay $309,000 in cash for a much bigger home overlooking a corn field on the fringes of Lancaster, Pa. Mrs. Zliczewski found the local schools better than she originally expected. Her husband got a job selling trailers for temporary office space. Though his new job pays only about two-thirds of what he made in New Jersey, the family no longer has a mortgage payment and can live comfortably. The neighbors include a doctor and a bank president. "We're like the Beverly Hillbillies here," Mr. Zliczewski says.

At their new church in Lancaster, the Zliczewskis met Jeff Geoghan, who recently moved with his wife and two children to Lancaster from Orange, Calif. The Geoghans sold a 1,200-square-foot bungalow in Orange for $400,000 and bought an 1,800-square-foot home in Lancaster for $175,000. Mr. Geoghan had to give up his job as an Internet sales manager in California, but he quickly found work in Lancaster as a real-estate agent. He expects to help people from the coasts find homes in Pennsylvania.

Home Construction in the U.S. Advanced Slightly in August
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
301 words
22 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A6
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The pace of U.S. home construction advanced slightly in August, despite hurricanes in the southeast and signs that new-home sales slowed in recent months.

The U.S. Commerce Department reported yesterday that the pace of new projects launched by home builders advanced 0.6% in August from the month before to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of two million units, after rising 9.4% in July to 1.988 million units. The activity was stronger than expected by economists.

Economists have worried for months that the housing market could falter as the Federal Reserve, the U.S. central bank, slowly raises short-term interest rates and as consumers react to rising home prices. Yesterday's report showed that construction has leveled off at a relatively high rate. One factor supporting this development: Long-term interest rates -- which are the benchmark for many home mortgages -- have moved lower in recent months, even as the Fed has pushed short-term rates higher. Long-term rates have been influenced by a benign inflation outlook and their low level has helped to keep homes affordable.

"We continue to see a healthy home-building market, particularly in our land-constrained markets, even though some of these markets are experiencing slightly slower sales as they absorb recent significant price appreciation," said Stuart Miller, chief executive of Lennar Corp., a Miami-based builder in a statement Monday. The company said the impact of hurricanes in Florida in August was small and it revised upward its profit guidance for 2004 and 2005. Housing starts in the South rose by 1%, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 907,000 units. Activity also rose in the northeast and midwest, but declined in the west.

U.S. Building Of New Homes Steps Up Pace --- August Had 0.6% Advance, Despite Fears Rate Rise Would Hurt Housing Starts
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
471 words
22 September 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A1
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

The pace of home construction advanced slightly in August, despite hurricanes in the U.S. South and despite signs that the pace of new-home sales has slowed in recent months.

The U.S. Commerce Department reported yesterday that the pace of new projects launched by home builders advanced 0.6% in August from the month before to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of two million units, after rising 9.4% in July to 1.988 million units. The pace of activity was stronger than expected by economists.

Economists have worried for months that the housing market could falter as the Federal Reserve slowly raises the level of short-term interest rates and as consumers react to ever-rising home prices. Yesterday's report showed that construction has instead leveled off at a relatively high pace. One factor supporting this development: Long-term interest rates -- which are the benchmark for many home mortgages -- have moved lower in recent months, even as the Fed has pushed short-term rates higher. Long-term rates have been influenced by a benign inflation outlook, and their low level has helped to keep homes affordable.

"We continue to see a healthy home-building market, particularly in our land-constrained markets, even though some of these markets are experiencing slightly slower sales as they absorb recent significant price appreciation," said Stuart Miller, chief executive of Lennar Corp., a Miami-based builder in a statement Monday. The company said the impact of hurricanes in Florida in August was small, and it revised up its profit guidance for 2004 and for 2005.

Housing starts in the South rose by 1%, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 907,000 units. The pace of activity also rose in the Northeast and Midwest, but declined in the West.

There were some signs of a slowdown in activity in the Commerce Department report. It showed that the pace of approvals for new-building activity slowed in August. Permits issued for new building declined by 5.5% to a 1.952-million-unit pace after jumping in July. Such permit activity is considered a leading indicator of future construction.

David Seiders, chief economist with the National Association of Home Builders, said he would nudge up his full-year forecast for housing starts to 1.945 million units after the latest report. Before the report, he was expecting starts to hit 1.92 million units for the year. He said he was sticking to his view that the sector is going through a "plateauing pattern."

Buyers Gain Power In Housing Market --- As Inventories Rise, Sellers Are Forced to Bend On Price; Free Vacation for Selling a Penthouse
By Ruth Simon
1786 words
8 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

THE RED-HOT housing market is showing its first signs of cooling, and the balance of power between buyers and sellers has begun to shift.

Sales of existing homes fell 2.9% in July from June's record pace, according to the National Association of Realtors, and brokers in many areas report that the number of houses on the market is beginning to rise. The result: Buyers are regaining some of their negotiating power and sellers are being forced to lower their sights after years of hefty price increases.

To be sure, home sales traditionally slow during the summer months as families turn their attention to vacations and the coming school year. But the heated spring selling season may have set the stage for a fall slowdown. "There's some indication we're in a transition period . . . from a seller's market to a buyer's market," says Tom Kunz, president of Century 21 Real Estate Corp., a unit of Cendant Corp.

And some real estate experts say the recent rise in inventories goes beyond the normal summer slowdown, with more sellers trying to cash out with big gains and more buyers nervous about overpaying at a market peak.

The apparent shift in the market's tenor follows a frenzied spring, when anxious buyers -- fearing they would get priced out by rising interest rates -- jumped into the market. The average price of a single-family home rose nearly 9.4% for the 12 months ended June 30, the largest 12-month increase since 1979, according to statistics released last week by the Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight. Now buyers are getting pickier, though many sellers still expect prices to keep rising at this spring's rapid clip, real-estate agents say.

In suburban Boston, inventories of high-end homes have risen roughly 15% during recent months, and many properties are selling for 15% to 20% below their listing price, says Alan Rice, a senior vice president with Carlson GMAC Real Estate. Buyers are increasingly asking sellers to include furniture or other personal property in the deal, says Mr. Rice, or to provide an allowance for new carpeting or a fresh coat of paint.

Across the country, in Orange County, Calif., which has been one of the nation's hottest markets, the change is even more dramatic: The supply of homes on the market rose to 7.5 months in July, based on the current rate of sales, up from 0.6 month in March, according to the California Association of Realtors.

Still, home sales are expected to reach record levels in 2004 for the fourth consecutive year. And with mortgage rates again hovering just below 6%, according to HSH Associates, purchases could spurt again this fall. Indeed, some markets haven't yet seen a shift. While July was slow, "August has been a whirlwind," says Jane Powers, a broker with Ewing &Clark Inc. in Seattle.

And Chicago, where the market never quite reached fever pitch, is continuing to see steady price gains, says Stephen Baird, president of Baird &Warner.

But elsewhere, there are signs the market is changing. One Orange County broker recently offered "a bonus of $2,500, champagne, roses, chocolates, mini vacation" plus the chance to win $10,000 to the agent who sells a penthouse condo in San Clemente priced at $1,225,000. The property has been listed for more than 60 days. "When something is on the market that long, you just take action to try and get it sold," says Marilyn Taylor, a broker-associate with Century 21 O.M.A., which is handling the property.

Linda Schermerhorn listed her two-bedroom townhouse in Irvine, Calif., in early June for $439,000, only to discover within days that two dozen similar properties appeared on the market. After consulting with her broker, Ms. Schermerhorn dropped her price to $419,000 -- slightly below recent comparable sales -- and spruced the home up with new curtains and towels. Within a week, she had two full-priced offers on the townhouse, which she had purchased as an investment.

Even Las Vegas, where prices increased by more than 52% over the past 12 months, is showing signs of cooling. The supply of homes on the market climbed to roughly four months in August, based on the current rate of sales, from a slim 1.7 months in January, says Lee Barrett, president of the Greater Las Vegas Association of Realtors. One reason for the increase in properties on the market: Owners who bought properties as an investment last year are cashing in. "Buyers now have an opportunity to look at a product without it selling in hours, and make an intelligent purchase," says Mr. Barrett.

The recent rise in inventories in some of the hottest markets could be a taste of things to come. Mortgage rates are likely to reach 6.25% by year end, says David Lereah, chief economist of the National Association of Realtors. He expects home sales to fall about 5% next year.

The rise in inventory is good news for home buyers, who face less competition for choice properties. "You still hear of multiple offers, but they are the exception rather than the rule," says Michael Turk, managing broker at Weichert Realtors in Alexandria, Va., where one house attracted a stunning 42 offers in May. Another sign of how things are changing: More buyers are opting for a home inspection. In the spring, many passed up an inspection because it could have reduced the chance of getting their offer accepted, Mr. Turk says.

Even in markets where bidding wars persist, the odds are improving for buyers. In June, the average buyer in Newport News, Va., had to bid on three or four properties before getting an offer accepted, says Liz Moore, president of Liz Moore &Associates. Now, the typical buyer has to make just two offers.

In other areas, inventories have risen to the point where buyers can drive harder bargains. In Rancho Santa Fe, Calif., many buyers are offering 4% to 7% below the asking price, says Charles Gifford, an associate broker with Coldwell Banker. Others are bidding 10% less on high-end homes "just to see what the seller's position is," he adds.

Russ and Terry Gieselman listed their four-bedroom, two-bath waterfront home in Massapequa, N.Y., for $785,000 in June. Just eight people showed up for the open house, including two neighbors, says their agent Gail Blumenstein, a broker-associate with Re/Max Shores in Massapequa. After several price reductions, the house now lists for $729,900. But so far there haven't been any offers. "I'm thinking . . . it has to do with the interest rate on mortgages," says Mr. Gieselman. "They've gone up a little and that's turned people off."

The shifting climate is a plus for buyers on a budget. When Lynn and Bob Merring bid $800,000 for a four-bedroom, three-bath home in Costa Mesa, Calif., priced at $849,000 in May, the seller offered to lower the price by $10,000. A few weeks later, the seller's agent suggested that the couple re-extend the offer, which was ultimately accepted.

Properties are also staying on the market longer. In Colorado Springs, Colo., listing times have climbed to 90 to 120 days "for the first time in a long time," says B.J. Burns, broker-owner of B.J. Burns Re/Max Real Estate Group there. Normally, she says, most properties sell within 60 days.

Judy Blea listed her three-bedroom, 2 1/2-bath home in Colorado Springs with Ms. Burns this spring, with an asking price of $179,000. With no sign of an offer, Ms. Blea debated about cutting the price, then pulled the house off the market last month. "There's a lot of houses for sale here," she says.

The new dynamic is also leading brokers and mortgage companies to alter their strategies. When homes were flying off the market, many sellers and listing agents balked at paying fees to brokers representing home buyers, says Maxine Golden of Re/Max Real Estate Services in Newport Beach, Calif. Now, they are offering higher commissions and even cash bonuses, she says.

Builders, meanwhile, are offering cheaper financing and training their staffs on how to best deal with pickier buyers. "We have to change from managing high demand to educating customers more," says Bill Probert, an executive vice president with builder John Laing Homes. "Buyers have more questions. They are more demanding. Every week it seems like there's more inventory."

Politics & Economy
Housing Supply Is Finally Rising In U.S., Particularly in California
By James R. Hagerty
796 words
1 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The number of houses on the market is finally rising in some parts of the U.S. where shortages have led to soaring prices.

The new supply should help soften price increases, and some economists say it may eventually bring prices down in some places where they have risen at double-digit rates during the past few years.

"There's going to be a day of reckoning," said Edward E. Leamer, an economist at the University of California, Los Angeles. "The question is how that reckoning is going to occur."

Any reckoning probably will be gradual. Unlike stocks, the general level of home prices couldn't tumble 20% or 30% in a few days. Many homeowners would refuse to sell if they couldn't get something near their target price, so it could take years for the market to adjust to a new supply-demand reality. Because local conditions vary, prices may continue to rise in some cities while falling in others.

Rising supplies are particularly notable in California, where the median home price has shot up 21% during the past year to $463,540 (384,740 euros). The California Association of Realtors says the inventory of previously occupied single-family homes in Orange County in July was enough to last 7.5 months at the current sales rate, up from 1.4 months in April. For the whole state, the supply stood at 3.3 months in July -- the first time since February 2003 that the inventory has exceeded three months.

Inventories also have risen in some other parts of the country. A survey by Jeffrey G. Otteau, who runs an appraisal firm in East Brunswick, New Jersey, showed that the inventory of homes in the 18 New Jersey counties he covers was up 14% in July from a year earlier. Inventories are up about 10% from a year earlier in Boston but have fallen more than 50% in the New York City borough of Manhattan. In Las Vegas, where the median home price has soared about 50% during the past year, developers are responding with more plans for high-rise housing.

In the U.S. as a whole, the inventory of newly built homes in July jumped 14% from a year earlier. The National Association of Realtors says the national supply of resale homes remains tight at 4.3 months, down from 4.7 months a year earlier. But Realtors report fewer bidding wars and more overpriced houses that linger for months rather than selling in days. Ben Coleman, owner of Century 21 Hartford Properties in San Francisco, said the market is still strong but no longer "on fire."

Many Realtors and housing-industry executives insist the boom will continue. David Lereah, chief economist for the National Association of Realtors, is so confident, he is finishing a book titled, "Are You Missing the Real Estate Boom?" due for publication early next year. Because interest rates are low, he said, many people can afford "exorbitant" prices. Mortgage payments account for about 18% of U.S. home buyers' income, down from more than 30% in the early 1980s when rates were high.

But economists expect interest rates to rise, and some worry home prices have raced too far ahead of income. Median U.S. home prices rose 33% in the four years ended June 30, while per-capita personal income climbed just 10.4%. "You are pricing a lot of people out of the market," said Sung Won Sohn, chief economist at Wells Fargo & Co.

Another danger signal is that home prices have risen far faster than rents, UCLA's Mr. Leamer said. In the long term, the price of a home shouldn't be too far out of line with the rent it could fetch, he said.

Realtors note that there hasn't been an annual decline in U.S. median home prices in the past five decades. That is true on a national basis, but prices have fallen brutally in some places, such as Los Angeles, where they sank nearly 30% in the early 1990s. On an inflation-adjusted basis, the national median home price has declined in eight of the past 33 years.

Frank Borges LLosa, a real-estate agent who works in the Virginia suburbs of Washington, D.C., said he warns his clients they can't bank on continued price gains. "Do I think it is a bubble? I don't know," he said, "but I'm sick of agents pretending like it is an impossibility." He is giving his customers T-shirts that read, "I bought a house during the '04 bubble and all I got was this lousy T-shirt!"

The Economy
Supply of Houses Is Finally Rising In Parts of U.S.
By James R. Hagerty
786 words
31 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The number of houses on the market is finally rising in some parts of the U.S. where shortages have led to soaring prices.

The new supply should help soften price increases, and some economists say it may eventually bring prices down in some places where they have risen at double-digit rates in the past few years.

"There's going to be a day of reckoning," said Edward E. Leamer, an economist at the University of California, Los Angeles. "The question is how that reckoning is going to occur."

Any reckoning probably will be gradual. Unlike stocks, the general level of home prices couldn't plunge 20% or 30% in a few days. Many homeowners would refuse to sell if they couldn't get something near their target price, so it could take years for the market to adjust to a new supply-demand reality. Because local conditions vary, prices may continue to rise in some cities while falling in others.

Rising supplies are particularly notable in California, where the median home price has shot up 21% in the past year to $463,540. The California Association of Realtors says the inventory of previously occupied single-family homes in Orange County was enough to last 7.5 months at the current sales rate in July, up from 1.4 months in April. For the whole state, the supply stood at 3.3 months in July -- the first time since February 2003 that the inventory has topped three months.

Inventories also have risen in some other parts of the country. A survey by Jeffrey G. Otteau, who runs an appraisal firm in East Brunswick, N.J., showed that the inventory of homes in the 18 New Jersey counties he covers was up 14% in July from a year earlier. Inventories are up about 10% from a year earlier in Boston but have fallen more than 50% in Manhattan. In Las Vegas, where the median home price has soared about 50% in the past year, developers are responding with more plans for high-rise housing.

Nationwide, the inventory of newly built homes in July jumped 14% from a year earlier. The National Association of Realtors says the national supply of resale homes remains tight at 4.3 months, down from 4.7 months a year earlier. But Realtors report fewer bidding wars and more overpriced houses that linger for months rather than selling in days. Ben Coleman, owner of Century 21 Hartford Properties in San Francisco, said the market is still strong but no longer "on fire."

Many Realtors and housing-industry executives insist the boom will continue. David Lereah, chief economist for the National Association of Realtors, is so confident that he is finishing a book titled, "Are You Missing the Real Estate Boom?" due for publication early next year. Because interest rates are low, he said, many people can afford "exorbitant" prices. Mortgage payments account for about 18% of home buyers' income, down from more than 30% in the early 1980s when rates were high.

But economists expect interest rates to rise, and some worry that home prices have raced too far ahead of income. Median U.S. home prices rose 33% in the four years ended June 30, while per-capita personal income climbed just 10.4%. "You are pricing a lot of people out of the market," said Sung Won Sohn, chief economist at Wells Fargo &Co.

Another danger signal is that home prices have risen far faster than rents, said UCLA's Mr. Leamer. In the long term, the price of a home shouldn't be too far out of line with the rent it could fetch, he argued.

Realtors note that there hasn't been an annual decline in U.S. median home prices in the past five decades. That is true on a national basis, but prices have fallen brutally in some places, such as Los Angeles, where they sank nearly 30% in the early 1990s. On an inflation-adjusted basis, the national median home price has declined in eight of the past 33 years.

Frank Borges LLosa, a real-estate agent who works in the Virginia suburbs of Washington, said he warns his clients they can't bank on continued price gains. "Do I think it is a bubble? I don't know," he said, "but I'm sick of agents pretending like it is an impossibility." He is giving his customers T-shirts that read, "I bought a house during the '04 bubble and all I got was this lousy T-shirt!"

Letters to the Editor
Homebuilders: Get Ready to Raise Roofbeams
483 words
31 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Karl Case and Robert Shiller may make homeowners anxious with their jeremiad on home prices ("Mi Casa Es Su Housing Bubble," editorial page, Aug. 24), but they are very unlikely to accurately predict the future price of housing in America. Basic economics tells us that price movement in free markets is a function of demand and supply. Consequently, for housing prices to drop significantly, there must be either a substantial increase in supply or a decrease in demand. The extensive analysis of homebuilding conducted by Prof. August Saibeni of Sacramento shows clearly that neither is on the horizon.

Demand for housing is determined by a combination of interest rate movements and the number of people turning 30 in a given year. Demand for housing almost doubled between the middle 1960s and the mid 1970s as the baby boomers began to turn 30. Demand then dropped off in the mid 1990s as the last of the baby boomers turned 30 and the baby bust generation started their families. In 1972-73, the nation averaged almost 6,000 housing starts per million people, whereas in 2000-2001, that number had dropped below 4,500 housing starts per million people. In 2003, the number of housing starts was 9% fewer than in 1977 and 22% fewer than in 1972. The number of Americans turning 30 each year is now beginning to climb again. Consequently, demand for housing is likely to grow relatively significantly over the next 10 to 15 years.

The homebuilding industry is not raising more capital to increase supply. In fact, since the end of 1998, there has been one homebuilder IPO, raising a mere $6 million; just enough to build 40 median-priced houses. California is now building about half as many houses as were built in the early 1980s despite the fact that the population has grown by almost 10 million people. Over the past decade, there have been only two years when the national supply of new houses matched or exceeded demand. In the coming years, not only will the demand for housing begin to pick up again, but the country has residual demand left over from the underbuilding that occurred in the 1990s.

Demand for housing is a function of demographics and cannot be captured via surveys of homeowners. When people turn 30 they tend to get married, have children, and then need a place to live. If a sufficient number of new houses and apartments are not being built to accommodate this demand, prices will go up. Rather than worry about falling prices for housing, public policy makers in the future are more likely to be concerned about how to encourage an increase in supply so housing prices don't continue to dramatically outpace inflation.

Gary C. Byrne

Santa Barbara, Calif.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Politics & Economy
U.S. Data Suggest a Soft Patch for the Economy
By Nicholas Zamiska
406 words
26 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- A sharp drop in new-home sales in July coupled with a modest gain in orders for durable goods suggest the U.S. economy remains stuck in a soft patch but isn't stalled.

While the two economic reports appeared mixed, analysts agreed that they pointed to slightly slower growth as the housing market continues to lose steam and the outlook for business investment remains uncertain. The U.S. Commerce Department reported that new-home sales fell 6.4% last month, and in a separate report said orders for durable goods rose 1.7% as orders for civilian aircraft doubled.

Higher mortgage rates, following the U.S. Federal Reserve's recent moves to raise interest rates, appear to be cooling the housing market. Sales of new single-family homes tumbled to 1,134,000 units last month from a downwardly revised 1,211,000 units in June. The average new-house price in the U.S. rose slightly in July to $274,200 (227,092 euros) from $262,500.

Analysts with Goldman Sachs Group Inc. pointed out that the July sales were nearly 15% below the initial June estimate of 1,326,000 units sold. "That's a very large drop and probably too large just to be noise in the data, even though the data are very noisy," said Andrew Tilton, an economist with Goldman Sachs.

Michael Moran, chief economist with Daiwa Securities America Inc., said that while the "new figures show a softer picture than previously believed," home sales remain strong by historical standards.

The rise in orders for durable goods, items expected to last at least three years, followed a 1.1% increase in June. Nondefense capital goods excluding aircraft -- widely viewed as a bellwether of business equipment investment -- rose 0.6% in July.

New orders for civilian aircraft and parts doubled to more than $12 billion in July, while demand for defense aircraft dropped by 38.4%. But without the volatile transportation sector, which had the largest increase of $3.1 billion, or 5.6%, new orders rose only 0.1%.

"Lack of momentum for nontransportation orders suggests that the soft patch for growth may be continuing," said Stephen Gallagher, chief U.S. economist for Societe Generale SA.

International News
U.S. Factory Orders Rose in July --- Demand for Durable Goods Surged as New-Home Sales Fell, Offering Mixed Picture
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
563 words
26 August 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A4
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- U.S. factories posted the biggest gain in four months in July orders for costly manufactured goods. Sales of new homes, meanwhile, declined, offering a mixed picture of economic activity.

The U.S. Commerce Department said Wednesday that orders for durable goods -- big-ticket items expected to last at least three years -- rose by 1.7% in July from the previous month. Orders were lifted by stronger demand for aircraft, machinery and communications equipment.

The increase -- the largest since March -- followed a revised 1.1% advance in June. The showing in July also was stronger than the 1% rise some economists were forecasting.

The data is good news for the U.S. manufacturing sector, which has regained steam in recent months after being hit hard by the 2001 recession. From January 2001 through July, about 2.7 million U.S. factory jobs have been lost.

Recently, however, the job picture has stabilized and output, driven by a boom in worker productivity, has increased. The U.S. Federal Reserve said factories ran at their fastest operating rate -- 76.3% of full capacity -- in more than three years in July.

The Federal Reserve, wanting to keep inflation under control, moved to boost interest rates on Aug. 10 by one-quarter percentage point to 1.5%. The Fed took the action even though Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan had acknowledged weeks before the Fed meeting that the economy had hit a "soft patch" in June.

In the manufacturing report Wednesday, orders for all transportation equipment rose by 5.6% in July, the biggest gain since February, mostly reflecting stronger demand for airplanes. In June, transportation orders rose by 4.7%.

Excluding orders for transportation equipment, bookings for all other goods nudged up by 0.1% in July, an improvement from the 0.3% decline in June. Orders for machinery in July increased by 2.1%, up from a 1.1% rise the month before.

For communications equipment, orders rose by 5.1% in July, compared with a 4.4% drop in June. Orders for electrical equipment and household appliances increased by 5% in July, a turnaround from the 5.5% decline recorded for June.

The Commerce Department also reported Wednesday that U.S. new-home sales fell more than expected in July to the their lowest pace since December, as higher mortgage rates cooled the housing market.

Sales of new homes tumbled 6.4% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.134 million units last month from a downwardly revised 1.211 million in June, the Commerce Department said. Inventories of homes available for sale at the current sales pace ballooned to a 4.2 months' supply, the highest level since February 2003.

Mortgage rates, which hovered slightly above record-low levels in early 2004, began to creep upward in early summer on expectations of a strengthening U.S. economy and rising short term-interest rates. While mortgage rates drifted lower from summer highs, they edged up last week and new applications for home loans fell, a mortgage-banking trade group reported early Wednesday.
