"Lack of momentum for nontransportation orders suggests that the soft patch for growth may be continuing," said Stephen Gallagher, chief U.S. economist for Societe Generale SA.

Politics & Economy
Home Resales Declined 2.9% In U.S. in July
By Dawn Kopecki Dow Jones Newswires
433 words
25 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Sales of existing homes in the U.S. fell a larger-than-expected 2.9% in July, according to a survey by the National Association of Realtors.

Home resales dropped to a 6.72 million annual rate last month from June's revised 6.92 million annual pace. June resales previously were reported at a 6.95 million annual rate, a 2.1% rise from May's record level.

The Realtors group now expects U.S. sales to top 6.4 million in 2004.

"There are those that talk about price bubbles and I continue to tell you there are no price bubbles," said the group's chief economist, David Lereah. "Prices are surging, particularly in the West, where they rose 17%," he said. "We have a supply problem."

In California, where the boom has been particularly strong, the median price of a single-family detached home in July was $463,540 (381,630 euros). That was down 1.1% from June, but up 21% from July 2003. The inventory of unsold homes was 3.3 months, up from two months a year earlier.

Sales declined in all regions except the South, where they rose 0.4%. Home resales fell 1.4% in the Northeast, 4.8% in the Midwest and 6.6% in the West.

The July report failed to meet Wall Street expectations, but Mr. Lereah said the numbers were the third-best annualized rate of home resales to date and the first drop since January, reflecting rising interest rates. Analysts expected existing-home sales to decline 2.2% to a 6.8 million rate.

Home mortgage rates settled below 6% in recent weeks to an average of 5.81% last week, according to a survey compiled by Freddie Mac, a government-chartered mortgage buyer. The average interest rate for a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage hovered around 6% for most of July, compared with an average of 6.29% in June and 6.27% in May.

In July, the inventory of existing homes on the market remained stable at 4.3 months, up slightly from a revised 4.2 months in June, the National Association of Realtors said. The median home price rose to $191,300 in July, compared with a revised $191,000 in June. The average home price edged down to $244,700 in July from a revised $245,500 in June.

The Economy
Hot Housing Market Simmers Down --- Existing-Home Sales Fell In July, Prices Were Stable; California's Supply Swelled
By James R. Hagerty and Dawn Kopecki
700 words
25 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The housing market is showing signs of cooling.

The number of previously occupied homes sold in July declined 2.9% from June's record pace, and the median price of those homes was essentially unchanged, the National Association of Realtors reported. In California, the median price of homes slipped 1.1% from a month earlier, as agents found themselves with an increasing number of houses on the market.

Realtors insist the boom that has enriched American homeowners during the past five years isn't over. "There are those who talk about price bubbles, and I continue to tell you there are no price bubbles," said David Lereah, chief economist for the association. He said the supply of new homes remains tight by historical standards in much of the country. Meanwhile, mortgage interest rates have fallen in the past few weeks, making house payments more affordable.

Even so, some home shoppers are becoming less aggressive in their bids amid fears that a slowing domestic economy and an eventual rise in interest rates could end, or at least interrupt, the boom. At the same time, the huge increase in home values in the past few years has emboldened some sellers to put unrealistic prices on their homes. A widening gap between bids and offering prices in some areas has slowed sales.

Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan, in a letter to the chairman of the Senate Banking Committee released yesterday,seemed to suggest he shares some of these concerns. Responding to questions about the U.S. housing market and other issues posed by Sen. Richard Shelby (R., Ala.) following Mr. Greenspan's appearance before the panel last month, the Fed chairman said he agreed with the assessment of Fed Governor Donald Kohn, who in a speech this year said "the odds have risen" that house prices in some parts of the country could be too high for current market conditions. He added that the Fed is monitoring real-estate prices closely in developing its economic projections.

The market "is not quite as wacky as it was, but it's still strong," said Pamela Liebman, chief executive of the Corcoran Group, a large real-estate brokerage firm in New York.

On a national basis, home resales slipped to an annual rate of 6.72 million units last month from June's revised 6.92 million annual pace. Despite the fall, July was the third-highest monthly level on record, Mr. Lereah said. Sales were down in all regions except the South, where sales rose 0.4%. Home resales fell 1.4% in the Northeast, 4.8% in the Midwest and 6.6% in the West.

The inventory of resale homes on the market was equivalent to a 4.3-month supply, slightly up from a revised 4.2 months in June, the association said. The median home price was $191,300, up 8.7% from a year earlier but barely changed from a revised $191,000 in June.

In California, where the boom has been particularly strong, the median price of a single-family detached home in July was $463,540. That was down 1.1% from June but up 21% from July 2003. The inventory of unsold homes was 3.3 months, up from two months a year earlier. It was the first time since February 2003 that the inventory has exceeded three months, according to the California branch of the Realtors.

The interest charged on 30-year fixed-rate mortgages increased in May and June but has fallen in recent weeks, hitting an average of 5.81% last week, according to a survey compiled by Freddie Mac.

The Commerce Department is due to report July sales of newly constructed homes today. Economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires and CNBC expect a decline of 2.3% to an annual rate of about 1.3 million.

Politics & Economy
U.S. Inflation Rate Fell in July
By Greg Ip
721 words
18 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- U.S. consumers got some relief on the rising cost of living in July as a drop in gasoline prices brought the inflation rate down for the first time since February. Although crude-oil prices have risen further in August, some analysts think gasoline prices may have peaked.

Separately, in two reports suggesting the economy regained its footing in July, industrial production rose 0.4% after falling 0.5% in June, and housing starts jumped 8.3% to an annual rate of 1.978 million. The evidence on the vigor of economic growth right now remains mixed, however.

The consumer price index fell 0.1% in July from June, the Labor Department said, mostly due to a drop in gasoline prices. As a result, the year-over-year increase in consumer prices fell to 3% in July from 3.3% in June.

In a related report, the Labor Department said that the drop in consumer prices helped inflation-adjusted weekly earnings jump 0.7% in July from June. But this recovered just half the 1.5% loss of purchasing power of the prior five months.

Excluding food and energy, "core" consumer prices rose just 0.1% in July from June, the second such tame monthly reading, as education, medical and housing inflation all moderated and car prices declined due to sales incentives. That pulled the year-over-year core inflation rate down to 1.8% from 1.9% in June.

That should comfort the Federal Reserve, which is counting on inflation remaining low so it may continue raising interest rates from ultralow levels at a slow, measured pace.

Buttressing the view that inflation is moderating, a survey by the National Federation of Independent Business found price increases at small firms have slowed. A net 20% of member firms raised prices in July, down from 29% in June. The net figure is the percentage raising prices minus the percentage cutting prices.

Analysts are divided on the course for gasoline prices. They were driven higher in the spring and early summer by higher crude costs and constrained refinery capacity, said Larry Goldstein, president of Petroleum Industry Research Associates, a New York energy-consulting firm. That drove refiners' profit margins sharply higher. They have since fallen back to normal levels.

So, despite crude oil hitting new highs, gasoline prices have dropped, according to the U.S. Energy Department. Mr. Goldstein said with summer demand ebbing and refineries soon switching to more easily produced winter grades, the peak in gasoline prices may have passed.

But Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High Frequency Economics, a research firm in Valhalla, New York, said a recent increase in wholesale gasoline prices suggests that retail costs could be headed back up.

Separately, the Fed said industrial production rose 0.4% last month. A decline in utilities output was offset by a 0.6% advance in manufacturing output. Excluding mining and utility plants, manufacturers operated at 76.3% of capacity, up from June's revised 75.9%.

High tech led the production increase. Semiconductor production rose 2.7% in July, computers and office equipment rose 1.9% and production of communications equipment rose 2.8%. Motor vehicles and parts production fell 0.7% in July after 2.1% declines in both June and May.

In another report, the Commerce Department said single-family home starts rose by 8.5% in July from June while multifamily-home starts -- apartment buildings -- dropped 6.5%. Starts rose in all regions of the country amid favorable weather conditions.

Building permits, an indicator of future building activity, rose 5.7% to a 2.055 million annual rate. Housing has remained stronger for longer than expected in part because mortgage rates have declined in the wake of recent weaker economic reports.

Mr. Shepherdson said reports for July so far are "quite encouraging," but surveys that hint at a decline in business confidence "would suggest the corporate sector is beginning to wobble a bit in the face of a continuing slowdown in consumption." He noted that chain-store sales declined last week, although that may have been aggravated by Hurricane Charley threatening the Southeast.

---

Rosy U.S. Data Give Outlook Slight Boost --- Drop in Gas Prices Helps Bring Down Inflation Rate; Housing Starts Jump 8.3%
By Greg Ip
704 words
18 August 2004
The Asian Wall Street Journal
AWSJ
A1
English
(c) 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- A couple of reports painted a slightly brighter picture of the U.S. economy, but neither suggested that it is roaring back.

Industrial production rose 0.4% last month after falling 0.5% in June, and total housing starts jumped 8.3% to an annual rate of 1.978 million. In addition, a drop in gasoline prices brought the U.S. inflation rate down for the first time since February.

Despite the upbeat July reports, they don't show the American economy rebounding to its strong winter pace, and the continued upward march in crude-oil prices and recent disappointing reports from major technology companies also are a cloud over the near-term business outlook.

The Federal Reserve said industrial production rose 0.4% last month. A decline in utilities output was offset by a 0.6% advance in manufacturing output. Industry operated at 77.1% of capacity, up from June's revised level of 76.9%. Excluding mining and utility plants, manufacturers operated at 76.3% of capacity, up from June's revised level of 75.9%.

High tech led the production increase. Semiconductor production rose 2.7% in July, computers and office-equipment production rose 1.9%, and production of communications equipment rose 2.8%. Motor vehicles and parts production fell 0.7% in July after a 2.1% decline in both June and May. Production of consumer automotive products edged down 0.1% last month after a 2.3% drop in June and a 2% fall in May.

Separately, the Commerce Department said single-family housing starts rose 8.5% in July from June, while multifamily starts, or starts on apartment buildings, dropped 6.5%. Starts rose in all regions of the U.S. amid favorable weather conditions. Building permits, an indicator of future building activity, rose 5.7% to a 2.055 million annual rate.

Mortgage rates have dropped in recent months as weaker economic reports have prompted markets to assume a slower increase in interest rates by the Fed. That has helped extend the housing boom, against expectations that it would be easing by now.

The consumer-price index fell 0.1% in July from June, the Labor Department said, mostly because of a drop in the price of gasoline. As a result, the year-over-year change in consumer prices fell to 3% in July from 3.3% in June.

In a related report, the Labor Department said the drop in consumer prices, combined with advances in hours and hourly earnings in July, meant inflation-adjusted weekly earnings jumped 0.7% in July from June. But this recovered just half of the 1.5% loss of purchasing power of the previous five months.

Excluding food and energy, "core" consumer prices rose just 0.1% in July from June, the second such tame monthly reading as education, medical and housing inflation moderated and car prices declined thanks to fresh sales incentives. That pulled the year-over-year core inflation rate down to 1.8% from 1.9% in June. The report could provide comfort to the Fed, which had predicated its plan to raise interest rates only gradually from ultralow levels on inflation remaining low.

Some analysts said gasoline prices may have peaked. They were driven higher in the spring and early summer not just by higher crude costs but by constrained refinery capacity, said Larry Goldstein, president of Petroleum Industry Research Associates, a New York energy-consulting firm. As a result, refiners' profit margins soared to $8 in May from $4 a barrel at Gulf Coast refineries in the third week of March.

Since then, margins have come back down to about $3.30, Mr. Goldstein said, which is in the normal range. That is why gasoline prices have edged down to $1.84 a gallon nationwide this week from a peak of $2.03 in late May, according to the Energy Department, even as crude prices hit highs.

---

The Economy
U.S. Inflation Rate Declines For First Time Since February --- Consumer-Price Index Slipped 0.1% Last Month, As Gasoline Prices Eased
By Greg Ip
761 words
18 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- U.S. consumers got some relief on the rising cost of living in July as a decline in gasoline prices brought the inflation rate down for the first time since February. Although crude-oil prices have risen further in August, analysts are divided on whether gasoline prices are headed much higher.

Separately, in two reports suggesting that the economy regained its footing in July, industrial production rose 0.4% after falling 0.5% in June, and housing starts jumped 8.3% to an annual rate of 1,978,000. The evidence on the vigor of economic growth right now remains mixed, however.

The consumer-price index fell 0.1% in July from June, the Labor Department said, mostly because of a decline in gasoline prices. As a result, the year-over-year increase in consumer prices fell to 3% in July from 3.3% in June.

In a related report, the Labor Department said the decline in consumer prices helped inflation-adjusted weekly earnings jump 0.7% in July from June. Still, this recovered just half the 1.5% loss of purchasing power of the prior five months.

Excluding food and energy, so-called core consumer prices rose just 0.1% in July from June, the second such tame monthly reading in a row, as education, medical and housing inflation all moderated and car prices declined as a result of sales incentives. That pulled the year-over-year core inflation rate down to 1.8% from 1.9% in June.

That should comfort the Federal Reserve, which is counting on inflation remaining low so it may continue raising interest rates from ultralow levels at a slow, measured pace.

Buttressing the view that inflation is moderating, a survey by the National Federation of Independent Business found price increases at small firms have slowed. A net 20% of member firms raised prices in July, down from 29% in June. The net figure is the percentage raising prices minus the percentage cutting prices.

Analysts are divided on the course for gasoline prices. Prices were driven higher in the spring and early summer by higher crude-oil costs and constrained refinery capacity, said Larry Goldstein, president of the Petroleum Industry Research Foundation, a New York energy-research group. That drove refiners' profit margins sharply higher, though margins have since fallen back to more-normal levels.

So, despite crude oil hitting new highs, gasoline prices have fallen, according to the U.S. Energy Department. Mr. Goldstein said with summer demand ebbing and refineries soon switching to more easily produced winter grades, barring significant increases in crude, the peak in gasoline prices may have passed.

Still, Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High Frequency Economics, a research firm in Valhalla, N.Y., said a recent increase in wholesale gasoline prices suggests that retail costs could be headed back up.

Separately, the Fed said industrial production rose 0.4% last month. A decline in utilities output was offset by a 0.6% advance in manufacturing output. Excluding mining and utility plants, manufacturers operated at 76.3% of capacity, up from June's revised 75.9%.

High technology led the production increase. Semiconductor production rose 2.7% in July, computers and office equipment rose 1.9% and production of communications equipment rose 2.8%. Motor vehicles and parts production fell 0.7% in July after 2.1% declines in both June and May.

In another report, the Commerce Department said single-family home starts rose 8.5% in July from June, while multifamily-home starts -- apartment buildings -- fell 6.5%. Starts rose in all regions of the country amid favorable weather conditions.

Building permits, an indicator of future building activity, rose 5.7% to a 2,055,000 annual rate. The housing sector has remained stronger for longer than expected in part because mortgage rates have declined in the wake of recent weaker economic reports.

Mr. Shepherdson said reports for July so far are "quite encouraging," but surveys that hint at a decline in business confidence "would suggest the corporate sector is beginning to wobble a bit in the face of a continuing slowdown in consumption." He noted that chain-store sales declined last week, although that may have been aggravated by Hurricane Charley threatening the Southeast.

The Home Front:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
The Home Front: Private Properties
By Danielle Reed
511 words
6 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
W10
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Anka's High Note

SINGER AND SONGWRITER Paul Anka recently paid $1 million for a condo at the Park Towers condominium in Las Vegas, public records show. The contemporary of Elvis Presley and composer of "Put Your Head on My Shoulder" and the theme from "The Tonight Show" in its Johnny Carson days bought a two-bedroom, 2 1/2-bath, 2,100-square-foot apartment in the 17-story luxury building. Earlier this summer, Mr. Anka and his ex-wife, Anne, sold their home in a gated community near Beverly Hills for $5.9 million. Mr. Anka declined to comment. Park Towers, located at Howard Hughes Center and started in the late 1990s, was one of the first two luxury residential high-rise condominiums to go up in Vegas (the other, also a late '90s development, is Turnberry Place). Casino mogul Steve Wynn and real-estate developer Irving Molasky developed Park Towers, a two-tower building with concierge service and its own spa. Other residents of Park Towers include Tim Poster, the Internet mogul turned Golden Nugget casino owner, and the co-owner of the Golden Nugget, Tom Breitling (the two star in Fox's new reality show "The Casino"). Lately Las Vegas has seen a boom in high-rise construction. Eleven new condos are preselling units, says agent Cindy DellaValle of Vertical Properties Group and, based on her own estimates, she expects new projects to nearly double in the next two years.

Whoopi in Vermont

ACTRESS Whoopi Goldberg paid $950,000 for a 745-acre retreat near Brattleboro, Vt., a few months ago, public records show. The property, which had been listed at $985,000, includes a Colonial-style clapboard house. The sellers were Jennifer Ramstetter, a college biology professor, and her husband, Brian McNeice, a farmer. The partially fenced land comes with a barn, pond, stream, river, a shed, mountain views and wooded trails. Berkley &Veller Greenwood Country Realtors listed and sold the property.

Palm Beach's Hot Summer

THIS IS SUPPOSED to be the slow season for Palm Beach real estate, but the home of Lawrence Ladove -- former president of LaDove, a Miami Lakes, Fla., personal-care products company now run by his daughter, Sheree -- was listed at $15 million in mid-July, and went into contract a week later.

Home View / Refinancing

THE UPTICK IN interest rates, as many predicted, has drastically slowed consumer interest in refinancing. In its latest long-range forecast, the Mortgage Bankers Association predicts that refinancings will drop 54%, to $191 billion, in the third quarter of 2004 from the second quarter. The trade association also expects that refinancings will only reach $127 billion in the fourth quarter of this year, a 34% decline.

Meanwhile, a recent survey of 881 respondents around the country by Domania.com, found that while nearly 30% have refinanced multiple times since they've been homeowners, only 13% say they're still planning on refinancing.

The Economy
Factory Activity Rose in July On Higher Orders and Output --- Manufacturing Data Beat Forecasts of Economists; Inflation Pressure Eased
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
476 words
3 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2004, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Factory activity in the U.S. increased in July amid a jump in new orders and production, a private survey found. Inflation pressures moderated though remained high, while hiring slowed.

The Institute for Supply Management's July manufacturing index rose to 62 points from 61.1 in June. Economists had expected a reading of 61.6, according to a survey by and CNBC. A reading above 50 indicates expansion. Of 20 industries surveyed, 18 reported growth.

"The manufacturing sector continues to grow at a rapid rate," said Norbert J. Ore, chairman of the institute's survey committee. "The growth of new orders and production accelerated during the month, adding strength to the index."

The institute noted that with July's report, overall readings in the survey have been above 60 for nine months, the longest stretch since the period between July 1972 and June 1973. If the July reading of 62 were to be maintained for an extended period, it would be consistent with a 7% annualized increase in the U.S.'s gross domestic product, or the total value of goods and services produced there, the institute said.

Within the survey, the new-orders index rose to 64.7 from 60 in June, while the production index climbed to 66.1 from 63.2. Meanwhile, the employment reading fell to 57.3 from 59.7. Inventories contracted to 49.9 from 51.1, and order backlogs fell to 58 from 58.5.

David Greenlaw, an economist at Morgan Stanley, wrote to clients that the downturn in the employment index "was not nearly large enough" to alter forecasts for Friday's report on July nonfarm payrolls. On average, economists predict the U.S. economy added 215,000 new jobs in July, according to Dow Jones Newswires.

For the 29th consecutive month, manufacturers faced higher prices, though price growth was slower in July. The institute noted that "energy prices remain a major concern for purchasers, as prices are at or near record highs."

Crude-oil futures have marched higher in recent days -- ending up two cents at $43.82 cents a barrel in New York trading -- amid supply jitters and new terrorism warnings in the U.S.

Separately, the Commerce Department said total construction spending in June fell 0.3%, the first decline since January, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $985.16 billion. That followed a revised 0.1% gain in construction spending in May, which had been estimated as a 0.3% rise. On an annual basis, total construction spending was 8.6% higher.
